  • TUESDAY, SEPTEMBER 10, 2013

EPIGRAPH

• • •

She's buried under a silver birch tree, near the old train tracks. Her grave is marked by a small pile of stones. It's not much, just a little stack. I didn't want anyone to notice her grave, but I had to leave something to remember her by. She'll sleep there quietly, with only birds singing and trains passing by.

• • •

One for sadness, two for happiness, three for a girl... Three for a girl. I can't get past three. My head is full of noises, and my mouth is full of blood. Three for a girl. I hear the magpies laughing at me. It's a loud, harsh sound. A warning. A bad warning. I see them now, dark against the sun. Not the birds, something else. Someone is coming. Someone is talking to me. Now look. Now look what you made me do.

RACHEL

• • •

FRIDAY, JULY 5, 2013

MORNING

There's a pile of clothes next to the train tracks. Light blue fabric—maybe a shirt—mixed with something dirty white. It's probably trash, part of a load dumped in the small, overgrown woods by the bank. It could be from the workers who fix this part of the track; they're here often. Or it could be something else. My mom used to say I had a wild imagination; Tom said that too. I can't help it. I see these thrown-away things, a dirty shirt or a single shoe, and I just think about the other shoe and the feet that wore them.

The train bumps and scrapes and starts moving again. The little pile of clothes disappears, and we slowly head towards London. Someone behind me sighs, annoyed. The 8:04 slow train from Ashbury to Euston can make anyone lose patience. The trip should take 54 minutes, but it rarely does. This part of the track is old, broken down, with signal problems and constant repairs.

The train moves slowly, passing warehouses, water towers, bridges, sheds, and simple old houses that face away from the track.

My head is against the window, and I watch these houses go by like a movie scene. I see them in a way others don't; even the people who live there probably don't see them like this. Twice a day, I get a quick look into other people's lives. It's comforting to see strangers safe at home.

Someone's phone is ringing, playing a happy, upbeat song. They're slow to answer, and it keeps ringing. I can feel the other people on the train shift in their seats, rustle their papers, and type on their computers. The train rocks and sways around a curve, slowing down at a red light. I try not to look up. I try to read the free newspaper I got at the station, but the words are blurry, and I can't focus. I still see that little pile of clothes by the track, left behind.

EVENING

The fizzy gin and tonic spills over the top of the can as I drink. It's tangy and cold, like the taste of my first trip with Tom. We went to a fishing village on the Basque coast in 2005. In the mornings, we'd swim to a small island in the bay and make love on hidden beaches. In the afternoons, we'd sit at a bar, drinking strong gin and tonics, watching lots of people play chaotic soccer games on the beach.

I take another sip, and another. The can is already half empty, but I have three more in the bag at my feet. It's Friday, so I don't feel bad about drinking on the train. Thank God it's Friday. The fun starts now.

The weather forecast says it will be a lovely weekend with sunshine and clear skies. In the past, we might have driven to Corly Wood with a picnic and the newspaper. We would have spent the afternoon lying on a blanket in the sun, drinking wine. We might have had a barbecue with friends or gone to the Rose pub and sat in the beer garden. Our faces would have gotten red from the sun and alcohol as the afternoon went on. We would have walked home, arm in arm, and fallen asleep on the sofa.

Beautiful sunshine, clear skies, but no one to spend time with and nothing to do. Living like this is harder in the summer. There's so much daylight and not much darkness. Everyone is out, being obviously happy. It's tiring, and it makes you feel bad if you're not joining in.

The weekend is ahead of me, forty-eight empty hours to fill. I lift the can to my mouth again, but it's empty.

MONDAY, JULY 8, 2013

MORNING

It's nice to be back on the 8:04 train. It's not that I'm excited to go to London to start my week. I don't really want to be in London at all. I just want to relax in the soft seat, feel the sun coming through the window, and feel the train moving back and forth. The sound of the wheels on the tracks is comforting. I'd rather be here, looking at the houses by the track, than almost anywhere else.

There's a problem with a signal on this line, about halfway through my trip. I think it must be broken because it's almost always red. We stop there most days, sometimes for a few seconds, sometimes for many minutes. If I sit in carriage D, which I usually do, and the train stops at this signal, which it almost always does, I have a perfect view of my favorite house by the track: number fifteen.

Number fifteen is like the other houses along this part of the track. It's a two-story Victorian house with a narrow, well-kept garden. The garden goes about twenty feet down to a fence. Beyond the fence is a small area before the railway track. I know this house very well. I know every brick, the color of the curtains in the upstairs bedroom (beige with a dark blue pattern), that the paint is peeling off the bathroom window, and that there are four tiles missing from the roof on the right side.

I know that on warm summer nights, the people who live in this house, Jason and Jess, sometimes climb out of the big window to sit on the flat roof of their kitchen. They are a perfect couple. He has dark hair and is strong and kind. He laughs a lot. She is small and pretty, with pale skin and short blonde hair. She has sharp cheekbones with freckles and a nice jaw.

When we stop at the red light, I look for them. Jess is often out there in the mornings, especially in the summer, drinking coffee. Sometimes, when I see her, I feel like she sees me too, and I want to wave. But I'm too shy. I don't see Jason as much because he's often away for work. But even if they're not there, I think about what they might be doing. Maybe they both have the day off and she's in bed while he makes breakfast, or maybe they went for a run together. (Tom and I used to run together on Sundays, me going a little faster than usual, and him going slower, so we could run together.) Maybe Jess is upstairs painting, or maybe they're in the shower together.

EVENING

I turn slightly towards the window, with my back to the other people on the train. I open a small bottle of white wine I bought at the train station. It's not cold, but it's okay. I pour some into a plastic cup, put the top back on, and put the bottle in my bag. It's not really okay to drink on the train on a Monday, unless you're with someone, which I'm not.

I see the same people on these trains every week. I know them, and they probably know me. But I don't know if they see the real me.

It's a nice evening, warm but not too hot. The sun is going down, making the shadows longer and the trees look gold. The train is moving fast, and we pass Jason and Jess's house quickly. Sometimes, I can see them from this side of the train tracks. If there's no train going the other way, and if we're going slow enough, I can sometimes see them on their roof. If not, like today, I imagine them. Jess will be sitting with her feet up, with a glass of wine, and Jason will be standing behind her with his hands on her shoulders. I can imagine how his hands feel, strong and safe. Sometimes I try to remember the last time someone touched me in a nice way, like a hug, and it makes me sad.

TUESDAY, JULY 9, 2013

MORNING

The pile of clothes from last week is still there, and it looks dusty and sad. I read that a train can rip your clothes off when it hits you. It's not unusual to die by train. They say it happens a few hundred times a year. I don't know how many of those are accidents. I look carefully for blood on the clothes as the train goes by, but I don't see any.

The train stops at the light as usual. I can see Jess standing on the patio in front of the glass doors. She's wearing a colorful dress, and her feet are bare. She's looking back into the house, probably talking to Jason, who's making breakfast. I keep looking at Jess and her house as the train starts to move. I don't want to see the other houses, especially the one four doors down, the one that used to be mine.

I lived at number twenty-three Blenheim Road for five years. I was very happy and very sad there. I can't look at it now. It was my first home. Not my parents' house, not a shared place with other students, but my first home. I hate to look at it. Well, I can, I do, I want to, I don't want to, I try not to. Every day I tell myself not to look, and every day I look. I can't stop myself, even though I don't want to see anything there, even though anything I do see will hurt me. I still remember clearly how it felt when I looked up and saw that the cream blind in the upstairs bedroom was gone. It was replaced by a soft pink one. I still remember the pain I felt when I saw Anna watering the roses by the fence. Her T-shirt was tight over her big belly, and I bit my lip so hard it bled.

I close my eyes tightly and count to ten, fifteen, twenty. It's gone now, nothing to see. We go into Witney station and out again. The train starts to speed up as the suburbs turn into dirty North London. Houses are replaced by bridges with graffiti and empty buildings with broken windows. The closer we get to Euston, the more worried I feel. I feel pressure building. How will today be? There's a dirty, low concrete building on the right side of the track, about five hundred meters before Euston. Someone has painted on its side: LIFE IS NOT A PARAGRAPH. I think about the pile of clothes by the track, and I feel like my throat is closing up. Life is not a paragraph, and death is not something you can ignore.

EVENING

The train I take in the evening, the 5:56, is a little slower than the morning one. It takes one hour and one minute, seven minutes longer than the morning train, even though it doesn't stop at any extra stations. I don't mind. I'm not in a hurry to get to London in the morning, and I'm not in a hurry to get back to Ashbury in the evening either. It's not just because it's Ashbury, although the place is bad enough. It's a new town from the 1960s, spreading like a disease over the middle of Buckinghamshire. It's no better or worse than many other towns like it. The center is full of cafes, phone shops, and JD Sports stores. Around that is a ring of suburbs, and beyond that are the movie theaters and big Tesco stores. I live in a fairly nice, new building where the shops start to turn into houses. But it's not my home. My home is the old house by the tracks, the one I used to own part of. In Ashbury, I'm not a homeowner, not even a renter. I'm a lodger, staying in the small second bedroom in Cathy's plain apartment. I depend on her kindness.

Cathy and I were friends at university. Not really close friends. We were never that close. She lived across the hall from me in my first year, and we were in the same class. So we were friends in those first few scary weeks, before we met people we had more in common with. We didn't see each other much after the first year, and hardly at all after college, except for the occasional wedding. But when I needed help, she had a spare room. It made sense. I was sure it would only be for a couple of months, six at the most. I didn't know what else to do. I'd never lived alone. I'd gone from my parents to roommates to Tom. The idea of living alone was too much, so I said yes. That was almost two years ago.

It's not terrible. Cathy is a nice person, in a strong way. She makes you notice how nice she is. Her niceness is very obvious. It's her main quality, and she needs people to notice it, often, almost every day. It can be tiring. But it's not so bad. I can think of worse things in a roommate. No, it's not Cathy, and it's not even Ashbury that bothers me most about my new situation (I still think of it as new, even though it's been two years). It's the loss of control. In Cathy's apartment, I always feel like a guest who has stayed too long. I feel it in the kitchen, where we fight for space when we cook dinner. I feel it when I sit next to her on the sofa, with the remote control in her hand. The only space that feels like mine is my tiny bedroom. A double bed and a desk are crammed in, with barely enough space to walk between them. It's comfortable enough, but it's not a place you want to be. So instead, I stay in the living room or at the kitchen table, feeling uncomfortable and powerless. I have lost control over everything, even the places in my mind.

WEDNESDAY, JULY 10, 2013

MORNING

The heat is building. It's barely past eight thirty, and the day is already hot. The air is heavy with moisture. I wish there was a storm, but the sky is just a blank, pale blue. I wipe the sweat off my lip. I wish I had remembered to buy a bottle of water.

I can't see Jason and Jess this morning, and I feel very disappointed. It's silly, I know. I look closely at the house, but there's nothing to see. The curtains are open downstairs, but the glass doors are closed. The sun is reflecting off the glass. The window upstairs is closed, too. Jason might be away working. I think he's a doctor, maybe for one of those groups that works overseas. He's always on call, with a bag packed on top of the closet. If there's an earthquake in Iran or a tsunami in Asia, he drops everything. He grabs his bag and is at the airport within hours, ready to fly out and save lives.

Jess, with her bright clothes, her sneakers, and her beauty, her confidence, works in fashion. Or maybe in music, or advertising—she could be a stylist or a photographer. She's also a good painter, very artistic. I can picture her now, in the extra room upstairs, music loud, window open, a brush in her hand, a huge canvas against the wall. She'll be there until midnight; Jason knows not to bother her when she's working.

I can't really see her, of course. I don't know if she paints, or if Jason laughs a lot, or if Jess has great cheekbones. I can't see her face shape from here, and I've never heard Jason's voice. I've never seen them up close; they didn't live there when I lived down the road. They moved in after I left two years ago, I'm not sure exactly when. I guess I started noticing them about a year ago, and slowly, as time went on, they became important to me.

I don't know their names, so I had to name them myself. Jason, because he looks like a handsome British movie star, not like Depp or Pitt, but like Firth, or Jason Isaacs. And Jess just fits with Jason, and it fits her. It suits her, pretty and carefree as she is. They belong together, they're a pair. They're happy, I can tell. They're what I used to be, they're Tom and me five years ago. They're what I lost, they're everything I want to be.

EVENING

My shirt, too tight, buttons pulling across my chest, is stained with sweat, damp under my arms. My eyes and throat are itchy. Tonight, I don't want the trip to take long; I want to get home, take off my clothes, and get in the shower, to be where no one can see me.

I look at the man across from me. He's about my age, early to mid-thirties, with dark hair that's turning gray. Pale skin. He's wearing a suit, but he's taken off the jacket and put it on the seat next to him. He has a very thin laptop open in front of him. He types slowly. He's wearing a silver watch with a big face on his right wrist—it looks expensive, maybe a Breitling. He's biting the inside of his cheek. Maybe he's nervous. Or just thinking hard. Writing an important email to someone at work in New York, or a careful break-up message to his girlfriend. He looks up suddenly and sees me; his eyes look me over, then at the small bottle of wine on the table in front of me. He looks away. The way his mouth is set makes me think he's disgusted. He finds me disgusting.

I'm not the girl I used to be. I'm not attractive anymore, I'm unpleasant in some way. It's not just that I've gained weight, or that my face is swollen from drinking and not sleeping; it's like people can see the damage all over me, can see it in my face, the way I stand, the way I move.

One night last week, when I left my room to get a glass of water, I heard Cathy talking to Damien, her boyfriend, in the living room. I stood in the hallway and listened. "She's lonely," Cathy was saying. "I'm really worried about her. It doesn't help that she's alone all the time." Then she said, "Is there someone from work, maybe, or the rugby club?" and Damien said, "For Rachel? No offense, Cath, but I don't think I know anyone that desperate."

THURSDAY, JULY 11, 2013

MORNING

I'm picking at the plaster on my finger. It's wet because I washed my coffee mug this morning. It feels damp and dirty, even though it was clean earlier. I don't want to pull it off because the cut is deep. Cathy was out when I got home, so I went to the store and bought two bottles of wine. I drank one, and then I decided to cook myself a steak since she was out. I made a red onion relish and had it with a green salad. A good, healthy meal. I cut my finger while chopping the onions. I must have gone to the bathroom to clean it and then laid down for a while and forgot about it. I woke up around ten and heard Cathy and Damien talking. He was saying it was disgusting that I left the kitchen like that. Cathy came to see me. She knocked softly on my door and opened it a little. She tilted her head and asked if I was okay. I apologized, even though I wasn't sure why. She said it was fine, but asked if I could clean up. There was blood on the cutting board, the room smelled like raw meat, and the steak was still on the counter, turning grey. Damien didn't even say hello. He just shook his head when he saw me and went to Cathy's room.

After they went to bed, I remembered I hadn't drunk the second bottle, so I opened it. I sat on the couch and watched TV with the sound low so they wouldn't hear. I don't remember what I watched, but I must have felt lonely or happy or something, because I wanted to talk to someone. I really needed to talk to someone, and the only person I could call was Tom.

There's no one I want to talk to except Tom. My phone shows I called four times: at 11:02, 11:12, 11:54, and 12:09. Based on the call lengths, I left two messages. He might have even answered, but I don't remember talking to him. I remember leaving the first message. I think I just asked him to call me. That might be what I said in both messages, which isn't too bad.

The train stops at a red light, and I look up. Jess is on her patio, drinking coffee. Her feet are on the table, and her head is back, enjoying the sun. Behind her, I think I see a shadow, someone moving: Jason. I want to see him, to see his handsome face. I want him to come outside, to stand behind her like he does, and kiss her head.

He doesn't come out, and her head falls forward. Something about how she's moving today seems different. She seems heavier, weighed down. I want him to come out to her, but the train moves forward, and he's still not there. She's alone. Now, without thinking, I look at my house, and I can't look away. The doors are open, and light is shining into the kitchen. I can't tell if I'm seeing this or imagining it. Is she there, at the sink, washing dishes? Is there a little girl in a baby chair on the kitchen table?

I close my eyes and let the darkness grow until it changes from sadness into something worse: a memory, a flashback. I didn't just ask him to call me back. I remember now, I was crying. I told him I still loved him, that I always would. Please, Tom, please, I need to talk to you. I miss you. No no no no no no no.

I have to accept it. There's no point in trying to ignore it. I'm going to feel awful all day. It's going to come in waves, strong then weak then strong again. It's a twisting feeling in my stomach, the shame, the heat in my face, my eyes squeezed shut like I could make it all disappear. And I'll be telling myself all day, it's not the worst thing, is it? It's not the worst thing I've ever done. It's not like I fell in public or yelled at a stranger. It's not like I embarrassed my husband at a party by yelling at his friend's wife. It's not like we fought one night and I went after him with a golf club, hitting the wall in the hallway. It's not like going back to work after a long lunch and stumbling through the office, everyone staring, and Martin Miles telling me, I think you should go home, Rachel. I once read a book by a former alcoholic who talked about giving oral sex to two men she just met in a restaurant. I read it and thought, I'm not that bad. That's how low the bar is.

EVENING

I've been thinking about Jess all day, unable to focus on anything but what I saw this morning. What made me think something was wrong? I couldn't see her face from that far away, but I felt like she was alone. More than alone, lonely. Maybe she was. Maybe he's away, gone to one of those hot countries to help people. And she misses him and worries, even though she knows he has to go.

Of course she misses him, just like I do. He is kind and strong, everything a husband should be. And they are a team. I can see it, I know how they are. His strength and protectiveness don't mean she's weak. She's strong in other ways. She understands things quickly, which amazes him. She can get to the heart of a problem faster than anyone else. At parties, he often holds her hand, even though they've been together for years. They respect each other and don't put each other down.

I feel very tired tonight. I haven't had any alcohol. Some days I feel so bad that I need to drink; other days I feel so bad that I can't. Today, the idea of alcohol makes me sick. But staying sober on the train home is hard, especially now, in this heat. I'm covered in sweat, my mouth feels dry, my eyes itch, and my mascara is smudged.

My phone vibrates in my bag, making me jump. Two girls across from me look at me and then at each other, smiling secretly. I don't know what they think of me, but I know it's not good. My heart is beating fast as I reach for my phone. I know this won't be good either: it will be Cathy, maybe asking me nicely to stop drinking tonight? Or my mother, telling me she's coming to London next week and wants to have lunch. I look at the screen. It's Tom. I wait a second and then answer.

"Rachel?"

For the first five years I knew him, I was never Rachel, always Rach. Sometimes Shelley, because he knew I hated it and it made him laugh to see me get annoyed and then laugh too. "Rachel, it's me." His voice is heavy, he sounds tired. "Listen, you have to stop this, okay?" I don't say anything. The train is slowing down, and we're almost at my old house. I want to say to him, Come outside, stand on the grass. Let me see you. "Please, Rachel, you can't keep calling me like this. You need to get yourself together." I have a lump in my throat, hard and smooth like a stone. I can't swallow. I can't speak. "Rachel? Are you there? I know you're not doing well, and I'm sorry, I really am, but... I can't help you, and these calls are upsetting Anna. Okay? I can't help you anymore. Go to AA or something. Please, Rachel. Go to an AA meeting after work today."

I pull the dirty bandage off my finger and look at the pale, wrinkled skin underneath, with dried blood around my fingernail. I press my right thumbnail into the cut and feel it open up, the pain sharp and hot. I gasp. Blood starts to come out of the wound. The girls across from me are watching me, their faces blank.

MEGAN

• • •

One year earlier

WEDNESDAY, MAY 16, 2012

MORNING

I hear the train coming; I know its sound well. It gets faster as it leaves Northcote station. Then, after going around the curve, it slows down. It goes from a fast rattle to a low rumble, and sometimes the brakes screech as it stops at the signal near the house. My coffee is cold, but I'm too warm and relaxed to get up for more.

Sometimes I just listen to the trains, without looking. Sitting here in the morning, with my eyes closed and the sun warm on my face, I could be anywhere. I could be in Spain, at the beach. I could be in Italy, with the colorful houses and trains carrying tourists. I could be back in Holkham, hearing seagulls and tasting salt, with an old train track nearby.

The train isn't stopping today; it goes slowly past. I hear the wheels on the tracks and feel it rocking. I can't see the people on the train, and I know they're just going to work. But I can imagine other trips, to exciting places. I often think about Holkham, especially on mornings like this. I remember it fondly, with longing. The wind in the grass, the big sky, the old house with mice, candles, dirt, and music. It feels like a dream now.

My heart is beating a little too fast.

I hear him walking on the stairs, he says my name.

"Do you want another coffee, Megs?"

The spell is broken, I'm awake.

EVENING

I feel cool from the breeze and warm from the small amount of vodka in my martini. I'm on the terrace, waiting for Scott to come home. I'm going to try to get him to take me to dinner at the Italian place on Kingly Road. We haven't gone out in a long time.

I haven't done much today. I was supposed to work on my application for the fabric course at St. Martins. I did start it, working in the kitchen, when I heard a woman screaming. It was a horrible noise, and I thought someone was being killed. I ran outside to the garden, but I couldn't see anything.

I could still hear her, though. It was awful, and it really bothered me. Her voice was very high and desperate. "What are you doing? What are you doing with her? Give her to me, give her to me." It seemed to go on for a long time, but it probably only lasted a few seconds.

I ran upstairs and went out onto the terrace. I could see, through the trees, two women by the fence a few gardens away. One of them was crying—maybe both were—and a child was crying loudly, too.

I thought about calling the police, but it seemed to calm down. The woman who had been screaming ran into the house, carrying the baby. The other woman stayed outside. She ran towards the house, stumbled, got up, and then just walked around the garden in circles. It was very strange. I have no idea what was happening. But it's the most exciting thing that's happened in weeks.

My days feel empty now that I don't have the gallery to go to anymore. I really miss it. I miss talking to the artists. I even miss dealing with the annoying mothers who used to come in with their Starbucks, looking at the pictures and saying their kids could do better.

Sometimes I want to see if I can find anyone from the old days, but then I think, what would we talk about now? They wouldn't even know me as the happily married person in the suburbs. Anyway, I shouldn't look back, it's never a good idea. I'll wait until the summer is over, then I'll look for work. It seems like a waste to not enjoy these long summer days. I'll find something, here or somewhere else, I know I will.

TUESDAY, AUGUST 14, 2012

MORNING

I'm standing in front of my closet, looking again at my nice clothes. They're perfect for a manager of a cool art gallery. But none of them look like something a nanny would wear. The word "nanny" even makes me feel sick. I put on jeans and a T-shirt and pull my hair back. I don't even put on makeup. What's the point of trying to look nice if I'm just going to spend the day with a baby?

I stomp downstairs, ready to argue. Scott is making coffee. He smiles at me, and I feel better right away. I change my frown into a smile. He gives me coffee and kisses me.

It's not his fault. This was my idea. I offered to watch the baby for the people down the street. I thought it would be fun. I must have been crazy. Bored, crazy, curious. I wanted to see what it was like. I think I got the idea after I heard the mom yelling in the garden. I wanted to know what was going on. But I haven't asked, of course. You can't really, can you?

Scott was happy when I suggested it. He thinks being around babies will make me want one. But it's doing the opposite. When I leave their house, I rush home to shower and wash the baby smell off me.

I miss my days at the gallery, when I was dressed up, talking to adults about art or movies or anything. Talking about nothing would be better than talking to Anna. She's so boring! I think she used to have her own thoughts, but now she only talks about the baby: Is she warm enough? Is she too hot? How much milk did she drink? And she's always there, so I feel like I'm just extra. My job is to watch the baby while Anna rests. A break from what? She's also very nervous. I always feel her watching me. She jumps when the phone rings. "They're just so fragile, aren't they?" she says, and I agree.

I walk slowly to their house, just fifty yards away. I'm not excited at all. Today, the husband opens the door. Tom is wearing a suit, ready for work. He looks good in his suit, but not as good as Scott. He's smaller and paler, and his eyes are a little too close together. But he's not bad. He gives me a big smile, like Tom Cruise, and then he's gone. Now it's just me, Anna, and the baby.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 16, 2012

AFTERNOON

I quit!

I feel so much better, like I can do anything. I'm free!

I'm sitting on the porch, waiting for the rain. The sky is dark above me, birds flying around, and the air is heavy with water. Scott will be home in about an hour, and I have to tell him. He'll be mad for a little bit, but I'll make it up to him. And I won't just stay home all day: I've been making plans. I could take a photography class, or have a stand at a market, selling jewelry. I could learn to cook.

A teacher at school once told me I was good at changing myself. I didn't understand what he meant then, I thought he was joking, but now I like the idea. I've been a runaway, a lover, a wife, a waitress, a gallery manager, a nanny, and more. So, who do I want to be tomorrow?

I didn't really mean to quit, the words just came out. We were sitting at the kitchen table, Anna with the baby, and Tom came back to get something, so he was there too, drinking coffee. It just felt silly, like there was no reason for me to be there. Even worse, I felt like I was in the way.

"I found another job," I said, without thinking. "So I can't do this anymore." Anna looked at me—I don't think she believed me. She just said, "Oh, that's too bad," and I could tell she didn't mean it. She looked happy. She didn't even ask what the job was, which was good, because I didn't have a good lie ready.

Tom looked a little surprised. He said, "We'll miss you," but that's not true either.

The only person who will really be sad is Scott, so I need to think of something to tell him. Maybe I'll tell him Tom was flirting with me. That will stop it.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 20, 2012

MORNING

It's a little after seven, and it's cold outside. But it's beautiful like this, with all the garden strips next to each other. They're green and cold, waiting for the sun to reach them and make them come alive. I've been awake for hours; I can't sleep. I haven't slept in days. I hate this, I hate not sleeping more than anything. I just lie there, my brain going round and round. I feel itchy all over. I want to shave my head.

I want to run away. I want to take a road trip in a convertible with the top down. I want to drive to any coast and walk on the beach. My big brother and I were going to be road trippers. We had big plans, Ben and I. Well, they were mostly Ben's plans—he was such a dreamer. We were going to ride motorcycles from Paris to the French Riviera, or down the Pacific coast of the USA, from Seattle to Los Angeles. We were going to follow Che Guevara's route from Buenos Aires to Caracas. Maybe if I had done all that, I wouldn't be here now, not knowing what to do. Or maybe, if I had done all that, I would still be here and be happy. But I didn't do any of that, because Ben never made it to Paris. He didn't even make it to Cambridge. He died on the A10, his head crushed under a truck.

I miss him every day. More than anyone, I think. He's a big hole in my life, in my soul. Or maybe he was just the start of it. I don't know. I don't even know if this is really about Ben, or about everything that happened after that, and everything since. All I know is, one minute I'm fine and life is good, and the next I can't wait to leave. I'm all over the place again.

So, I'm going to see a therapist! It might be weird, but it could also be fun. I've always thought it would be nice to be Catholic, to go to confession and tell someone everything, and have them forgive you and make you feel clean.

This isn't the same thing, of course. I'm a bit nervous, but I haven't been able to sleep, and Scott has been telling me to go. I told him it's hard enough talking to people I know about this stuff—I can barely talk to him about it. He said that's the point, you can say anything to strangers. But that's not really true. You can't just say anything. Poor Scott. He doesn't know the half of it. He loves me so much, it hurts. I don't know how he does it. I would drive me crazy.

But I have to do something, and this feels like action. All those plans I had—photography and cooking classes—they feel pointless, like I'm just pretending to live. I need to find something I must do, something real. I can't just be a wife. I don't understand how anyone does it—there's nothing to do but wait. Wait for a man to come home and love you. Or find something to distract you.

EVENING

I've been waiting. My appointment was half an hour ago, and I'm still here, looking through Vogue in the waiting room. I'm thinking about leaving. I know doctors' appointments can be late, but therapists? Movies make it seem like they kick you out as soon as your time is up. I guess Hollywood isn't talking about the kind of therapist you get from the NHS.

I'm about to tell the receptionist I'm leaving when the doctor's door opens. A tall, thin man comes out, looking sorry and holding out his hand to me.

"Mrs. Hipwell, I'm so sorry to keep you waiting," he says. I smile and tell him it's okay. I feel like it will be okay, because I've only been with him for a moment and I already feel calm.

I think it's his voice. It's soft and quiet. He has a slight accent, which I expected because his name is Dr. Kamal Abdic. I guess he's in his mid-thirties, but he looks very young with his amazing dark, honey-colored skin. He has hands I could imagine touching me, long and delicate fingers. I can almost feel them on my skin.

We don't talk about anything important. It's just the first meeting, the getting-to-know-you stuff. He asks me what's wrong, and I tell him about the panic attacks, the trouble sleeping, and how I lie awake at night, too scared to fall asleep. He wants me to talk more about that, but I'm not ready yet. He asks if I use drugs or drink alcohol. I tell him I have other bad habits these days, and I look at him, and I think he knows what I mean. Then I feel like I should be taking this more seriously, so I tell him about the gallery closing and how I feel lost all the time, without direction, and that I spend too much time thinking. He doesn't say much, just asks a question now and then, but I want to hear him talk, so as I'm leaving, I ask him where he's from.

"Maidstone," he says, "in Kent. But I moved to Corly a few years ago." He knows that wasn't what I was asking. He gives me a sly smile.

Scott is waiting for me when I get home. He shoves a drink into my hand and wants to know everything about it. I say it was okay. He asks about the therapist: did I like him, did he seem nice? Okay, I say again, because I don't want to seem too excited. He asks if we talked about Ben. Scott thinks everything is about Ben. He might be right. He might know me better than I think.

TUESDAY, SEPTEMBER 25, 2012

MORNING

I woke up early this morning, but I did sleep for a few hours, which is better than last week. I felt almost refreshed when I got out of bed, so instead of sitting on the patio, I decided to go for a walk.

I've been keeping to myself, almost without realizing it. The only places I seem to go these days are the shops, my Pilates classes, and the therapist. Sometimes I go to Tara's. The rest of the time, I'm at home. It's no wonder I feel restless.

I walk out of the house, turn right, and then left onto Kingly Road. I walk past the pub, the Rose. We used to go there all the time. I can't remember why we stopped. I never liked it that much. There were too many couples, just under forty, drinking too much and looking for something better, wondering if they were brave enough. Maybe that's why we stopped going, because I didn't like it. Past the pub, past the shops. I don't want to go far, just a short walk to stretch my legs.

It's nice being out early, before the school rush, before people go to work. The streets are empty and clean, and the day feels full of possibilities. I turn left again and walk down to the small playground, the only small green space we have. It's empty now, but in a few hours, it will be full of toddlers, mothers, and nannies. Half the Pilates girls will be here, dressed in Sweaty Betty clothes, stretching competitively, with their perfect nails wrapped around their Starbucks cups.

I walk past the park and down Roseberry Avenue. If I turned right, I'd go past my old gallery—now just an empty shop—but I don't want to. It still hurts a bit. I tried so hard to make it work. It was the wrong place and time. There wasn't a need for art in this area, not with the way the economy is. Instead, I turn right, past the small Tesco, past the other pub where people from the local housing area go, and head home. I feel nervous now, like I have butterflies in my stomach. I'm worried about seeing the Watsons. It's always awkward because it's clear I don't have a new job. I lied because I didn't want to work for them anymore.

It's really awkward when I see her. Tom just ignores me. But Anna seems to take it personally. She probably thinks I stopped being a nanny because of her or her child. It wasn't about her child at all, even though the child cried a lot, which made her hard to like. It's more complicated than that, but I can't explain it to her. Anyway, that's one reason I've been staying inside. I don't want to see the Watsons. Part of me hopes they'll move. I know she doesn't like it here. She hates the house, living with his ex-wife's things, and the trains.

I stop at the corner and look into the underpass. The cold, damp smell always makes me shiver. It's like looking under a rock and seeing moss, worms, and dirt. It reminds me of playing in the garden as a kid, looking for frogs with Ben. I keep walking. The street is empty—no sign of Tom or Anna—and the part of me that likes drama is actually a little disappointed.

EVENING

Scott just called to say he has to work late, which is not what I wanted to hear. I feel restless and have been all day. I can't sit still. I need him to come home and calm me down. Now it's going to be hours before he gets here, and my mind will keep racing. I know I won't sleep well tonight.

I can't just sit here watching the trains. I'm too jumpy. My heart feels like it's fluttering, like a bird trying to escape. I put on my flip-flops and go downstairs, out the front door, and onto Blenheim Road. It's about 7:30 pm. A few people are walking home from work. There's no one else around, but I can hear kids playing in their backyards, enjoying the last of the summer sun before they have to go in for dinner.

I walk down the road toward the station. I stop for a moment outside number twenty-three and think about ringing the doorbell. What would I say? That I needed sugar? That I just wanted to chat? Their blinds are partly open, but I can't see anyone inside.

I keep walking toward the corner and, without thinking, I go down into the underpass. I'm halfway through when a train goes overhead. It's amazing, like an earthquake. I can feel it in my whole body. I look down and see something on the floor, a purple hair band, stretched out and used a lot. A runner probably dropped it, but it makes me feel uneasy, and I want to get out of there quickly, back into the sunshine.

On the way back up the road, he drives past me in his car. Our eyes meet for a second, and he smiles at me.

RACHEL

• • •

FRIDAY, JULY 12, 2013

MORNING

I'm very tired, my head feels heavy with sleep. When I drink, I barely sleep. I fall asleep hard for an hour or two, then I wake up, feeling scared and disgusted with myself. If I have a day without drinking, I sleep very deeply that night. I'm completely unconscious, and in the morning, I can't wake up easily. The sleep stays with me for hours, sometimes all day.

There are only a few people in my train car today, and no one is close to me. No one is watching me, so I lean my head against the window and close my eyes.

The loud screech of the train's brakes wakes me up. We're at the signal. At this time of morning, and this time of year, the sun shines right onto the back of the houses by the tracks, filling them with light. I can almost feel the warm morning sun on my face and arms as I sit at the breakfast table. Tom is across from me, my bare feet are on top of his because they're always warmer than mine. I look down at the newspaper. I can feel him smiling at me, and I blush from my chest to my neck, like I always did when he looked at me a certain way.

I blink hard, and Tom is gone. We're still at the signal. I see Jess in her garden, and behind her, a man is walking out of the house. He's carrying something, maybe mugs of coffee. I look at him and realize it's not Jason. This man is taller, thinner, and darker. He's a family friend, maybe her brother or Jason's brother. He bends down and puts the mugs on the metal table on their patio. He's a cousin from Australia, visiting for a couple of weeks. Or maybe he's Jason's oldest friend, the best man at their wedding. Jess walks to him, puts her arms around his waist, and kisses him for a long time. The train starts moving.

I can't believe it. I gasp for air and realize I was holding my breath. Why would she do that? Jason loves her, I can see it, they're happy. I can't believe she would do that to him, he doesn't deserve it. I feel very disappointed, like I have been cheated on. A familiar pain fills my chest. I've felt this way before. It was worse before, but I remember the feeling of the pain. You don't forget it.

I found out the way everyone seems to find out these days: through a digital mistake. Sometimes it's a text or a voice message. In my case, it was an email, like a modern version of finding lipstick on a collar. It was an accident, I wasn't trying to find anything. I wasn't supposed to use Tom's computer because he was worried I would delete something important by mistake, or click on something I shouldn't and get a virus or something. "Technology isn't really your strong point, is it, Rach?" he said after I accidentally deleted all the contacts in his email address book. So I wasn't supposed to touch it. But I was actually trying to do something nice. I was trying to make up for being a bit sad and difficult. I was planning a special fourth-anniversary trip, to remind us of how we used to be. I wanted it to be a surprise, so I had to secretly check his work schedule. I had to look.

I wasn't looking around secretly, or trying to trick him. I knew better than that. I didn't want to be a wife who checks her husband's pockets. Once, I answered his phone when he was in the shower, and he got upset, saying I didn't trust him. I felt bad because he seemed so hurt.

I needed to see his work schedule. He'd left his laptop on because he was late for a meeting. It was a good chance, so I looked at his calendar and wrote down some dates. When I closed the calendar, his email was open. There was a message at the top from aboyd@cinnamon.com. I clicked it. XXXXX. That was it, just _X_s. I thought it was spam at first, then I realized they were kisses.

It was a reply to a message he'd sent a few hours before, just after seven, when I was still asleep.

"I fell asleep last night thinking of you. I dreamed about kissing your mouth, your breasts, the inside of your thighs. I woke up this morning wanting to touch you. I can't think straight, not with you."

I read his messages. There were many, hidden in a folder called "Admin." I found out her name was Anna Boyd, and my husband was in love with her. He told her that often. He said he'd never felt like this before, that he couldn't wait to be with her, and that they'd be together soon.

I can't explain how I felt that day. Now, on the train, I'm angry. My nails are digging into my palms, and tears are in my eyes. I feel a sudden, strong anger. I feel like something has been taken from me. How could she? How could Jess do this? What's wrong with her? Look at their life, it's so good! I don't understand how people can ignore the harm they cause by following their feelings. Who said following your heart is good? It's selfish and wants to take over everything. I feel hatred. If I saw that woman now, if I saw Jess, I would spit in her face. I would scratch her eyes out.

EVENING

There's a problem on the train line. The 5:56 train to Stoke was canceled, so those people are on my train. It's very crowded. I have a seat, but it's by the aisle, not the window. People are pushing against my shoulder and knee. I want to push back and shove them. The heat has been building all day, and I feel like I'm breathing through a mask. All the windows are open, but the train feels stuffy, like a locked metal box. I can't get enough air. I feel sick. I keep thinking about the coffee shop this morning. I can't stop feeling like I'm still there, seeing their faces.

I blame Jess. This morning, I was thinking about Jess and Jason, about what she did and how he would feel, about their fight when he finds out and his world falls apart like mine. I was walking around confused, not paying attention. Without thinking, I went into the coffee shop that people from Huntingdon Whitely use. I was inside before I saw them. It was too late to turn back. They were looking at me, their eyes widening for a second before they smiled. Martin Miles with Sasha and Harriet, all awkward, were waving me over.

"Rachel!" Martin said, opening his arms and hugging me. I wasn't expecting it. My hands were stuck between us, touching his body. Sasha and Harriet smiled and gave me quick air-kisses, trying not to get too close. "What are you doing here?"

For a long moment, my mind went blank. I looked at the floor, feeling my face turn red. Knowing it made things worse, I forced a laugh and said, “Interview. Interview.”

“Oh.” Martin looked surprised, while Sasha and Harriet nodded and smiled. “Who is it with?”

I couldn't think of a single public relations company. Not one. I also couldn't think of a real estate company, especially one that might be hiring. I just stood there, rubbing my lip with my finger and shaking my head. Finally, Martin said, “Top secret, is it? Some companies are like that, right? They don't want you to say anything until the contracts are signed.” It was a lie, and he knew it. He was trying to help me, but no one believed it. Still, everyone pretended to and nodded. Harriet and Sasha were looking at the door, embarrassed for me and wanting to leave.

“I should go get my coffee,” I said. “Don't want to be late.”

Martin put his hand on my arm and said, “It’s good to see you, Rachel.” I could almost feel his pity. I had never realized how awful it is to be pitied until the last year or two.

I had planned to go to Holborn Library, but I couldn't. Instead, I went to Regent’s Park. I walked to the far end, near the zoo. I sat under a tree, thinking about the empty hours ahead. I replayed the conversation in the coffee shop, remembering Martin’s face when he said goodbye.

I had been there for less than half an hour when my phone rang. It was Tom again, calling from the house phone. I tried to picture him working on his laptop in our sunny kitchen, but the image was ruined by his new life. She would be there, making tea or feeding the little girl, her shadow falling over him. I let the call go to voicemail. I put the phone back in my bag and tried to ignore it. I didn't want to hear anything more. Today was already bad enough, and it wasn't even 10:30 in the morning. I waited about three minutes before checking my voicemail. I prepared myself to hear his voice—the voice that used to be full of laughter and now was only used to scold or comfort or pity—but it wasn't him.

“Rachel, it’s Anna.” I hung up.

I couldn't breathe. My mind was racing, and my skin was itching. I got up and walked to the corner shop and bought four canned gin and tonics. Then, I went back to my spot in the park. I opened the first one and drank it quickly, then opened the second. I turned away from the path so I couldn't see the runners, mothers with strollers, and tourists. If I couldn't see them, I could pretend they couldn't see me. I checked my voicemail again.

“Rachel, it’s Anna.” Long pause. “I need to talk to you about the phone calls.” Another long pause—she's talking to me while doing something else, like busy wives and mothers do, cleaning up or doing laundry. “Look, I know you’re having a hard time,” she says, as if she has nothing to do with my pain, “but you can’t call us at night all the time.” Her voice is sharp and annoyed. “It’s bad enough that you wake us up, but you wake Evie, too, and that’s not okay. We’re having trouble getting her to sleep through the night.” We’re having trouble getting her to sleep through. We. Us. Our little family. With our problems and routines. Fucking bitch. She's a cuckoo, laying her egg in my nest. She has taken everything from me. She has taken everything, and now she calls to tell me that my pain is inconvenient for her?

I finish the second can and start on the third. The happy feeling from the alcohol only lasts a few minutes, and then I feel sick. I'm drinking too fast, even for me. I need to slow down, or something bad will happen. I'm going to do something I'll regret. I'm going to call her back and tell her I don't care about her or her family. I don't care if her child never sleeps well. I'm going to tell her that the line he used with her— don't expect me to be sane —he used it with me too, when we first met. He wrote it in a letter, saying he loved me deeply. It's not even his line; he stole it from Henry Miller. Everything she has is used. I want to know how that makes her feel. I want to call her and ask, How does it feel, Anna, to live in my house, with the furniture I bought, to sleep in the bed I shared with him, to feed your child at the kitchen table he had sex with me on?

I still can't believe they stayed there, in that house, in my house. I was shocked when he told me. I loved that house. I was the one who wanted to buy it, even though it was by the train tracks. I liked being there, watching the trains go by. I liked the sound of them, not the loud noise of a city train, but the old-fashioned sound of older trains. Tom told me, "It won't always be like this, they'll make the line faster and the trains will be loud," but I didn't think it would happen. I would have stayed there. I would have bought him out if I had the money. But I didn't, and we couldn't sell it for a good price when we divorced. So, he said he'd buy me out and stay there until he could sell it. But he never found a buyer. Instead, he moved her in, and she loved the house like I did, and they decided to stay. She must feel very sure of herself and them, for it not to bother her, to be where another woman has been. She probably doesn't think of me as a threat. I think about Ted Hughes, moving Assia Wevill into the house he shared with Plath, her wearing Sylvia's clothes, using the same hairbrush. I want to call Anna and remind her that Assia killed herself, just like Sylvia did.

I must have fallen asleep, the gin and the sun making me sleepy. I woke up suddenly, looking for my bag. It was still there. My skin was itchy, I was covered in ants. They were in my hair, on my neck and chest. I jumped up, trying to get them off. Two teenage boys, playing football nearby, stopped to watch and laughed.

The train stops. We are almost across from Jess and Jason's house, but I can't see because there are too many people in the way. I wonder if they are there, if he knows, if he's left, or if he's still living a life that he doesn't know is a lie.

SATURDAY, JULY 13, 2013

MORNING

I know it's between seven forty-five and eight fifteen without looking at a clock. I know from the light, the sounds outside, and Cathy vacuuming right outside my room. Cathy cleans the house early every Saturday, no matter what. Even if it's her birthday, she will clean. She says it makes her feel good and ready for the weekend. She also says it's exercise, so she doesn't have to go to the gym.

The early vacuuming doesn't bother me because I wouldn't be asleep anyway. I can't sleep in the mornings. I wake up suddenly, my heart racing, my mouth dry, and I know I'm awake. The more I want to sleep, the less I can. Life won't let me. I lie there, listening to Cathy cleaning, and I think about the clothes by the train tracks and Jess kissing her lover in the sun.

The day is long and empty.

I could go to the farmer's market. I could buy meat and cook all day.

I could relax on the couch with tea and watch Saturday Kitchen on TV.

I could go to the gym.

I could fix my resume.

I could wait for Cathy to leave, go to the store, and buy two bottles of white wine.

In another life, I also woke up early to the sound of the 8:04 train. I opened my eyes and listened to the rain on the window. I felt him behind me, sleepy, warm, and close. Later, he got the newspaper, and I made scrambled eggs. We drank tea in the kitchen, had a late lunch at the pub, and fell asleep together in front of the TV. I think it's different for him now. No lazy Saturday sex or scrambled eggs. Instead, he has a different kind of happiness, a little girl between him and his wife, talking away. She's probably learning to talk now, saying "Dada" and "Mama" and using a secret language only parents understand.

The pain is strong and heavy in my chest. I really want Cathy to leave the house.

EVENING

I am going to see Jason.

I stayed in my room all day, waiting for Cathy to leave so I could drink. She didn't. She stayed in the living room, "doing some paperwork." By late afternoon, I couldn't take being stuck inside or being bored anymore. So, I told her I was going for a walk. I went to the Wheatsheaf, a big, normal pub near High Street, and drank three large glasses of wine. I also had two shots of Jack Daniel's. Then, I walked to the train station, bought two cans of gin and tonic, and got on the train.

I am going to see Jason.

I won't go see him. I won't show up at his house and knock. Nothing like that. Nothing wild. I just want to go by the house, on the train. I have nothing else to do, and I don't want to go home. I just want to see him. I want to see them.

This is not a good idea. I know it's not a good idea.

But what's the harm?

I'll go to Euston, turn around, and come back. (I like trains, and what's wrong with that? Trains are great.)

Before, when I was still myself, I dreamed of taking romantic train trips with Tom. (The Bergen Line for our fifth anniversary, the Blue Train for his fortieth.)

Wait, we're about to pass them.

The light is bright, but I can't see well. (My vision is blurry. Close one eye. Better.)

There they are! Is that him? They're on the patio. Right? Is that Jason? Is that Jess?

I want to be closer, I can't see. I want to be closer to them.

I'm not going to Euston. I'm getting off at Witney. (I shouldn't get off at Witney, it's too risky. What if Tom or Anna sees me?)

I will get off at Witney.

This is not a good plan.

This is a very bad plan.

There's a man across the train with light brown hair that looks a little red. He's smiling at me. I want to talk to him, but I can't say anything. The words disappear before I can speak. I can almost taste them, but I don't know if they are good or bad.

Is he smiling or making fun of me? I don't know.

SUNDAY, JULY 14, 2013

MORNING

My heart feels like it's in my throat, loud and uncomfortable. My mouth is dry, and it hurts to swallow. I turn to my side, facing the window. The curtains are closed, but the little light that gets through hurts my eyes. I put my hand on my face and press my fingers on my eyelids, trying to make the pain go away. My fingernails are dirty.

Something is wrong. For a moment, I feel like I'm falling, like the bed is gone. Last night. Something happened. I breathe in quickly and sit up too fast. My heart is racing, and my head hurts.

I wait for the memory to come back. Sometimes it takes a while. Sometimes I remember right away. Sometimes I don't remember at all.

Something bad happened. We argued. We yelled. Maybe we fought? I don't know, I don't remember. I went to a bar, then the train, then the station, then the street. Blenheim Road. I went to Blenheim Road.

A wave of fear washes over me.

Something happened, I know it. I can't see it, but I can feel it. My mouth hurts, like I bit my cheek. I taste blood. I feel sick and dizzy. I touch my hair. I feel a painful bump on the right side of my head. My hair is bloody.

I must have tripped. On the stairs at Witney station. Did I hit my head? I remember the train, but then it's all black. I breathe deeply, trying to calm down. Think. What did I do? I went to the bar, then the train. A man was there, with red hair. He smiled at me. I think he talked to me, but I don't remember what he said. There's more to him, but I can't remember it.

I'm scared, but I don't know why. This makes it worse. I don't even know if I should be scared. I look around. My phone isn't on the table. My bag isn't on the floor or the chair. I must have had it, because I have my keys.

I get out of bed. I'm naked. I see myself in the mirror. My hands shake. My makeup is smeared, and my lip is cut. My legs are bruised. I feel sick. I sit down and put my head between my knees, waiting for the sickness to pass. I get up, grab my robe, and open the door a little. The apartment is quiet. I think Cathy isn't here. Did she say she was staying at Damien's? I think she did, but I don't remember when. Before I went out? Or later? I walk quietly to the hallway. Cathy's door is open. I look in. Her bed is made. Maybe she got up early, but I don't think she stayed here last night. That's good. If she wasn't here, she didn't see me come in, so she doesn't know how bad I was. This shouldn't matter, but it does. I feel more ashamed when more people see what happened.

At the top of the stairs, I feel dizzy again and hold the railing. I'm scared of falling down the stairs and breaking my neck. This makes me feel sick again. I want to lie down, but I need to find my bag and phone. I need to know if I lost my cards and who I called. My bag is in the hallway, by the door. My clothes are next to it, and I can smell pee. I grab my bag and find my phone, thank God. There's also money and a bloody tissue. I feel sick again, and I throw up on the stairs.

I have to lie down. If I don't, I'll pass out. I'll clean up later.

Upstairs, I plug in my phone and lie on the bed. I look at my arms and legs. My legs are bruised, like from bumping into things. My upper arms have dark, oval marks, like fingerprints. This isn't good, but it could be from someone helping me up. My head hurts, but it could be from getting into a car. I might have taken a taxi home.

I pick up my phone. There are two messages. One is from Cathy, from 5:00, asking where I am. She's at Damien's for the night and will see me tomorrow. She hopes I'm not drinking alone. The other is from Tom, from 10:15. I almost drop the phone when I hear his voice; he's yelling.

"Rachel, what is wrong with you? I've had enough of this. I spent almost an hour driving around looking for you. You scared Anna. She thought you were going to... she thought... It was hard to stop her from calling the police. Leave us alone. Stop calling me, stop coming around, just leave us alone. I don't want to talk to you. Do you understand? I don't want to talk to you, I don't want to see you, I don't want you near my family. You can mess up your own life, but not mine. Not anymore. I won't protect you anymore, okay? Just stay away from us."

I don't know what I did. What did I do? Between five o'clock and ten fifteen, what was I doing? Why was Tom looking for me? What did I do to Anna? I pull the blanket over my head and close my eyes tightly. I imagine going to their house, walking on the path between their yard and the neighbor's yard, climbing over the fence. I think about sliding open the glass doors and quietly going into the kitchen. Anna is sitting at the table. I grab her from behind, I grab her long blond hair, I pull her head back, I pull her to the floor and smash her head against the blue tiles.

EVENING

Someone is yelling. The light coming through my window tells me I've been sleeping a long time. It must be late afternoon or early evening. My head hurts. There's blood on my pillow. I hear someone yelling downstairs.

"I can't believe this! For goodness sake! Rachel! RACHEL!"

I fell asleep. Oh no, and I didn't clean up the throw up on the stairs. And my clothes in the hallway. Oh no, oh no.

I put on some sweatpants and a t-shirt. Cathy is standing right outside my door when I open it. She looks shocked when she sees me.

"What happened to you?" she says, then raises her hand. "Actually, Rachel, I don't want to know. I can't have this in my house. I can't have..." She stops talking, but she's looking back down the hall, towards the stairs.

"I'm sorry," I say. "I'm so sorry, I was sick and I was going to clean it up—"

"You weren't sick, were you? You were drunk. You were hungover. I'm sorry, Rachel. I can't have this. I can't live like this. You have to leave, okay? I'll give you four weeks to find a new place, but then you have to go." She turns around and walks to her bedroom. "And please, clean up that mess?" She slams her bedroom door.

After I finish cleaning, I go back to my room. Cathy's door is still closed, but I can feel her anger through it. I don't blame her. I'd be angry if I came home to pee-soaked underwear and vomit on the stairs. I sit on the bed and open my laptop, log into my email, and start writing to my mother. I think it's time. I need to ask her for help. If I moved home, I couldn't keep living like this. I would have to change and get better. But I can't find the words to explain it to her. I imagine her face as she reads my request, the disappointment and frustration. I can almost hear her sigh.

My phone beeps. I have a message from hours ago. It's Tom again. I don't want to hear it, but I have to. My heart beats faster as I check my voicemail, bracing for the worst.

"Rachel, can you call me back?" He doesn't sound as angry, and my heart slows a little. "I want to know you got home okay. You were not doing well last night." A long sigh. "I'm sorry I yelled last night, that things got too heated. I feel sorry for you, Rachel, I really do, but this has to stop."

I listen to the message again, hearing the kindness in his voice, and I start to cry. It takes a long time before I stop and can text him that I'm sorry and I'm home. I can't say more because I don't know what I'm sorry for. I don't know what I did to Anna or how I scared her. I don't really care that much, but I do care about making Tom unhappy. He deserves to be happy after everything he's been through. I want him to be happy, even if it can't be with me.

I lie on the bed and get under the covers. I want to know what happened; I wish I knew what I did wrong. I try to remember a small piece of what happened. I think I was in an argument, or I saw one. Was it with Anna? I touch the cut on my head and lip. I can almost see it and hear the words, but it goes away again. I can't remember it. Every time I think I'm close, it disappears, just out of reach.

MEGAN

• • •

TUESDAY, OCTOBER 2, 2012

MORNING

It's going to rain soon, I can feel it. My teeth are shaking, and my fingertips are pale and a little blue. I don't want to go inside. I like it out here; it feels good, like a cold bath. Scott will probably come get me soon anyway. He'll wrap me in blankets, like I'm a kid.

I had a panic attack on my way home last night. A motorcycle was making a lot of noise, and a red car was driving very slowly. Two women with strollers were blocking the sidewalk. I couldn't get around them, so I walked into the street and almost got hit by a car. I didn't even see it coming. The driver honked and yelled at me. I couldn't breathe, my heart was racing, and I felt that sick feeling in my stomach, like when you're about to feel the effects of a drug. It was a rush of adrenaline that made me feel sick, excited, and scared all at once.

I ran home, through the house, and down to the train tracks. I sat there, waiting for a train to pass and take away the other noises. I waited for Scott to come and calm me down, but he wasn't home. I tried to climb over the fence to sit on the other side, where no one goes. I cut my hand, so I went inside. Then Scott came home and asked what happened. I said I was washing dishes and dropped a glass. He didn't believe me and got very upset.

I got up in the night, left Scott sleeping, and went to the patio. I called his phone and listened to his voice when he answered. At first, he sounded sleepy, then louder, worried, and annoyed. I hung up and waited to see if he would call back. I didn't hide my number, so I thought he might. He didn't, so I called again and again. Then I got his voicemail, which was very formal and said he would call me back soon. I thought about calling my doctor's office to move up my next appointment, but I don't think their system works in the middle of the night, so I went back to bed. I didn't sleep at all.

I might go to Corly Wood this morning to take some pictures. It will be foggy and dark, which will look cool. I should be able to get some good photos. I was thinking about making little cards and trying to sell them at the gift shop on Kingly Road. Scott keeps saying I don't need to work and that I should just rest. Like I'm sick! The last thing I need is rest. I need to find something to do with my time. I know what will happen if I don't.

EVENING

Dr. Abdic—Kamal, who told me to call him that—said in our meeting today that I should start a diary. I almost said, I can't do that, my husband might read it. I didn't say it because it would feel like I was betraying Scott. But it's true. I could never write down my real feelings or thoughts or actions. For example, when I got home tonight, my laptop was warm. He knows how to delete his search history and stuff, so he can hide what he does, but I know I turned the computer off before I left. He's reading my emails again.

I don't really care, there's nothing interesting in them. (Just spam from job companies and Jenny from Pilates asking me to join her dinner club, where she and her friends cook for each other. I'd hate that.) I don't care because it makes him feel better that I'm not doing anything wrong. And that's good for me—it's good for us—even if it's not true. And I can't be mad at him because he has a reason to be suspicious. I've given him reasons in the past and probably will again. I'm not a perfect wife. I can't be. No matter how much I love him, it won't be enough.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 13, 2012

MORNING

I slept for five hours last night, which is more than I have in a long time. It's weird because I was so energetic when I got home yesterday, I thought I'd be up all night. I told myself I wouldn't do it again, not after last time, but then I saw him and I wanted him and I thought, why not? I don't see why I should have to stop myself, lots of people don't. Men don't. I don't want to hurt anyone, but you have to be true to yourself, right? That's all I'm doing, being true to my real self, the self nobody knows—not Scott, not Kamal, no one.

After Pilates last night, I asked Tara if she wanted to see a movie with me next week, and if she'd cover for me.

"If he calls, can you just say I'm with you, that I'm in the bathroom and I'll call him back? Then you call me, and I call him, and it's all good."

She smiled and said, "Okay." She didn't even ask where I was going or who with. She really wants to be my friend.

I met him at the Swan in Corly, he got us a room. We have to be careful, we can't get caught. It would be bad for him, it would ruin his life. It would be a disaster for me, too. I don't even want to think about what Scott would do.

He wanted me to talk after, about what happened when I was young, living in Norwich. I'd mentioned it before, but last night he wanted the details. I told him things, but not the truth. I lied, made things up, told him all the bad things he wanted to hear. It was fun. I don't feel bad about lying, I don't think he believed most of it anyway. I'm pretty sure he lies, too.

He was on the bed, looking at me as I put on my clothes. He said, “This can’t happen again, Megan. You know it can’t. We can’t keep doing this.” And he was right, I know we can’t. We shouldn’t, but we will. It won’t be the last time. He won’t refuse me. I thought about it on the way home, and that’s what I like most about it, having control over someone. That’s the exciting thing.

EVENING

I’m in the kitchen, opening a bottle of wine, when Scott comes up behind me. He puts his hands on my shoulders, squeezes them, and says, “How did it go with the therapist?” I tell him it was fine, that we’re getting better. He’s used to me not telling him any details. Then: “Did you have fun with Tara last night?”

I can’t tell, because my back is to him, if he’s really asking or if he thinks something happened. I can’t hear anything in his voice.

“She’s really nice,” I say. “You would like her. We’re going to the movies next week. Maybe I should bring her over for dinner after?”

“Am I not invited to the movies?” he asks.

“You’re welcome to come,” I say, and I turn to him and kiss him, “but she wants to see that movie with Sandra Bullock, so . . .”

“Okay! Bring her over for dinner afterwards, then,” he says, his hands gently on my lower back.

I pour the wine and we go outside. We sit next to each other on the edge of the patio, our feet in the grass.

“Is she married?” he asks me.

"Tara? No. I'm single."

"No boyfriend?"

"I don't think so."

"Girlfriend?" he asks, raising an eyebrow, and I laugh. "How old is she?"

"I don't know," I say. "About forty."

"Oh. And she's all alone. That's kind of sad."

"Yeah. I think she might be lonely."

"Lonely people always like you, don't they? They go straight for you."

"Do they?"

"She doesn't have kids, then?" he asks. I might be imagining it, but as soon as he mentions kids, his voice sounds sharp. I can feel an argument coming, and I don't want it. I can't deal with it. So, I stand up and tell him to bring the wine glasses. We're going to the bedroom.

He follows me, and I start taking off my clothes as I walk up the stairs. When we get to the bedroom, he pushes me onto the bed. I'm not even thinking about him at that moment, but he doesn't know that. I'm good at making him think that I care about him.

RACHEL

• • •

MONDAY, JULY 15, 2013

MORNING

Cathy called me back as I was leaving my apartment this morning and gave me a quick hug. I thought she would say she wasn't making me move out, but instead she gave me a typed note. It was an official notice that I had to leave, with a date. She wouldn't look at me. I felt bad for her, but also for myself. She smiled sadly and said, "I really don't want to do this, Rachel." It was very awkward. We were in the hallway, which still smelled a little like sick, even though I had cleaned it. I wanted to cry, but I didn't want her to feel worse, so I smiled and said, "It's okay, no problem," like she had just asked me for a small favor.

On the train, I started crying. I didn't care if people saw me. They could think my dog died, or I had a bad illness. They could think I was a sad, divorced, soon-to-be-homeless alcoholic.

It's silly when I think about it. How did I get here? I wonder when things started going wrong. When could I have stopped it? Where did I make a mistake? It wasn't when I met Tom, who helped me after my dad died. It wasn't when we got married, happy and carefree, on a cold day in May seven years ago. I was happy, had money, and was doing well. It wasn't when we moved to our bigger, nicer house. I remember those first days clearly, walking around without shoes, feeling the warm wood floors, enjoying the space. Tom and I made plans: what to plant in the garden, what to hang on the walls, what color to paint the extra room—which I already thought of as the baby's room.

Maybe that was it. Maybe that's when things started to go wrong. When I imagined us as a family, not just a couple. After that, just the two of us was never enough. Was that when Tom started to look at me differently, disappointed like I was? He gave up a lot for us to be together, and I made him feel like he wasn't enough.

I cried until we got to Northcote. Then, I pulled myself together, wiped my eyes, and started writing a list of things to do today on the back of Cathy's eviction letter:

Holborn Library

Email Mum.

Email Martin, what is the reference?

Find out about AA meetings in central London or Ashbury.

Tell Cathy about the job.

When the train stops, I look up and see Jason on the terrace, looking at the track. I feel like he's looking right at me, and it's strange—I feel like he's looked at me like that before; I feel like he's really seen me. I imagine him smiling, and I feel afraid.

He turns away and the train moves.

EVENING

I'm in the emergency room at University College Hospital. A taxi hit me when I was crossing Gray's Inn Road. I was not drunk, but I was upset and distracted. A very handsome young doctor is stitching a small cut above my right eye. He is quick and professional. When he's done, he sees the bump on my head.

"It's not new," I say.

"It looks new," he says.

"Not new today."

"Looks like you've been hurt."

"I hit it getting into a car."

He looks at my head for a few seconds and then says, "Really?" He steps back and looks at me. "It doesn't look like that. It looks like someone hit you," he says, and I feel scared. I remember ducking to avoid a hit, raising my hands. Is that a real memory? The doctor comes closer and looks at the cut. "Something sharp, maybe with edges..."

"No," I say. "It was a car. I hit it getting into a car." I'm trying to believe it myself.

"Okay." He smiles and steps back, bending down a little so we're eye to eye. "Are you okay..." He looks at his notes. "Rachel?"

"Yes."

He looks at me for a long time; he doesn't believe me. He's worried. Maybe he thinks I'm being abused. "Okay. I'm going to clean this up, because it looks bad. Is there someone I can call? Your husband?"

"I'm divorced," I say.

"Someone else, then?" He doesn't care that I'm divorced.

"Please, my friend will worry about me." I tell him Cathy's name and number. Cathy won't worry at all—I'm not even late yet—but I hope that hearing I was hit by a taxi will make her feel sorry for me and forgive me for yesterday. She'll probably think I was drunk. I wonder if I can ask the doctor for a blood test to prove I was sober. I smile at him, but he's writing notes, not looking at me. It's a silly idea anyway.

It was my fault, not the taxi driver's. I stepped—actually, ran—right in front of the taxi. I don't know why I was running. I wasn't thinking about myself. I was thinking about Jess. Who isn't Jess, she's Megan Hipwell, and she's missing.

I was at the library on Theobalds Road. I just emailed my mother (I didn't say anything important, just testing how she feels about me) using my Yahoo account. Yahoo's homepage has news stories based on your location. I don't know how they know my location, but they do. There was a picture of her, Jess, my Jess, the blonde, next to a headline that said "CONCERN FOR MISSING WITNEY WOMAN."

At first, I wasn't sure. It looked like her, exactly how she looks in my mind, but I doubted myself. Then I read the story and saw the street name, and I knew.

Buckinghamshire Police are worried about Megan Hipwell, 29, of Blenheim Road, Witney. Her husband, Scott Hipwell, last saw her on Saturday night when she left home to visit a friend around seven o'clock. Her disappearance is "very unusual," Mr. Hipwell said. She was wearing jeans and a red T-shirt. She is five foot four, thin, with blonde hair and blue eyes. Anyone with information should contact Buckinghamshire Police.

She's missing. Jess is missing. Megan is missing. Since Saturday. I Googled her—the story was in the Witney Argus, but with no more details. I thought about seeing Jason—Scott—this morning, on the terrace, looking and smiling at me. I grabbed my bag, stood up, and ran out of the library, into the road, and into a taxi.

"Rachel? Rachel?" The doctor is trying to get my attention. "Your friend is here to pick you up."

MEGAN

• • •

THURSDAY, JANUARY 10, 2013

MORNING

Sometimes, I don't want to go anywhere. I think I'd be happy if I never left the house again. I don't even miss working. I just want to stay safe and warm in my home with Scott, with no one bothering us.

It helps that it's dark and cold and the weather is awful. It helps that it's been raining for weeks—freezing, hard, and nasty rain with strong winds blowing through the trees. The wind is so loud it covers the sound of the train. I can't hear it on the tracks, calling to me, tempting me to go somewhere else.

Today, I don't want to go anywhere. I don't want to run away. I don't even want to go down the street. I want to stay here, cozy with my husband, watching TV and eating ice cream. I want to call him to come home from work early so we can have sex in the afternoon.

I will have to go out later, because it's my day to see Kamal. I've been talking to him about Scott, about all the things I've done wrong, how I've failed as a wife. Kamal says I need to find a way to make myself happy. I need to stop looking for happiness in other places. It's true, I know I do. But then I just think, forget it, life's too short.

I think about the time we went on a family trip to Santa Margherita during the Easter school break. I had just turned fifteen and I met a guy on the beach. He was much older than me—maybe in his thirties or early forties. He asked me to go sailing the next day. Ben was with me and he was invited too. But Ben, being the protective older brother, said we shouldn't go because he didn't trust the guy. He thought he was a creepy person. Which, of course, he was. But I was angry. When would we ever get another chance to sail around the Ligurian Sea on someone's private boat? Ben told me we'd have many chances like that, that our lives would be full of adventure.
