In the end, we didn't go. That summer, Ben lost control of his motorcycle on the A10, and we never got to go sailing.

I miss how we were when we were together, Ben and I. We were fearless.

I've told Kamal about Ben, but now we're getting to the real stuff, the complete truth—what happened with Mac, before and after. It's safe with Kamal, he can't tell anyone because of patient privacy.

But even if he could, I don't think he would. I trust him a lot. It's strange, but the reason I haven't told him everything isn't because I'm afraid of what he'd do or think. It's because of Scott. It feels like I'm betraying Scott if I tell Kamal something I can't tell him. Considering all the other bad things I've done, this should be easy, but it's not. Somehow this feels worse, because this is real life, this is my true self, and I don't share it with him.

I'm still holding back, because I can't say everything I feel. I know that's the point of therapy, but I just can't. I have to keep things unclear, mix up all the men, the lovers and exes, but I tell myself it's okay, because who they are doesn't matter. It's how they make me feel that matters. Trapped, restless, wanting more. Why can't I just have what I want? Why can't they give it to me?

Well, sometimes they do. Sometimes Scott is all I need. If I can just learn to hold on to this feeling, the one I have now—if I could just learn to focus on this happiness, enjoy the moment, and not worry about the next thrill—then everything will be okay.

EVENING

I have to focus when I'm with Kamal. It's hard not to let my mind wander when he looks at me with those intense eyes, when he puts his hands together on his lap, with his long legs crossed. It's hard not to think about what we could do together.

I have to focus. We've been talking about what happened after Ben's funeral, after I left. I was in Ipswich for a while; not long. I met Mac there, the first time. He was working at a bar or something. He gave me a ride home. He felt sorry for me.

"He didn't even want... you know." I start laughing. "We got to his place and I asked for money, and he looked at me like I was crazy. I told him I was old enough, but he didn't believe me. And he waited, he really did, until my sixteenth birthday. He had moved by then, to an old house near Holkham. An old stone house at the end of a road that didn't go anywhere, with some land around it, about half a mile from the beach. There was an old train track next to the property. At night I'd lie awake—I was always excited then, we were smoking a lot—and I used to think I could hear the trains, I was so sure, I'd get up and go outside and look for the lights."

Kamal moves in his chair, he nods slowly. He doesn't say anything. This means I should keep talking.

"I was actually really happy there, with Mac. I lived with him for... wow, it was about three years, I think, in the end. I was... nineteen when I left. Yeah. Nineteen."

"If you were happy there, why did you leave?" he asks. We're there now, and we got here faster than I thought. I haven't had time to think about it all yet. I can't do it. It's too soon.

"Mac left me. He broke my heart," I say. It's true, but it's also not the whole story. I'm not ready to tell everything yet.

Scott isn't home when I get back, so I get my laptop and search for Mac online for the first time. It's been ten years since I last looked for him. I can't find him. There are many Craig McKenzies, but none of them seem to be him.

FRIDAY, FEBRUARY 8, 2013

MORNING

I'm walking in the woods. I've been out since before sunrise. It's barely dawn now, very quiet except for the magpies in the trees. I feel them watching me, with their sharp eyes. A group of magpies. One for sadness, two for joy, three for a girl, four for a boy, five for silver, six for gold, seven for a secret never to be told.

I have a few of those secrets.

Scott is away on a course in Sussex. He left yesterday morning and won't be back until tonight. I can do anything I want.

Before he left, I told Scott I was going to the movies with Tara after my therapy. I told him my phone would be off, and I told her too. I warned her that he might call, that he might check on me. This time, she asked what I was really doing. I just winked and smiled, and she laughed. I think she might be lonely and want some excitement in her life.

In my therapy session with Kamal, we talked about Scott and the laptop. It happened about a week ago. I had been searching for Mac. I just wanted to know where he was and what he was doing. Most people have pictures online these days, and I wanted to see his face. I couldn't find him. I went to bed early that night. Scott stayed up watching TV, and I forgot to delete my search history. It was a stupid mistake. I usually do that before I turn off my computer, no matter what I've been looking at. I know Scott can find out what I've been doing anyway, because he's good with technology, but it takes him longer, so he usually doesn't bother.

Anyway, I forgot. The next day, we had a bad fight. He wanted to know who Craig was, how long I had known him, where we met, and what Craig did for me that Scott didn't. I made a mistake and told Scott that Craig was an old friend, which made things worse. Kamal asked if I was scared of Scott, and I got very angry.

“He’s my husband,” I said sharply. “Of course I’m not scared of him.”

Kamal looked surprised. I was surprised too. I didn't expect to feel so angry and protective of Scott.

“Many women are scared of their husbands, Megan.” He stopped me from speaking. “The things you're describing—him reading your emails and looking at your internet history—you talk about them like they're normal. They're not. It's not normal to invade someone's privacy like that. It's often seen as emotional abuse.”

I laughed because it sounded like too much drama. “It's not abuse,” I said. “Not if I don't mind, and I don't. I really don't mind.”

He smiled sadly. “Don't you think you should mind?” he asked.

I shrugged. “Maybe I should, but I don't. He's jealous and possessive. That's just how he is. It doesn't stop me from loving him, and some fights aren't worth having. I'm usually careful. I hide what I do, so it's usually not a problem.”

He shook his head slightly.

“I didn't think you were here to judge me,” I said.

When the session was over, I asked if he wanted to get a drink. He said no, it wasn't right. So I followed him home. He lives in an apartment near his office. I knocked on his door, and when he opened it, I asked, “Is this right?” I put my hand on his neck, stood on my toes, and kissed him.

"Megan," he said, his voice soft. "Please don't. I can't do this. Don't."

It was a wonderful feeling, that back and forth, wanting and holding back. I didn't want it to end. I wanted to keep feeling it.

I woke up very early, my head full of thoughts. I couldn't just lie there, awake and alone, thinking about all the choices I could make. So, I got up, got dressed, and started walking. I ended up here. I've been walking and thinking about everything—what he said, what she said, wanting something, letting it go. If only I could decide on something, choose to stay with it, not change my mind. What if what I'm looking for can't be found? What if it's just not possible?

The air is cold in my lungs, and my fingers are turning blue. Part of me wants to lie down here, among the leaves, and let the cold take over. But I can't. It's time to leave.

It's almost nine when I get back to Blenheim Road. As I turn the corner, I see her walking towards me, pushing the stroller. The child is quiet for once. She looks at me, nods, and gives me a weak smile, which I don't return. Usually, I would pretend to be nice, but this morning I feel real, like myself. I feel excited, almost like I'm on drugs, and I couldn't pretend to be nice even if I tried.

AFTERNOON

I fell asleep in the afternoon. I woke up feeling hot, worried, and guilty. I do feel guilty, but not enough.

I thought about him leaving in the middle of the night, telling me again that this was the last time, the very last time, we can't do this again. He was getting dressed, putting on his jeans. I was lying on the bed and I laughed because he said that last time, and the time before, and the time before that. He looked at me. I don't know how to describe it. It wasn't exactly anger or dislike—it was a warning.

I feel uneasy. I walk around the house. I can't relax. I feel like someone else was here while I was sleeping. Nothing is out of place, but the house feels different, like things have been touched and moved slightly. As I walk around, I feel like someone else is here, always just out of sight. I check the doors to the garden three times, but they're locked. I can't wait for Scott to come home. I need him.

RACHEL

• • •

TUESDAY, JULY 16, 2013

MORNING

I'm on the 8:04 train, but I'm not going to London. I'm going to Witney instead. I hope that being there will help me remember things. I hope I'll get to the station and see everything clearly, and I'll know what happened. I don't really think it will work, but I don't know what else to do. I can't call Tom. I'm too embarrassed, and he doesn't want to talk to me anyway.

Megan is still missing. She's been gone for more than sixty hours, and it's now a big news story. It was on the BBC website and the Daily Mail this morning. Some other websites mentioned it too.

I printed out the BBC and Daily Mail stories. I have them with me. From them, I learned these things:

Megan and Scott argued on Saturday night. A neighbor heard them yelling. Scott said they argued and that he thought his wife went to stay with a friend, Tara Epstein, who lives in Corly.

Megan never got to Tara's house. Tara says she last saw Megan on Friday afternoon at their Pilates class. (I knew Megan would do Pilates.) According to Ms. Epstein, "She seemed okay, normal. She was happy and talking about doing something special for her thirtieth birthday next month."

Someone saw Megan walking towards Witney train station around 7:15 on Saturday night.

Megan doesn't have any family nearby. Her parents have both died.

Megan is not working. She used to own a small art gallery in Witney, but it closed in April last year. (I knew Megan was artistic.)

Scott works for himself as an IT consultant. (I can't believe Scott is an IT consultant.)

Megan and Scott have been married for three years. They have lived in the house on Blenheim Road since January 2012.

The Daily Mail says their house is worth £400,000.

Reading this, I think things look bad for Scott. It's not just because of the argument. It's because when something bad happens to a woman, the police usually look at the husband or boyfriend first. But in this case, the police don't know everything. They are only looking at the husband, probably because they don't know about the boyfriend.

I might be the only person who knows about the boyfriend.

I look in my bag for a piece of paper. On the back of a card for two bottles of wine, I write down the most likely reasons why Megan Hipwell has disappeared:

  1. She ran away with her boyfriend, who I will call B from now on.
  2. B hurt her.
  3. Scott hurt her.
  4. She left her husband and went to live somewhere else.
  5. Someone else hurt her.

I think the first idea is most likely. The fourth idea is also possible because Megan is a strong-willed woman, I'm sure of it. If she was having an affair, she might need to get away to think, right? The fifth idea doesn't seem very likely, since murder by a stranger is not common.

My head is throbbing, and I can't stop thinking about the argument I saw, or thought I saw, or dreamed about, on Saturday night. As we drive past Megan and Scott's house, I look up. I can hear my heart beating in my head. I feel excited and afraid. The windows of number fifteen, reflecting the morning sun, look like empty eyes.

EVENING

I'm just sitting down when my phone rings. It's Cathy. I let it go to voicemail.

She leaves a message: "Hi, Rachel, just calling to see if you're okay." She's worried about me because of the taxi thing. "I just wanted to say I'm sorry about what I said about you moving out. I shouldn't have. I overreacted. You can stay as long as you want." There's a pause, and then she says, "Call me back, okay? And come straight home, Rach, don't go to the bar."

I don't plan to. I wanted a drink at lunch; I really wanted one after what happened in Witney this morning. But I didn't have one because I needed to think clearly. It's been a long time since I've needed to think clearly.

It was so strange, my trip to Witney this morning. It felt like I hadn't been there in a long time, even though it's only been a few days. It felt like a completely different place, a different station in a different town. I was a different person than the one who went there on Saturday night. Today I was stiff and sober, very aware of the noise, the light, and the fear of being seen.

I felt like I was trespassing. That's what it felt like this morning, because it's their place now, it's Tom and Anna's and Scott and Megan's. I'm the outsider, I don't belong there, but everything is so familiar to me. Down the concrete steps at the station, past the newspaper stand into Roseberry Avenue, half a block to the end of the T-junction, to the right the archway to a dark underpass under the train tracks, and to the left Blenheim Road, narrow and tree-lined, with its nice old houses. It feels like coming home—not just any home, but a childhood home, a place from a long time ago. It's like walking up stairs and knowing which one will creak.

The familiarity isn't just in my head, it's in my body; it's muscle memory. This morning, as I walked past the dark tunnel entrance, the entrance to the underpass, I walked faster. I didn't have to think about it because I always walked a little faster there. Every night, coming home, especially in winter, I used to walk faster, quickly looking to the right, just to make sure. There was never anyone there—not on any of those nights and not today—but I stopped as I looked into the darkness this morning, because I could suddenly see myself. I could see myself a few feet in, leaning against the wall, my head in my hands, and both my head and hands covered in blood.

My heart pounding, I stood there. People going to the station walked around me, some looking at me as they passed, as I stood still. I didn't know—don't know—if it was real. Why would I have gone into the underpass? Why would I have gone down there, where it's dark and wet and smells bad?

I turned around and went back to the station. I didn't want to be there anymore; I didn't want to go to Scott and Megan's door. I wanted to leave. Something bad happened there, I know it did.

I bought my ticket and quickly walked up the station steps to the other side of the platform. As I did, it came back to me again: not the underpass this time, but the steps. I remembered stumbling on the steps and a man taking my arm, helping me up. The man from the train, with the reddish hair. I could see him, a blurry picture but no words. I remembered laughing—at myself, or at something he said. He was nice to me, I'm sure of it. Almost sure. Something bad happened, but I don't think it was because of him.

I got on the train and went to London. I went to the library and used a computer to look for news about Megan. There was a short article on the Telegraph website that said "a man in his thirties is helping police with their questions." That must be Scott. I can't believe he would have hurt her. I know he wouldn't. I've seen them together; I know how they are together. They also gave a phone number for people to call if they have information. I'm going to call it on the way home, from a pay phone. I'm going to tell them about B, about what I saw.

My phone rings just as we're arriving in Ashbury. It's Cathy again. She's really worried about me.

"Rach? Are you on the train? Are you coming home?" She sounds worried.

"Yes, I'm on my way," I tell her. "I'll be there in fifteen minutes."

"The police are here, Rachel," she says, and I feel very cold. "They want to talk to you."

WEDNESDAY, JULY 17, 2013

MORNING

Megan is still missing, and I have lied—many times—to the police.

I was panicking when I got back to the apartment last night. I tried to tell myself they were there about my taxi accident, but that didn't make sense. I'd talked to the police at the scene, and it was clearly my fault. It had to be about Saturday night. I must have done something. I must have done something terrible and forgotten it.

I know it sounds unlikely. What could I have done? Gone to Blenheim Road, attacked Megan Hipwell, hidden her body, and then forgotten it all? It sounds crazy. It is crazy. But I know something happened on Saturday. I knew it when I looked into that dark tunnel under the train tracks, and my blood ran cold.

Blackouts happen, and it's not just being a little fuzzy about getting home from a club or forgetting a joke from the pub. It's different. It's a total blank; hours are lost, never to be remembered.

Tom bought me a book about it. Not very romantic, but he was tired of me saying sorry in the morning when I didn't even know what I was sorry for. I think he wanted me to see the harm I was doing, the things I might be capable of. It was written by a doctor, but I don't know if it was correct. The author said that blacking out wasn't just forgetting what happened, but having no memories at all. He thought that you get into a state where your brain stops making new memories. And while you're in that blank state, you don't act like yourself because you're just reacting to the last thing you think happened. Since you're not making memories, you might not know what really happened. He had stories, too, warnings for people who black out: There was a guy in New Jersey who got drunk at a party. Afterward, he drove the wrong way on the highway and crashed into a van with seven people. The van caught fire, and six people died. The drunk guy was fine. They always are. He didn't remember getting into his car.

There was another man, in New York, who left a bar, drove to his childhood home, killed the people inside, took off his clothes, got back in his car, drove home, and went to bed. He woke up feeling awful, wondering where his clothes were and how he got home. But it wasn't until the police came that he found out he had killed two people for no reason.

So it sounds crazy, but it's not impossible. By the time I got home last night, I was sure I was somehow involved in Megan's disappearance.

The police officers were sitting on the sofa in the living room. One was a man in his forties in plain clothes, and the other was a younger one in uniform with acne on his neck. Cathy was standing by the window, wringing her hands. She looked terrified. The policemen stood up. The plainclothes one, very tall and a little hunched over, shook my hand and said he was Detective Inspector Gaskill. He told me the other officer's name, but I don't remember it. I wasn't paying attention. I was barely breathing.

"What's this about?" I asked them sharply. "Has something happened? Is it my mother? Is it Tom?"

"Everyone's okay, Ms. Watson. We just need to talk to you about what you did on Saturday evening," Gaskill said. It's the kind of thing they say on TV; it didn't seem real. They want to know what I did on Saturday evening. What the heck did I do on Saturday evening?

"I need to sit down," I said, and the detective told me to sit on the sofa next to Neck Acne. Cathy was shifting her weight, biting her lip. She looked panicked.

"Are you okay, Ms. Watson?" Gaskill asked me. He pointed to the cut above my eye.

"A taxi hit me," I said. "It happened yesterday afternoon in London. I went to the hospital. You can check if you want."

"Okay," he said, shaking his head a little. "So, Saturday evening?"

"I went to Witney," I said, trying not to sound shaky.

"Why?"

Neck Acne had a notebook out and was ready to write.

"I wanted to see my husband," I said.

"Oh, Rachel," Cathy said.

The detective didn't pay attention to her. "Your husband?" he said. "You mean your ex-husband? Tom Watson?" Yes, I still use his name. It was just easier. I didn't have to change my credit cards, email, get a new passport, and things like that.

"That's right. I wanted to see him, but then I thought it wasn't a good idea, so I came home."

"What time was this?" Gaskill's voice was calm, his face showed no emotion. His lips barely moved when he spoke. I could hear Neck Acne's pencil scratching on the paper, and I could hear my heart pounding in my ears.

"It was... um... I think it was around six thirty. I mean, I think I got on the train around six o'clock."

"And you came home...?"

"Maybe seven thirty?" I looked up and saw Cathy's face. I could tell from her look that she knew I was lying. "Maybe a little later than that. Maybe it was closer to eight. Yes, actually, I remember now—I think I got home just after eight." I could feel my face getting red. If this man didn't know I was lying, he shouldn't be a police officer.

The detective turned, grabbed a chair from under the table in the corner, and pulled it towards him quickly, almost roughly. He put it right in front of me, a few feet away. He sat down, his hands on his knees, head tilted to one side. "Okay," he said. "So you left around six, which means you were in Witney by six thirty. And you were back here around eight, which means you must have left Witney around seven thirty. Does that sound right?"

"Yes, that seems right," I said. My voice was shaky again, giving me away. In a moment, he was going to ask me what I had been doing for an hour, and I had no answer.

"And you didn't actually go see your ex-husband. So what did you do during that hour in Witney?"

"I walked around for a bit."

He waited to see if I would say more. I thought about saying I went to a pub, but that would be dumb—that could be checked. He would ask me which pub, and if I talked to anyone. As I was thinking about what to say, I realized that I hadn't asked him why he wanted to know where I was on Saturday evening. That must have seemed strange. That must have made me look guilty.

"Did you talk to anyone?" he asked, as if reading my mind. "Go into any shops, bars...?"

"I talked to a man at the station!" I said loudly, almost like I was proud of it, as if it was important. "Why do you need to know this? What's happening?"

Detective Inspector Gaskill leaned back in his chair. "You might know that a woman from Witney is missing. She lives on Blenheim Road, near your ex-husband's house. We're asking people if they saw her that night, or if they heard anything strange. Your name came up during our questions." He paused to let me think about this. "You were seen on Blenheim Road that evening, around the time Mrs. Hipwell, the missing woman, left her house. Mrs. Anna Watson told us she saw you in the street, close to Mrs. Hipwell's home. She said you were acting strangely and that she was worried. She even thought about calling the police."

My heart was beating fast. I couldn't talk because I was picturing myself in the underpass, with blood on my hands. Blood on my hands. It had to be mine. I looked at Gaskill, saw him looking at me, and knew I had to say something to stop him from knowing what I was thinking. "I didn't do anything," I said. "I didn't. I just... I just wanted to see my husband..."

"Your ex-husband," Gaskill corrected me again. He took a photo from his pocket and showed it to me. It was a picture of Megan. "Did you see this woman on Saturday night?" he asked. I looked at it for a long time. It felt strange to see her like that, the perfect blonde I had watched and imagined. It was a close-up photo, a professional one. Her face was a bit fuller than I had thought, not as delicate as the Jess in my mind. "Ms. Watson? Did you see her?"

I didn't know if I had seen her. I really didn't know. I still don't.

"I don't think so," I said.

"You don't think so? So you might have seen her?"

"I... I'm not sure."

"Had you been drinking on Saturday evening?" he asked. "Before you went to Witney, had you been drinking?"

My face felt hot again. "Yes," I said.

"Mrs. Watson—Anna Watson—said she thought you were drunk when she saw you outside her house. Were you drunk?"

"No," I said, looking right at the detective so I wouldn't look at Cathy. "I had a couple of drinks in the afternoon, but I wasn't drunk."

Gaskill sighed. He seemed disappointed in me. He looked at Neck Acne, then back at me. Slowly, he stood up and pushed his chair back under the table. "If you remember anything about Saturday night that could help us, please call me," he said, giving me a business card.

As Gaskill nodded sadly at Cathy, getting ready to leave, I fell back into the sofa. I felt my heart slow down, then speed up again when he asked, "You work in public relations, right? At Huntingdon Whitely?"

"That's right," I said. "Huntingdon Whitely."

He's going to check, and he's going to know I lied. I can't let him find out on his own, I have to tell him.

So that's what I'm going to do this morning. I'm going to go to the police station to tell the truth. I'm going to tell him everything: that I lost my job months ago, that I was very drunk on Saturday night and I don't know what time I got home. I'm going to say what I should have said last night: that he's looking in the wrong place. I'm going to tell him that I think Megan Hipwell was having an affair.

EVENING

The police think I'm just curious. They think I'm a stalker, crazy, not stable. I should never have gone to the police station. I've made things worse for myself and I don't think I've helped Scott, which was why I went there in the first place. He needs my help, because the police will probably think he did something to her, and I know that's not true, because I know him. I really feel that, even if it sounds crazy. I've seen how he is with her. He couldn't hurt her.

OK, so helping Scott wasn't the only reason I went to the police. There was also the lie, which I needed to fix. The lie about working for Huntingdon Whitely.

It took me a long time to get the courage to go into the station. I almost turned around and went home many times, but I finally went in. I asked the desk sergeant if I could talk to Detective Inspector Gaskill. He led me to a stuffy waiting room. I sat there for over an hour until someone came to get me. By then, I was sweating and shaking like someone about to be punished. I was taken to another room, smaller and even stuffier, with no windows or air. I was left alone for another ten minutes before Gaskill and a woman, also in plain clothes, came in. Gaskill greeted me politely; he didn't seem surprised to see me. He introduced his partner as Detective Sergeant Riley. She was younger than me, tall, thin, dark-haired, and pretty in a sharp, fox-like way. She didn't smile back at me.

We all sat down, and no one spoke. They just looked at me, waiting.

"I remembered the man," I said. "I told you there was a man at the station. I can describe him." Riley raised her eyebrows slightly and moved in her seat. "He was about average height and build, with reddish hair. I slipped on the steps, and he caught my arm." Gaskill leaned forward, his elbows on the table, with his hands together in front of his mouth. "He was wearing... I think he was wearing a blue shirt."

This wasn't really true. I do remember a man, and I'm pretty sure he had reddish hair. I think he smiled at me, or smirked at me, when I was on the train. I think he got off at Witney, and I think he might have spoken to me. It's possible I might have slipped on the steps. I have a memory of it, but I can't tell if it was from Saturday night or another time. I've slipped many times on many stairs. I have no idea what he was wearing.

The detectives didn't seem impressed with my story. Riley shook her head almost without moving. Gaskill opened his hands and showed his palms, facing up. "Okay. Is that really why you came here, Ms. Watson?" he asked. He didn't sound angry, almost encouraging. I wished Riley would leave. I could talk to him; I could trust him.

"I don't work for Huntingdon Whitely anymore," I said.

"Oh." He leaned back, looking more interested.

"I left three months ago. My flatmate—well, she's my landlady, really—I haven't told her. I'm trying to find another job. I didn't want her to know because I thought she would worry about the rent. I have some money. I can pay my rent, but... Anyway, I lied to you yesterday about my job, and I apologize for that."

Riley leaned forward and gave me a fake smile. "I see. You no longer work for Huntingdon Whitely. You don't work for anyone, is that right? You're unemployed?" I nodded. "Okay. So... you're not signed up to get unemployment benefits, or anything like that?"

"No."

"And... your roommate, she hasn't noticed that you don't go to work every day?"

"I do. I mean, I don't go to the office, but I go into London, like I used to, at the same time and everything, so that she... so that she won't know." Riley looked at Gaskill; he kept looking at me, with a small frown. "It sounds strange, I know..." I said, and then stopped talking, because it doesn't just sound strange, it sounds crazy when you say it out loud.

"Okay. So, you pretend to go to work every day?" Riley asked me, her eyebrows also pulled together, like she was worried about me. Like she thought I was completely crazy. I didn't speak or nod or do anything, I stayed quiet. "Can I ask why you left your job, Ms. Watson?"

There was no reason to lie. If they hadn't planned to check my work history before this talk, they would now. "I was fired," I said.

"You were dismissed," Riley said, sounding pleased. It was clearly the answer she expected. "Why was that?"

I sighed a little and looked at Gaskill. "Is this really important? Does it matter why I left my job?"

Gaskill didn't say anything, he was looking at some notes that Riley had put in front of him, but he did shake his head a little. Riley changed the subject.

"Ms. Watson, I wanted to ask you about Saturday night."

I looked at Gaskill—we've already talked about this—but he wasn't looking at me. "Okay," I said. I kept touching my head, worrying about my injury. I couldn't stop myself.

"Tell me why you went to Blenheim Road on Saturday night. Why did you want to talk to your ex-husband?"

"That's none of your concern," I said, then quickly added, "Could I have a glass of water?" before she could reply.

Gaskill stood up and left, which wasn't what I wanted. Riley didn't speak; she just kept looking at me with a small smile. I couldn't look at her, so I looked at the table and then around the room. I knew she was doing this on purpose: she was being quiet so I would feel awkward and have to talk, even if I didn't want to. "I needed to talk to him about some things," I said. "Private things." I sounded silly and full of myself.

Riley sighed. I bit my lip, deciding not to talk until Gaskill came back. As soon as he returned with a glass of murky water, Riley spoke.

"Private things?" she asked.

"Yes."

Riley and Gaskill looked at each other, and I wasn't sure if they were annoyed or amused. I could feel sweat on my lip. I drank some water; it tasted old. Gaskill moved the papers in front of him, then pushed them away, like he was done with them or didn't care about them.

"Ms. Watson, your ex-husband's current wife, Mrs. Anna Watson, is worried about you. She said you've been bothering her and her husband, going to their house without being invited, and that once..." Gaskill looked at his notes, but Riley cut him off.

"Once you broke into Mr. and Mrs. Watson's house and took their baby, their newborn child."

I felt like I was falling into a dark hole. "That's not true!" I said. "I didn't take... It didn't happen like that, that's wrong. I didn't... I didn't take her."

I got very upset, started shaking and crying, and said I wanted to leave. Riley pushed her chair back, stood up, shrugged at Gaskill, and left. Gaskill gave me a tissue.

"You can leave whenever you want, Ms. Watson. You came here to talk to us." He smiled at me, a kind of sorry smile. I liked him then. I wanted to hold his hand, but I didn't because that would have been strange. "I think you have more to tell me," he said. I liked him even more for saying "tell me" instead of "tell us."

"Maybe," he said, standing up and leading me to the door. "You might want to take a break, walk around, get something to eat. Then, when you're ready, come back, and you can tell me everything."

I was going to forget everything and go home. I was walking back to the train station, ready to give up. Then I thought about the train ride, going back and forth past the house—Megan and Scott's house—every day. What if they never found her? I would always wonder—even though it's not likely—if saying something might have helped her. What if Scott was blamed just because they didn't know about B? What if she was at B's house right now, hurt or buried?

I did what Gaskill said. I bought a sandwich and a water from a small shop and went to the only park in Witney. It was a small, sad area with houses from the 1930s around it. Most of it was a playground. I sat on a bench, watching parents tell their kids not to eat sand. I used to dream of this, a few years ago. I dreamed of coming here—not to eat a sandwich between police talks, of course. I dreamed of coming here with my own baby. I thought about the stroller I would buy, all the time I would spend looking at baby clothes and toys. I thought about sitting here with my baby.

It didn't happen. Doctors can't explain why I can't get pregnant. I'm young and healthy. I wasn't drinking a lot when we were trying. My husband's sperm was healthy. It just didn't happen. I didn't have a miscarriage, I just didn't get pregnant. We tried IVF once, which was all we could afford. It was hard and didn't work, like everyone said it would be. Nobody told me it would break us. But it did. It broke me, and then I broke us.

The problem with not being able to have kids is that you can't escape it. Not when you're in your thirties. My friends were having kids, friends of friends were having kids. There were babies and birthday parties everywhere. People asked me about it all the time. My mother, our friends, people at work. When was it going to be my turn? Our childlessness became a normal topic at lunch, not just between Tom and me, but with everyone. What we were trying, what we should be doing, should you really have another glass of wine? I was still young, but I felt like a failure. It overwhelmed me, and I gave up hope. I was angry that it was always seen as my fault. But Tom got Anna pregnant quickly, so it was never his problem. I was wrong to think we shared the blame; it was all me.

Lara, my best friend from college, had two kids in two years. I didn't like them. I didn't want to hear about them. I didn't want to be near them. Lara stopped talking to me after a while. A girl at work told me she had an abortion, like it was nothing. I couldn't talk to her after that. Things got awkward at work; people noticed.

Tom didn't feel the way I did. It wasn't his fault, and he didn't need a child like I did. He wanted to be a dad, I'm sure he did. But he thought our lives could be good without kids too. "We're happy," he used to say. "Why can't we just stay happy?" He got annoyed with me. He didn't understand that you can miss something you've never had.

I felt alone in my sadness. I became lonely, so I drank a little, then more, and then I became even lonelier because no one likes being around a drunk. I lost and I drank and I drank and I lost. I liked my job, but I wasn't very successful. And even if I was, women are still mostly valued for their looks and being mothers. I'm not beautiful, and I can't have kids, so what does that make me? Worthless.

I can't blame my drinking on all of this. It's my fault. I liked to drink anyway. But I became sadder, and sadness gets boring after a while. And then I went from being a drinker to being a drunk, and that's even more boring.

I'm doing better now about the kids. I've improved since being on my own. I had to. I've read books and articles, and I've learned that I need to accept it. There are ways to cope, and there is hope. If I get myself together and stop drinking, I might be able to adopt. And I'm not thirty-four yet—it's not too late. I'm better than I was a few years ago, when I would leave my shopping cart and run out of the store if it was full of moms and kids. I wouldn't have been able to come to a park like this, to sit near the playground and watch little kids playing. There were times, when I felt the worst, when I thought I was going crazy.

Maybe I did, for a while. The day they asked me about at the police station, I might have been crazy then. Something Tom said made me snap. Something he wrote, actually: I saw it on Facebook that morning. It wasn't a surprise—I knew she was having a baby, he'd told me, and I'd seen her, seen the pink curtain in the baby's room. So I knew it was coming. But I thought of the baby as her baby. Until the day I saw the picture of him, holding his new baby girl, looking down at her and smiling. Underneath, he'd written: So this is what everyone talks about! I've never felt love like this! The happiest day of my life! I thought about him writing that—knowing that I would see it, that I would read those words and they would hurt me, and writing it anyway. He didn't care. Parents only care about their kids. They are the most important thing; they are all that matters. No one else is important, no one else's pain or happiness matters, none of it is real.

I was angry. I was upset. Maybe I wanted revenge. Maybe I thought I'd show them that my pain was real. I don't know. I did something stupid.

I went back to the police station a couple of hours later. I asked if I could talk to Gaskill alone, but he said that he wanted Riley to be there too. I liked him a little less after that.

"I didn't break into their house," I said. "I did go there, I wanted to talk to Tom. No one answered the door..."

"So how did you get in?" Riley asked me.

"The door was open."

"The front door was open?"

I sighed. "No, of course not. The sliding door in the back, the one that goes to the garden."

"And how did you get into the back garden?"

“I went over the fence, I knew how to get in—”

“So you climbed over the fence to get into your ex-husband’s house?”

“Yes. We used to... We always kept a spare key in the back. We had a hiding spot for it, in case one of us lost our keys or forgot them. But I wasn’t breaking in—I didn’t. I just wanted to talk to Tom. I thought maybe... the doorbell wasn’t working or something.”

“This was during the day, during the week, right? Why did you think your husband would be home? Did you call to check?” Riley asked.

“Jesus! Can you just let me talk?” I yelled, and she shook her head and smiled again, like she knew me, like she could read my mind. “I went over the fence,” I said, trying to speak calmly, “and knocked on the glass doors, which were partly open. No one answered. I looked inside and called Tom’s name. Again, no answer, but I heard a baby crying. I went inside and saw that Anna—”

“Mrs. Watson?”

“Yes. Mrs. Watson was sleeping on the couch. The baby was in the carrier and was crying—screaming, really, her face was red, she’d been crying for a while.” As I said that, I realized I should have said that I could hear the baby crying from the street, and that’s why I went to the back of the house. That would have made me seem less crazy.

“So the baby’s screaming and her mother’s right there, and she doesn’t wake up?” Riley asks me.

“Yes.” Her elbows are on the table, her hands are in front of her mouth so I can’t see her face, but I know she thinks I’m lying. “I picked her up to comfort her. That’s all. I picked her up to calm her down.”

“But that’s not all, is it? Because when Anna woke up, you weren’t there, were you? You were by the fence, near the train tracks.”

"She kept crying for a while," I said. "I was bouncing her, but she was still fussy, so I took her outside."

"Down to the train tracks?"

"Into the garden."

"Did you mean to hurt the Watsons' child?"

I jumped up. It was dramatic, I know, but I wanted them to see—to make Gaskill see—how crazy that idea was. "I don't have to listen to this! I came here to tell you about the man! I came here to help you! And now... what are you accusing me of? What are you accusing me of?"

Gaskill didn't react. He didn't seem impressed. He told me to sit down again. "Ms. Watson, the other Mrs. Watson—Anna—told us about you when we were asking about Megan Hipwell. She said you've acted strangely and not in a stable way before. She mentioned the thing with the child. She said you've bothered her and her husband, that you keep calling their house a lot." He looked at his notes. "Almost every night, actually. That you won't accept that your marriage is over—"

"That's not true!" I said. It wasn't—yes, I called Tom sometimes, but not every night. That was a big lie. But I was starting to think Gaskill wasn't on my side, and I was starting to cry again.

"Why haven't you changed your name?" Riley asked.

"What?"

"You still use your husband's name. Why? If a man left me for another woman, I'd want to get rid of that name. I wouldn't want to share my name with the new woman..."

"Okay, maybe I'm not that childish." I am that childish. I hate that she's Anna Watson.

"Right. And the ring—the one on a chain around your neck. Is that your wedding ring?"

"No," I lied. "It's a . . . it was my grandma's."

"Is that true? Okay. Well, I have to say that to me, your actions suggest that—like Mrs. Watson said—you don't want to move on. You won't accept that your ex has a new family."

"I don't understand—"

"What this has to do with Megan Hipwell?" Riley finished my sentence. "Well. The night Megan disappeared, we have reports that you—an unstable woman who had been drinking a lot—were seen on the street where she lives. Keep in mind that there are some physical similarities between Megan and Mrs. Watson—"

"They don't look alike at all!" I was angry at the idea. Jess is nothing like Anna. Megan is nothing like Anna.

"They're both blonde, thin, small, and pale-skinned . . ."

"So I attacked Megan Hipwell thinking she was Anna? That's the dumbest thing I've ever heard," I said. But that bump on my head was hurting again, and everything from Saturday night was still a complete blank.

"Did you know that Anna Watson knows Megan Hipwell?" Gaskill asked me, and I was shocked.

"I . . . what? No. No, they don't know each other."

Riley smiled briefly, then became serious. "Yes, they do. Megan watched the Watsons' kids . . ." She looked at her notes. "In August and September last year."

I don't know what to say. I can't believe it: Megan in my house, with her, with her baby.

"Did you get that cut on your lip when you fell the other day?" Gaskill asked.

"Yes. I think I bit it when I fell."

"Where did this accident happen?"

"It was in London, on Theobalds Road. Near Holborn."

"And what were you doing there?"

"I'm sorry?"

"Why were you in central London?"

I shrugged. “I already told you,” I said in a cold voice. “My roommate doesn't know I lost my job. So, I go to London like normal. I go to libraries to look for jobs and work on my resume.”

Riley shook her head, maybe in disbelief or wonder. How does someone get to that point?

I pushed my chair back, ready to leave. I was tired of being talked down to, of being made to look like a fool or crazy. It was time to play my best card. “I don’t know why we’re talking about this,” I said. “I thought you’d have better things to do, like looking into Megan Hipwell’s disappearance. Have you talked to her lover?” They didn't say anything, just stared at me. They weren't expecting that. They didn't know about him. “Maybe you didn’t know. Megan Hipwell was having an affair,” I said, and started walking to the door. Gaskill stopped me. He moved quietly and fast. Before I could touch the door handle, he was standing in front of me.

“I thought you didn’t know Megan Hipwell,” he said.

“I don’t,” I said, trying to get past him.

“Sit down,” he said, blocking my way.

I told them what I'd seen from the train. I often saw Megan sitting on her porch, sunbathing in the evenings or having coffee in the mornings. I told them that last week I saw her with a man who wasn't her husband. I saw them kissing on the grass.

“When was this?” Gaskill asked sharply. He seemed annoyed, maybe because I should have told them this right away, instead of wasting time talking about myself.

“Friday. It was Friday morning.”

“So, the day before she disappeared, you saw her with another man?” Riley asked, sighing in frustration. She closed the file in front of her. Gaskill leaned back, looking at my face. She clearly thought I was lying. He wasn't so sure.

"Can you describe him?" Gaskill asked.

"Tall, dark—"

"Handsome?" Riley interrupted.

I blew out my cheeks. "Taller than Scott Hipwell. I know because I saw them together—Jess and—sorry, Megan and Scott Hipwell—and this man was different. He was smaller, thinner, and had darker skin. He might have been Asian," I said.

"You could tell his race from the train?" Riley said. "That's amazing. Who is Jess, by the way?"

"I'm sorry?"

"You said Jess a minute ago."

I felt my face get hot again. I shook my head. "No, I didn't," I said.

Gaskill stood up and offered his hand to shake. "I think that's enough." I shook his hand, ignored Riley, and turned to leave. "Don't go near Blenheim Road, Ms. Watson," Gaskill said. "Don't contact your ex-husband unless you have to, and don't go near Anna Watson or her child."

On the train home, I thought about all the things that went wrong today. I was surprised that I didn't feel as bad as I thought I would. Then I realized why: I didn't drink last night, and I don't want to drink now. For the first time in a long time, I'm interested in something other than my own sadness. I have a goal. Or at least, I have something to keep me busy.

THURSDAY, JULY 18, 2013

MORNING

I bought three newspapers before getting on the train this morning. Megan has been missing for four days and five nights, and the story is all over the news. The Daily Mail, as expected, found pictures of Megan in a bikini. They also have the most detailed story about her life that I've seen.

Megan Mills was born in Rochester in 1983. When she was ten, she moved with her parents to King’s Lynn in Norfolk. She was a smart, friendly child who was good at art and singing. A friend from school said she was "fun, very pretty, and a bit wild." Her wild side got worse after her brother, Ben, died. They were very close. He died in a motorcycle accident when he was nineteen, and she was fifteen. She ran away from home three days after his funeral. She was arrested twice, once for stealing and once for offering sex for money. The Mail says her relationship with her parents completely fell apart. Both her parents died a few years ago without ever making up with their daughter. (Reading this, I feel very sad for Megan. I realize that maybe she's not so different from me. She's also alone and lonely.)

When she was sixteen, she moved in with a boyfriend who had a house near the village of Holkham in north Norfolk. The school friend said, "He was an older guy, a musician or something. He used drugs. We didn't see Megan much after they got together." The boyfriend's name isn't given, so they probably haven't found him. He might not even exist. The school friend might be making things up to get her name in the paper.

The story then jumps ahead several years. Suddenly, Megan is twenty-four, living in London, and working as a waitress in a restaurant in North London. There, she meets Scott Hipwell, an IT worker who is friends with the restaurant manager. They quickly become a couple. After a "fast romance," Megan and Scott get married when she is twenty-six and he is thirty.

There are a few other quotes, including one from Tara Epstein, the friend Megan was supposed to stay with the night she disappeared. She says that Megan is "a lovely, easygoing girl" and that she seemed "very happy." "Scott would not have hurt her," Tara says. "He loves her very much." Everything Tara says is a cliché. The quote that interests me is from Rajesh Gujral, an artist who showed his work in the gallery Megan used to manage. He says that Megan is "a wonderful woman, smart, funny, and beautiful, a very private person with a kind heart." It sounds like Rajesh has a crush on her. The only other quote is from a man named David Clark, "a former coworker" of Scott's. He says, "Megs and Scott are a great couple. They're very happy together, very much in love."

There are also some news stories about the investigation, but the police statements don't say much. They have talked to "a number of witnesses" and are "looking into several possibilities." The only interesting comment is from Detective Inspector Gaskill, who says that two men are helping the police with their investigation. I'm pretty sure that means they are both suspects. One will be Scott. Could the other be B? Could B be Rajesh?

I've been so focused on the newspapers that I haven't paid attention to the train ride. It feels like I just sat down when the train stops at the usual red light. There are people in Scott's garden. There are two police officers just outside the back door. My head is spinning. Have they found something? Have they found her? Is there a body buried in the garden or hidden under the floor? I can't stop thinking about the clothes by the train tracks, which is silly because I saw them there before Megan went missing. And anyway, if she was hurt, it wasn't by Scott. It can't have been. He's deeply in love with her, everyone says so. The light is bad today, the weather has changed, and the sky is gray and threatening. I can't see into the house, I can't see what's happening. I feel desperate. I can't stand being on the outside. I'm part of this now, for better or worse. I need to know what's going on.

At least I have a plan. First, I need to find out if there's any way to remember what happened on Saturday night. When I get to the library, I plan to research whether hypnotherapy could help me remember, and if it's possible to recover that lost time. Second, and I think this is important because I don't think the police believed me about Megan's lover, I need to contact Scott Hipwell. I need to tell him. He deserves to know.

EVENING

The train is crowded with people soaked from the rain. Steam rises from their clothes and fogs the windows. The heavy smell of sweat, perfume, and soap hangs in the air above their heads. The dark clouds from this morning grew worse all day. Then, this evening, they burst open like a monsoon. This happened just as people left work, causing traffic jams and crowded train stations with people struggling with umbrellas.

I don't have an umbrella, so I'm completely wet. It feels like someone dumped a bucket of water on me. My cotton pants stick to my legs, and my thin blue shirt is see-through. I ran from the library to the train station, holding my bag to my chest to hide as much as I could. For some reason, I found this funny. There's something silly about being caught in the rain. I was laughing so hard by the time I reached the top of Gray's Inn Road that I could barely breathe. I can't remember the last time I laughed like that.

I'm not laughing now. As soon as I sat down, I checked my phone for news about Megan's case. It's the news I was afraid of. "A thirty-four-year-old man is being questioned by police in Witney about the disappearance of Megan Hipwell, who has been missing since Saturday evening." That's Scott, I'm sure of it. I hope he read my email before they took him in. Being questioned by the police is serious. It means they think he did something. But, of course, we don't know what "it" is yet. "It" might not have happened at all. Megan might be fine. Sometimes I imagine her alive and well, sitting on a hotel balcony with a sea view, her feet up, and a cold drink nearby.

The thought of her there both excites and disappoints me. Then, I feel bad for being disappointed. I don't want her to be hurt, even though I was angry with her for cheating on Scott and ruining my idea of them as a perfect couple. No, it's because I feel like I'm part of this mystery, like I'm connected to it. I'm not just a girl on the train anymore, going back and forth without a purpose. I want Megan to be safe. I really do. Just not quite yet.

I sent Scott an email this morning. His address was easy to find. I searched for him online and found www.shipwellconsulting.co.uk, where he advertises his business services. I knew it was him because his business address is also his home address.

I sent a short message to the contact address on the site:

Dear Scott,

My name is Rachel Watson. You don't know me. I want to talk to you about your wife. I don't know where she is, and I don't know what happened to her. But I think I have information that could help you.

You might not want to talk to me, and I would understand that. But if you do, please email me at this address.

Sincerely, Rachel

I don't know if he would have contacted me anyway. I doubt I would if I were him. Like the police, he'd probably think I'm crazy, just some weirdo who read about the case in the news. Now I'll never know. If he's been arrested, he might not see the message. If he's been arrested, the police might be the only ones who see it, which would be bad for me. But I had to try.

Now I feel desperate and frustrated. I can't see through the crowd of people in the train to the other side of the tracks—my side. Even if I could, the rain is still heavy, and I wouldn't be able to see past the fence. I wonder if evidence is being washed away. Maybe important clues are disappearing right now: blood, footprints, cigarette butts with DNA. I want a drink so badly, I can almost taste the wine. I can imagine how the alcohol will make my head feel.

I want a drink, but I also don't want one. If I don't drink today, it will be three days without alcohol. I can't remember the last time I went three days without drinking. There's also a feeling of stubbornness in me. I used to have willpower. I could run 10k before breakfast and eat only 1300 calories a day for weeks. Tom loved that about me, my stubbornness and strength. I remember an argument near the end, when things were really bad. He got angry with me. "What happened to you, Rachel?" he asked. "When did you become so weak?"

I don't know. I don't know where that strength went. I don't remember losing it. I think it was slowly taken away by life.

The train stops suddenly, the brakes screeching, at the signal near Witney. People apologize as they stumble into each other. I look up and see the man from Saturday night—the one with red hair who helped me up. He's staring at me, his blue eyes locked on mine. I get such a fright that I drop my phone. I pick it up and look up again, not directly at him this time. I look around the train, wipe the foggy window, and then look back at him. He smiles at me, tilting his head a little.

My face feels hot. I don't know how to react to his smile. Is it "Oh, hello, I remember you from the other night," or is it "Ah, it's that drunk girl who fell down the stairs and talked nonsense to me," or is it something else? I don't know. But now I remember him saying, "You all right, love?" when I slipped on the steps. I turn away and look out the window again. I can feel his eyes on me. I just want to hide. The train starts moving, and we're soon pulling into Witney station. People start getting ready to leave. I look up again and feel relieved—he's turned away and is getting off the train.

Then I realize I'm being an idiot. I should get up and talk to him. He can tell me what happened, or what didn't happen. He might be able to help me remember. I stand up. I hesitate. I know it's too late. The doors are about to close. I'm in the middle of the train, and I won't be able to get through the crowd in time. The doors beep and close. I turn and look out the window as the train leaves. He's standing on the platform in the rain, watching me.

The closer I get to home, the more annoyed I am with myself. I almost want to change trains at Northcote, go back to Witney, and look for him. It's a silly and risky idea since Gaskill told me to stay away from the area yesterday. But I'm feeling hopeless about remembering what happened on Saturday. I did some research online this afternoon, and it confirmed what I thought: hypnosis doesn't usually help people remember things from a blackout. We don't make memories during a blackout. There's nothing to remember. It will always be a blank space in my memory.

MEGAN

• • •

THURSDAY, MARCH 7, 2013

AFTERNOON

The room is dark and stuffy. It smells sweet, like us. We're at the Swan again, in the attic room. But it feels different because he's still here, looking at me.

"Where do you want to go?" he asks.

"A beach house on the Costa de la Luz," I say.

He smiles. "What will we do?"

I laugh. "Besides this?"

His fingers are slowly touching my stomach. "Besides this."

"We'll start a cafe, show art, and learn to surf."

He kisses my hip. "What about Thailand?" he asks.

I scrunch my nose. "Too many young travelers. Let's go to Sicily," I say. "The Egadi islands. We'll open a beach bar and go fishing..."

He laughs and then moves on top of me and kisses me. "You're irresistible," he whispers.

I want to laugh and say: See? I won! I told you it wasn't the last time, it's never the last time. I bite my lip and close my eyes. I was right, but saying it won't help. I enjoy my victory quietly, almost as much as his touch.

Afterwards, he talks to me differently. Usually, I do all the talking, but this time he shares his feelings. He talks about feeling empty, his family, the women before me, and the one who hurt him badly. I don't believe in soul mates, but we understand each other in a way I haven't felt before, or at least not for a long time. It comes from shared pain, from knowing what it's like to be broken.

Emptiness: I understand that. I'm starting to think you can't fix it. That's what I've learned from therapy: the holes in your life stay. You have to grow around them, like tree roots around concrete; you change to fit the gaps. I know all this, but I don't say it now.

"When will we go?" I ask, but he doesn't answer. I fall asleep, and he's gone when I wake up.

FRIDAY, MARCH 8, 2013

MORNING

Scott brings me coffee to the patio.

“You slept last night,” he says, bending to kiss my head. He’s behind me, hands on my shoulders, warm and strong. I lean my head back against him, close my eyes, and listen to the train going by until it stops near the house. When we first moved here, Scott would wave at the people on the train, which always made me laugh. He holds my shoulders a little tighter; he leans forward and kisses my neck.

“You slept,” he says again. “You must feel better.”

“I do,” I say.

“Do you think it helped, then?” he asks. “The therapy?”

“Do you mean, do I think I’m cured?”

“Not cured,” he says, and I hear the sadness in his voice. “I didn’t mean…”

“I know.” I lift my hand to his and squeeze it. “I was just kidding. I think it takes time. It’s not easy, you know? I don’t know if I’ll ever be able to say that it worked. That I’m better.”

There’s a quiet moment, and he holds me a little tighter. “So you want to keep going?” he asks, and I say yes.

There was a time when I thought he could be everything I needed. I thought that for years. I loved him completely. I still do. But I don’t want this anymore. The only time I feel like myself is on those secret, exciting afternoons like yesterday, when I feel alive in all that heat and dim light. What if I run and find that isn’t enough? What if I end up feeling the same way I do now—not safe, but trapped? Maybe I’ll want to run again, and again, and eventually I’ll end up back by those old tracks, because there’s nowhere else to go. Maybe. Maybe not. You have to try, right?

I go downstairs to say goodbye as he leaves for work. He puts his arms around my waist and kisses the top of my head.

"Love you, Megs," he whispers, and I feel awful then, like the worst person ever. I can't wait for him to close the door because I know I'm going to cry.

RACHEL

• • •

FRIDAY, JULY 19, 2013

MORNING

The 8:04 train is almost empty. The windows are open, and the air is nice and cool after the storm yesterday. Megan has been gone for about 133 hours, and I feel better than I have in a long time. When I looked in the mirror this morning, I could see a change in my face: my skin looks better, and my eyes are brighter. I feel lighter. I don't think I've actually lost any weight, but I don't feel weighed down. I feel like myself—the way I used to be.

I haven't heard anything from Scott. I checked the internet, and there was no news about an arrest, so I guess he just ignored my email. I'm a little sad, but I guess that's what I expected. Gaskill called this morning, just as I was leaving my house. He asked if I could come to the police station today. I was scared for a second, but then he said in his calm voice that he just wanted me to look at a few pictures. I asked him if Scott Hipwell had been arrested.

"No one has been arrested, Ms. Watson," he said.

"But the man, the one they're questioning...?"

"I can't talk about that."

His way of talking is so calm and comforting, it makes me like him again.

Yesterday evening, I sat on the sofa in my sweats and a T-shirt. I made lists of things to do and ways to do them. For example, I could go to Witney station during the busy time and wait for the red-haired man from Saturday night. I could ask him for a drink and see what happens. Maybe he saw something or knows something about that night. But, I might see Anna or Tom, and they would tell on me. Then I would get in more trouble with the police. Also, I might put myself in danger. I still remember the argument, and I might have marks on my head and lip. What if he is the man who hurt me? Just because he smiled and waved doesn't mean anything. He could be a crazy person. But, I don't think he is. I like him for some reason.

I could call Scott again. But I need a reason for him to talk to me. I'm worried that what I saw will make me seem crazy. He might even think I had something to do with Megan's disappearance and tell the police. Then I would be in big trouble.

I could try hypnosis. I don't think it will help me remember, but I'm curious. It can't hurt, right?

I was still making notes and reading the news stories when Cathy came home. She had been to the movies with Damien. She was happy to see me sober, but also careful because we haven't talked much since the police came on Tuesday. I told her I hadn't had a drink in three days, and she hugged me.

"I'm so glad you're getting back to normal!" she said, like she knows what "normal" is for me.

"The police thing was a mistake," I said. "There's no problem with me and Tom, and I don't know anything about the missing girl. You don't have to worry." She hugged me again and made us tea. I thought about telling her about my job, but I didn't want to ruin her good mood.

She was still happy with me this morning. She hugged me as I was leaving.

"I'm so pleased for you, Rach," she said. "You're getting better. I was worried." Then she said she was spending the weekend at Damien's. The first thing I thought was that I could have a drink tonight without anyone judging me.

EVENING

I love the bitter taste of a cold gin and tonic. The tonic water should be Schweppes in a glass bottle, not plastic. These premixed drinks are not good, but they will do. I know I shouldn't be drinking, but I've been wanting to all day. It's not just being alone, it's the excitement. I'm feeling good, my skin is tingling. I had a good day.

I spent an hour alone with Detective Inspector Gaskill this morning. I was taken to see him right away when I got to the station. We sat in his office, not the interview room. He offered me coffee, and I was surprised when he made it himself. He had a kettle and some Nescafé on a fridge in the corner of the office. He said sorry for not having sugar.

I liked being with him. I liked watching his hands move. He doesn't show much emotion, but he moves things a lot. I hadn't noticed this before because there wasn't much to move in the interview room. In his office, he kept changing the position of his coffee mug, his stapler, a jar of pens, and he made his papers into neater piles. He has big hands and long fingers with neat nails. He didn't wear any rings.

It felt different this morning. I didn't feel like a suspect, someone he was trying to trick. I felt helpful. I felt most helpful when he put a folder in front of me, showing me some photos. Scott Hipwell, three men I didn't know, and then B.

I wasn't sure at first. I looked at the picture, trying to remember the man I saw with her that day, his head down as he hugged her.

"That's him," I said. "I think that's him."

"You're not sure?"

"I think that's him."

He took the picture and looked at it himself. "You saw them kissing, right? Last Friday? A week ago?"

"Yes, that's right. Friday morning. They were outside, in the garden."

"And you're sure you didn't misunderstand what you saw? It wasn't a hug, or a friendly kiss?"

"No, it wasn't. It was a real kiss. It was... romantic."

I thought his lips moved like he was about to smile.

"Who is he?" I asked Gaskill. "Is he... Do you think she's with him?" He didn't answer, just shook his head a little. "Is this... Have I helped? Have I been helpful at all?"

"Yes, Ms. Watson. You've been helpful. Thank you for coming in."

We shook hands, and for a second he put his left hand lightly on my right shoulder. I wanted to turn and kiss it. It's been a while since anyone touched me gently. Well, except for Cathy.

Gaskill led me out of the door into the main office. There were about twelve police officers there. One or two looked at me quickly. They might have been interested or not liked me, I wasn't sure. We walked through the office and into the hall. Then I saw him walking towards me with Riley beside him: Scott Hipwell. He was coming through the main entrance. His head was down, but I knew it was him. He looked up and nodded to Gaskill, then he looked at me. For a second, our eyes met and I thought he recognized me. I remembered that morning when I saw him on the terrace, looking down at the track, when I felt him looking at me. We passed each other in the hall. He was so close I could have touched him. He was handsome, thin, and tense, like he had a lot of energy. As I got to the main hall, I turned to look at him, sure he was watching me. But when I looked back, Riley was watching me.

I took the train to London and went to the library. I read every article about the case, but didn't learn anything new. I looked for hypnotherapists in Ashbury, but didn't do anything about it. It's expensive and it's not clear if it helps people remember things. But reading stories of people who said they remembered things through hypnotherapy, I realized I was more afraid of success than failure. I'm afraid of what I might learn about that Saturday night, and more. I'm not sure I could handle remembering the stupid, bad things I've done, the mean words I said, or the look on Tom's face when I said them. I'm too afraid to go into that darkness.

I thought about sending Scott another email, but I don't need to. The meeting with Detective Gaskill showed me that the police are taking me seriously. I don't have any more to do. I have to accept that now. And I feel like I might have helped, because I can't believe it's a coincidence that Megan disappeared the day after I saw her with that man.

With a happy click, fizz, I open the second can of G&T and realize I haven't thought about Tom all day. Until now. I've been thinking about Scott, Gaskill, B, and the man on the train. Tom is now fifth on my list. I drink and feel like I have something to celebrate. I know I'm going to be better, that I'm going to be happy. It won't be long.

SATURDAY, JULY 20, 2013

MORNING

I never learn. I wake up feeling awful, full of shame, and I know I've done something stupid. I go through my usual, painful routine of trying to remember what I did. I sent an email. That's it.

Last night, I started thinking about Tom again, and I sent him an email. My laptop is on the floor next to my bed, like a silent accusation. I step over it to go to the bathroom. I drink water from the tap and quickly look at myself in the mirror.

I look bad. Still, three days off isn't terrible, and I'll start over today. I stand in the shower for a long time, making the water colder and colder. You can't jump into cold water, it's too shocking, but if you get there slowly, you barely notice it. The cold water feels good on my skin and helps with the pain from the cuts on my head and above my eye.

I take my laptop downstairs and make tea. There's a small chance I wrote the email but didn't send it. I take a deep breath and open my email. I'm relieved to see no new messages. But when I click on the Sent folder, there it is: I wrote to him, he just hasn't replied yet. The email was sent just after eleven last night; I'd been drinking for hours. The energy and buzz I had earlier would be gone. I click on the message.

Could you please tell your wife to stop lying to the police about me? It's pretty low to try to get me in trouble. Telling police I'm obsessed with her and her ugly kid? She needs to get over herself. Tell her to leave me alone.

I close my eyes and shut the laptop. I'm cringing, my whole body shrinking. I want to be small; I want to disappear. I'm also scared because if Tom shows this to the police, I could be in trouble. If Anna is collecting proof that I'm mean and obsessed, this could be important. And why did I mention the little girl? What kind of person does that? I don't hate her—I could never hate a child, especially Tom's child. I don't understand myself; I don't understand who I've become. God, he must hate me. I hate me—that version of me who wrote that email last night. She doesn't even feel like me, because I'm not like that. I'm not hateful.

Am I? I try not to think about the worst times, but the memories come back at times like this. Another fight, near the end: waking up after a party, after blacking out, Tom telling me how I acted the night before, embarrassing him again, insulting his colleague's wife for flirting with him. "I don't want to go anywhere with you anymore," he told me. "You ask why I never invite friends over, why I don't like going to the pub with you. You want to know why? It's because of you. Because I'm ashamed of you."

I grab my bag and keys. I'm going to the store down the road. I don't care that it's not even nine in the morning, I'm scared and I don't want to think. If I take painkillers and have a drink now, I can go to sleep all day. I'll deal with it later. I get to the door, my hand on the handle, then I stop.

I could apologize. If I apologize now, I might fix things. I might get him not to show the message to Anna or the police. It wouldn't be the first time he's protected me from her.

Last summer, when I went to Tom and Anna’s house, things didn't happen exactly as I told the police. I didn't ring the doorbell, for one thing. I wasn't sure what I wanted—I'm still not sure what I planned to do. I did walk down the path and over the fence. It was quiet, and I couldn't hear anything. I went to the sliding doors and looked inside. It's true that Anna was sleeping on the couch. I didn't call out to her or Tom. I didn't want to wake her. The baby wasn't crying; she was asleep in her carrier next to her mother. I picked her up and took her outside as fast as I could. I remember running with her toward the fence. The baby started to wake up and fuss a little. I don't know what I thought I was doing. I wasn't going to hurt her. I got to the fence, holding her tightly. She was crying loudly now, starting to scream. I was bouncing her and trying to quiet her when I heard a train. I turned away from the fence and saw Anna running toward me. Her mouth was open wide, and her lips were moving, but I couldn't hear what she was saying.

She took the child from me, and I tried to run, but I tripped and fell. She stood over me, yelling. She told me to stay there or she'd call the police. She called Tom, and he came home and sat with her in the living room. She was crying a lot and still wanted to call the police and have me arrested for kidnapping. Tom calmed her down and asked her to let it go and let me leave. He saved me from her. Afterward, he drove me home. When he dropped me off, he took my hand. I thought it was a kind gesture, but he squeezed harder and harder until I cried out. His face was red when he told me he would kill me if I ever did anything to hurt his daughter.

I don't know what I planned to do that day. I still don't. At the door, I hesitate, my fingers around the handle. I bite my lip hard. I know that if I start drinking now, I will feel better for an hour or two and worse for six or seven. I let go of the handle and walk back into the living room. I open my laptop again. I have to apologize and ask for forgiveness. I log back into my email and see one new message. It isn't from Tom. It's from Scott Hipwell.

Dear Rachel,

Thank you for contacting me. I don’t remember Megan mentioning you, but she knew many people at the gallery. I'm not good with names. I would like to talk to you about what you know. Please call me at 07583 123657 as soon as possible.

Regards,
Scott Hipwell

For a moment, I think he sent the email to the wrong person. This message must be for someone else. It's just a quick thought, and then I remember. I remember. Sitting on the couch, halfway through the second bottle, I realized I didn't want my part to be over. I wanted to be in the middle of it.

So I wrote to him.

I scroll down from his email to mine.

Dear Scott,

I'm sorry to bother you again, but I think it's important for us to talk. I don't know if Megan ever mentioned me. I'm a friend from the art place. I used to live in Witney. I think I have some information you might want to know. Please email me back at this address.

Rachel Watson

My face feels hot, and my stomach is upset. Yesterday, when I was thinking clearly, I decided I was done with this story. But I messed up again because I drank too much. When I'm drunk, I don't think about what might happen. I'm either too happy and hopeful or full of anger. I don't think about the past or the future. I only care about the moment. Drunk Rachel wanted to be part of the story and needed a way to get Scott to talk to her, so she lied. I lied.

I want to hurt myself so I can feel something other than shame, but I'm too scared to do it. I start writing to Tom, then deleting it, trying to find a way to say sorry for what I said last night. If I had to write down everything I need to apologize to Tom for, it would fill a book.

EVENING

A week ago, almost exactly a week ago, Megan Hipwell left her house at number fifteen Blenheim Road and disappeared. No one has seen her since. Her phone and bank cards haven't been used since Saturday either. When I read that in the news today, I started to cry. I'm ashamed of the secret thoughts I had. Megan isn't a puzzle to solve. She's not a character in a movie. She's a real person.

I'm on the train, going to her house. I'm going to meet her husband.

I had to call him. I already messed up. I couldn't just ignore the email. He would tell the police, right? I would if I were him, if a stranger contacted me saying they had information and then disappeared. He might have already called the police. They might be waiting for me when I get there.

Sitting here, in my usual seat, but not on my usual day, I feel like I'm about to crash. It felt the same this morning when I called him, like falling into the dark, not knowing when I'll hit the ground. He spoke quietly, like someone else was in the room and he didn't want them to hear.

"Can we talk in person?" he asked.

“I . . . no. I don’t think so . . .”

“Please?”

I paused for a second, and then I said yes.

“Could you come to my house? Not right now, because there are people here. Maybe this evening?” He told me his address, and I pretended to write it down.

“Thank you for calling me,” he said, and then he hung up.

I knew that saying yes was a bad idea. I don't know much about Scott from the news. What I think I know from watching him might not be true. I don't really know anything about Scott. I know things about Jason—who, I have to remember, isn't real. All I know for sure is that Scott’s wife has been missing for a week. I know that he is probably a suspect. And I know, because I saw that kiss, that he might have wanted to kill her. He might not even know that he had a reason, but . . . I've thought about it so much, but how could I not go to that house? The one I've watched so many times from the train tracks and the street. To walk up to his door, to go inside, to sit in his kitchen, on his patio, where they sat, where I watched them?

It was too tempting. Now I'm on the train, holding myself tightly, my hands pressed against my sides to stop them from shaking. I feel like a kid who's excited about an adventure. I was so happy to have a plan that I stopped thinking about the danger. I stopped thinking about Megan.

I'm thinking about her now. I have to make Scott believe that I knew her—a little, not a lot. That way, he'll believe me when I tell him that I saw her with another man. If I say I lied right away, he'll never trust me. So I try to imagine what it would have been like to visit the gallery and chat with her over coffee. Did she drink coffee? We would talk about art, maybe, or yoga, or our husbands. I don't know anything about art, and I've never done yoga. I don't have a husband. And she cheated on hers.

I think about what her real friends said about her: wonderful, funny, beautiful, kind. Loved. She made a mistake. It happens. None of us are perfect.

ANNA

• • •

SATURDAY, JULY 20, 2013

MORNING

Evie wakes up a little before six. I get out of bed, go into the baby's room, and pick her up. I feed her and bring her back to bed with me.

When I wake up again, Tom isn't next to me, but I can hear him walking on the stairs. He's singing quietly and off-key, "Happy birthday to you, happy birthday to you . . ." I hadn't even thought about it earlier; I had completely forgotten. I was only thinking about getting my little girl and going back to bed. Now I'm giggling before I'm even fully awake. I open my eyes, and Evie is smiling too. When I look up, Tom is standing at the end of the bed, holding a tray. He's wearing my Orla Kiely apron and nothing else.

"Breakfast in bed, birthday girl," he says. He puts the tray down and comes around to kiss me.

I open my presents. I get a pretty silver bracelet with black stones from Evie, and a black silk nightgown and matching underwear from Tom. I can't stop smiling.
