He gets back into bed, and we lie with Evie between us. She has her fingers wrapped around his finger, and I'm holding her small pink foot. I feel like fireworks are going off inside me. It's unbelievable how much love I feel.

Later, when Evie gets tired of lying there, I get her up, and we go downstairs, leaving Tom to sleep. He deserves it. I walk around, tidying up a bit. I drink my coffee outside, watching the almost empty trains go by. I think about lunch. It's hot—too hot for a roast, but I'll make one anyway because Tom loves roast beef. We can have ice cream afterward to cool down. I just need to go out to get the red wine he likes. So, I get Evie ready, put her in the stroller, and we walk to the shops.

Everyone told me I was crazy to agree to move into Tom's house. But then everyone thought I was crazy to get involved with a married man, especially one whose wife was very unstable. I've proven them wrong about that. No matter how much trouble she causes, Tom and Evie are worth it. But they were right about the house. On days like today, when the sun is shining, and you walk down our little street—with trees and neat houses, not quite a dead end, but with a close community feel—it could be perfect. The sidewalks are full of mothers like me, with dogs and kids on scooters. It could be ideal. It could be, if you couldn't hear the loud train brakes. It could be, as long as you didn't turn around and look back at number fifteen.

When I return, Tom is at the dining table, looking at his computer. He has shorts on but no shirt. I can see his muscles move when he does. It still makes me feel excited to see him. I say hi, but he's focused on the screen. When I touch his shoulder, he jumps. The laptop closes quickly.

"Hey," he says, standing up. He's smiling but looks tired and worried. He takes Evie from me without looking at me.

"What?" I ask. "What's wrong?"

"Nothing," he says, turning to the window, holding Evie.

"Tom, what is it?"

"It's nothing." He looks back at me, and I know what he'll say. "Rachel. Another email." He shakes his head, looking hurt and upset. I hate seeing him like this. Sometimes I want to hurt that woman.

"What did she say?"

He shakes his head again. "It's not important. Just... the same old stuff. Lies."

"I'm sorry," I say. I don't ask for details because I know he doesn't want to tell me. He doesn't want to worry me with this.

"It's OK. It's nothing. Just the usual angry nonsense."

"Will she ever leave? Will she ever let us be happy?"

He comes to me and, with our daughter between us, kisses me. "We are happy," he says. "We are."

EVENING

We are happy. We ate lunch and relaxed on the grass. When it got too hot, we came inside and ate ice cream while Tom watched the car race. Evie and I played with play dough, and she ate some of that, too. I think about what's happening nearby and how lucky I am. I got everything I wanted. When I look at Tom, I thank God that he found me, too. I was there to save him from that woman. She would have made him crazy, I really think that. She would have worn him down and changed him into someone he's not.

Tom took Evie upstairs to give her a bath. I can hear her happy squeals from here, and I'm smiling again. The smile has been on my face all day. I wash the dishes, tidy the living room, and think about dinner. Something easy. It's funny, because a few years ago I would have hated staying in and cooking on my birthday. But now it's perfect, it's how it should be. Just the three of us.

I pick up Evie's toys, scattered on the living room floor, and put them back in their box. I'm looking forward to putting her to bed early tonight, and then relaxing in the teddy Tom bought me. It won't be dark for hours yet, but I light the candles on the fireplace and open the second bottle of wine to let it breathe. I'm leaning over the sofa to close the curtains when I see a woman. Her head is down, and she's walking quickly on the other side of the street. She doesn't look up, but it's her, I'm sure of it. I lean forward, my heart beating fast, trying to see her better. But I can't see her now.

I turn, ready to run out the front door to chase her, but Tom is standing there in the doorway. Evie is wrapped in a towel in his arms.

"Are you OK?" he asks. "What's wrong?"

"Nothing," I say, putting my hands in my pockets so he can't see them shaking. "Nothing's wrong. Nothing at all."

RACHEL

• • •

SUNDAY, JULY 21, 2013

MORNING

I wake up thinking about him. It feels unreal, all of it. My skin feels tingly. I really want a drink, but I can't. I need to think clearly. For Megan. For Scott.

I tried yesterday. I washed my hair and put on makeup. I wore the only jeans that fit, with a cotton shirt and low-heeled sandals. I looked okay. I told myself it was silly to care how I looked, because Scott wouldn't be thinking about that, but I couldn't help it. It was the first time I'd be near him, so it mattered to me. More than it should have.

I took the train, leaving Ashbury around six thirty, and got to Witney just after seven. I walked along Roseberry Avenue, past the underpass. I didn't look this time, I couldn't. I hurried past number twenty-three, Tom and Anna's house, head down and sunglasses on, hoping they wouldn't see me. It was quiet, no one around, just a few cars driving slowly down the road between the parked cars. It's a calm, nice street, with many young families; they're all eating dinner around seven, or sitting on the couch, parents with their kids, watching The X Factor.

From number twenty-three to number fifteen is only a short walk, but it felt like a long time; my legs felt heavy, and I was unsteady, like I was drunk, like I might fall off the sidewalk.

Scott opened the door as soon as I knocked, my hand still up as he appeared, big in front of me.

"Rachel?" he asked, looking down at me, not smiling. I nodded. He held out his hand and I took it. He asked me to come in, but I didn't move for a moment. I was scared of him. Up close, he is big and scary, tall and with wide shoulders, his arms and chest strong. His hands are huge. I thought he could easily hurt me—my neck, my ribs—without much effort.

I walked past him into the hall, my arm touching his, and my face felt hot. He smelled like old sweat, and his dark hair was stuck to his head like he hadn't showered in a while.

In the living room, I felt a strong sense of déjà vu, almost scary. I knew the fireplace with alcoves on the far wall, the way light came in from the street through the blinds. I knew that if I turned left, I'd see glass and green, and the train tracks beyond. I turned and saw the kitchen table, the French doors, and the green lawn. I knew this house. I felt dizzy and wanted to sit down. I thought about the lost hours from last Saturday night.

It didn't mean anything, of course. I know this house, but not because I've been here. It's just like number twenty-three: a hall leads to the stairs, and the living room is on the right, connected to the kitchen. The patio and garden are familiar because I've seen them from the train. I didn't go upstairs, but I know there's a landing with a big window, and if you climbed out, you'd be on the roof terrace. There are two bedrooms, the main one with two big windows facing the street, and a smaller one at the back, looking at the garden. Just because I know this house well doesn't mean I've been here before.

Still, I was shaking when Scott led me to the kitchen. He offered me tea. I sat at the table while he made it, dropping a tea bag in a mug and spilling hot water on the counter, talking quietly to himself. The room smelled strongly of cleaning stuff, but Scott was a mess, with sweat on his shirt and loose jeans. I wondered when he last ate.

He put the tea in front of me and sat across from me, his hands together. The silence grew, filling the space between us, the whole room. It rang in my ears, and I felt hot and uneasy, my mind blank. I didn't know why I was there. Why had I come? In the distance, I heard a train. The familiar sound felt comforting.

"You're a friend of Megan's?" he finally said.

Hearing her name made my throat tighten. I looked at the table, holding the mug tightly.

"Yes," I said. "I know her... a little. From the gallery."

He looked at me, waiting. I saw his jaw tighten. I looked for words but couldn't find any. I should have planned better.

"Have you heard any news?" I asked. He looked at me, and for a second I felt scared. I'd said the wrong thing; it wasn't my business. He might be angry and ask me to leave.

"No," he said. "What did you want to tell me?"

The train slowly went by, and I looked at the tracks. I felt like I was floating, like I was watching myself from outside my body.

"In your email, you said you wanted to tell me something about Megan." His voice got a little higher.

I took a deep breath. I felt terrible. I knew that what I was about to say would make things worse and hurt him.

"I saw her with someone," I said. I just said it quickly and loudly, without any warning.

He stared at me. "When? You saw her on Saturday night? Did you tell the police?"

"No, it was Friday morning," I said, and his shoulders dropped.

"But... she was okay on Friday. Why does that matter?" The muscle in his jaw jumped again; he was getting angry. "You saw her with... who did you see her with? A man?"

"Yes, I—"

"What did he look like?" He stood up, blocking the light. "Did you tell the police?" he asked again.

"I did, but I don't think they believed me," I said.

"Why?"

"I just... I don't know... I thought you should know."

He leaned forward, his hands on the table, made into fists. "What are you saying? You saw her where? What was she doing?"

I took another deep breath. "She was... outside on your grass," I said. "Right there." I pointed to the garden. "She... I saw her from the train." He looked like he didn't believe me. "I take the train to London from Ashbury every day. It goes right past here. I saw her, she was with someone. And it... it wasn't you."

"How do you know? ... Friday morning? Friday—the day before she disappeared?"

"Yes."

"I wasn't here," he said. "I was away. I was at a meeting in Birmingham, I got back on Friday night." His cheeks turned red, his doubt changing to something else. "So you saw her, on the grass, with someone? And..."

"She kissed him," I said. I had to say it. I had to tell him. "They were kissing."

He stood up straight, his hands still in fists, hanging by his sides. The red on his cheeks got darker, angrier.

"I'm sorry," I said. "I'm so sorry. I know this is awful to hear..."

He held up his hand, waving me away. He looked disgusted. He didn't want my sympathy.

I know how that feels. Sitting there, I remembered clearly how it felt when I sat in my own kitchen, five doors down. Lara, my old best friend, sat across from me, her chubby toddler wiggling on her lap. I remember her saying she was sorry my marriage was over. I remember getting angry at her empty words. She didn't understand my pain. I told her to go away, and she told me not to talk like that in front of her child. I haven't seen her since.

"What did this man look like?" Scott asked. He was standing with his back to me, looking at the lawn.

"He was tall—maybe taller than you. Dark-skinned. I think he might have been Asian. Indian—something like that."

"And they were kissing, out here in the garden?"

"Yes."

He sighed deeply. "Jesus, I need a drink." He turned to me. "Would you like a beer?"

I did. I really wanted a drink, but I said no. I watched him get a bottle from the fridge, open it, and take a big gulp. I could almost feel the cold drink going down my throat as I watched him. My hand wanted a glass. Scott leaned against the counter, his head down.

I felt terrible then. I wasn't helping. I had just made him feel worse, increased his pain. I was bothering him in his sadness. It was wrong. I shouldn't have gone to see him. I shouldn't have lied. Of course, I shouldn't have lied.

I was just standing up when he spoke. “It could . . . I don’t know. It might be good, right? It could mean she’s okay. She’s just . . .” He laughed a little, sadly. “She’s just run off with someone.” He wiped a tear from his face, and my heart felt tight. “But I can’t believe she wouldn’t call.” He looked at me like I had the answers. “She would call me, wouldn’t she? She knows how worried I would be. She’s not mean like that, is she?”

He was talking to me like he trusted me—like I was Megan’s friend—and I knew it was wrong, but it felt good. He drank more of his beer and looked at the garden. I looked where he was looking, at a small pile of rocks by the fence, a rock garden that was never finished. He started to lift the bottle to his mouth, then stopped. He turned to me.

“You saw Megan from the train?” he asked. “So you were just looking out the window and saw her, someone you know?” The mood in the room changed. He wasn't sure if I was on his side anymore. He looked unsure.

“Yes, I . . . I know where she lives,” I said, and I wished I hadn't said it. “Where you live, I mean. I’ve been here before. A long time ago. So sometimes I’d look for her when I went by.” He was staring at me; I felt my face get hot. “She was often outside.”

He put his empty bottle on the counter, walked a few steps towards me, and sat down at the table, in the chair closest to me.

“So you knew Megan well? Well enough to come to the house?”

I could feel my heart pounding, sweat on my back, and a rush of fear. I shouldn't have said that, shouldn't have made the lie harder.

“It was just once, but I . . . I know where the house is because I used to live close by.” He looked surprised. “Down the road. Number twenty-three.”

He nodded slowly. “Watson,” he said. “So you’re, what, Tom’s ex-wife?”

“Yes. I moved out a couple of years ago.”

"But you still went to Megan's gallery?"

"Sometimes."

"And when you saw her, what did you... Did she talk about private things, about me?" His voice was rough. "About anyone else?"

I shook my head. "No, no. We were just... chatting, you know." There was a long silence. The room felt hotter, and the smell of cleaning stuff was strong. I felt dizzy. To my right was a small table with framed photos. Megan smiled at me, looking like she was teasing me.

"I should leave now," I said. "I've taken too much of your time." I started to get up, but he reached out and put his hand on my wrist, looking right at me.

"Don't leave yet," he said quietly. I didn't get up, but I pulled my hand away; it felt like he was holding me back. "This man," he said. "This man you saw her with—do you think you'd know him again? If you saw him?"

I couldn't say that I had already told the police who the man was. I came to him because the police didn't believe me. If I told him the truth, he wouldn't trust me. So I lied again.

"I'm not sure," I said. "But I think I might." I waited a second, and then I said, "In the news, there was a quote from a friend of Megan's. His name was Rajesh. I was wondering if—"

Scott was already shaking his head. "Rajesh Gujral? I don't think so. He's one of the artists who showed his work at the gallery. He's a nice guy, but... he's married, he has kids." Like that meant something. "Wait a minute," he said, standing up. "I think I might have a picture of him somewhere."

He went upstairs. I felt my shoulders relax and realized I had been very tense since I got here. I looked at the photos again: Megan in a summer dress on a beach; a close-up of her face, her eyes very blue. Just Megan. No pictures of them together.

Scott came back holding a small paper, which he showed me. It was a flyer, advertising a show at the art place. He flipped it over. “Look,” he said, “that’s Rajesh.”

The man was next to a bright, strange painting. He was older, with a beard, short, and heavy. It wasn't the man I had seen, the man I told the police about. “It’s not him,” I said. Scott stood next to me, looking at the flyer, then quickly turned and went out of the room and upstairs again. A few minutes later, he returned with a laptop and sat at the kitchen table.

“I think,” he said, opening the laptop, “I think I might . . .” He stopped talking and I watched him. He looked very focused, his jaw tight. “Megan was seeing a therapist,” he told me. “His name is . . . Abdic. Kamal Abdic. He’s not from Asia, he’s from Serbia, or Bosnia, somewhere like that. He has dark skin, though. He could look like he's from India from far away.” He typed on the computer. “There’s a website, I think. I’m sure there is. I think there’s a picture . . .”

He turned the laptop so I could see the screen. I leaned closer to look. “That’s him,” I said. “That’s definitely him.”

Scott closed the laptop quickly. He didn't say anything for a long time. He sat with his elbows on the table, his head in his hands, his arms shaking.

“She was having panic attacks,” he finally said. “Trouble sleeping, things like that. It started last year sometime. I don’t remember exactly when.” He spoke without looking at me, like he was talking to himself, like he forgot I was there. “I was the one who told her to talk to someone. I was the one who pushed her to go, because I couldn’t help her.” His voice broke a little. “I couldn’t help her. And she told me she had similar problems before and they went away, but I made her . . . I convinced her to go to the doctor. They told her about that guy.” He coughed a little. “The therapy seemed to be helping. She was happier.” He laughed a little, sadly. “Now I know why.”

I reached out to pat his arm, to comfort him. He quickly moved away and stood up. “You should leave,” he said quickly. “My mother will be here soon—she won’t leave me alone for more than an hour or two.” At the door, as I was leaving, he grabbed my arm.

“Have I seen you before?” he asked.

For a second, I thought about saying, Maybe. You might have seen me at the police station, or here on the street. I was here on Saturday night. I shook my head. “No, I don’t think so.”

I walked towards the train station as fast as I could. About halfway down the street, I turned to look back. He was still standing in the doorway, watching me.

EVENING

I keep checking my email, but I haven't heard from Tom. Life must have been easier for jealous people before emails, texts, and cell phones, before all this technology and the records it leaves.

There was hardly anything in the news about Megan today. They're already moving on. The front pages are about the political problems in Turkey, a young girl hurt by dogs, and the England soccer team's bad loss to Montenegro. Megan is being forgotten, and she's only been gone a week.

Cathy asked me to lunch. She was bored because Damien went to see his mother in Birmingham. She wasn't invited. They've been together for almost two years, and she still hasn't met his mother. We went to Giraffe, a place I hate. Sitting in a noisy room full of young kids, Cathy asked me what I'd been doing. She wanted to know where I was last night.

"Have you met someone?" she asked, looking hopeful. It was sweet, actually.

I almost said yes, because it was true, but lying was easier. I told her I'd been to a meeting in Witney.

"Oh," she said, looking embarrassed. "I thought maybe you had a drink. On Friday."

"Yes. It won't be easy, Cathy," I said, and I felt bad, because I think she really wants me to stay sober. "But I'm trying my best."

"If you want me to go with you..."

"Not yet," I said. "But thank you."

"Maybe we could do something else, like go to the gym?" she asked.

I laughed, but when I saw she was serious, I said I would think about it.

She just left. Damien called to say he was back from his mother's, so she went to his place. I thought about saying something to her—Why do you always go to him when he calls? But I'm not the right person to give advice about relationships, or any advice at all. Anyway, I feel like a drink. (I've wanted one since we sat down at Giraffe and the waiter asked if we wanted wine, and Cathy said "No, thank you" very firmly.) So I wave goodbye and feel a little excited. I push away the good thoughts (Don't do this, you're doing well). I'm putting on my shoes to go to the store when my phone rings. It's Tom. It has to be Tom. I grab the phone and look at the screen, and my heart beats fast.

"Hi." There's silence, so I ask, "Is everything okay?"

After a short pause, Scott says, "Yeah, fine. I'm okay. I just wanted to say thank you for yesterday. For taking the time to tell me."

"Oh, that's okay. You didn't need to—"

"Am I bothering you?"

"No. It's fine." There's silence again, so I say, "It's fine. Has something happened? Did you talk to the police?"

"The family officer was here this afternoon," he says. My heart beats faster. "Detective Riley. I told her about Kamal Abdic. I said she should talk to him."

"You said... you told her that you talked to me?" My mouth is very dry.

"No, I didn't. I thought maybe... I don't know. I thought it would be better if I thought of the name myself. I said... it's a lie, I know, but I said I had been trying hard to think of something important, and that I thought it might be good to talk to her therapist. I said I had been worried about their relationship before."

I can relax now. "What did she say?" I ask him.

"She said they already talked to him, but they would again. She asked me many questions about why I hadn't mentioned him before. She's... I don't know. I don't trust her. She's supposed to help me, but I always feel like she's watching me, like she's trying to trick me."

I'm happy that he doesn't like her either; it's another thing we agree on, another connection between us.

"I just wanted to thank you for coming forward. It was actually... it sounds strange, but it was good to talk to someone... someone I don't know well. I felt like I could think more clearly. After you left, I kept thinking about the first time Megan saw him—Abdic—and how she was when she came back. There was something different about her, a happiness." He breathes out loudly. "I don't know. Maybe I'm imagining it."

I feel the same as yesterday—that he's not really talking to me, he's just talking. I've become someone he can talk to, and I'm happy about it. I'm glad I can help him.

"I've spent all day looking through Megan's things again," he says. "I've already searched our room, the whole house, many times, looking for something, anything that would tell me where she could be. Something from him, maybe. But there's nothing. No emails, no letters, nothing. I thought about contacting him, but his office is closed today and I can't find his phone number."

"Is that a good idea, do you think?" I ask. "I mean, don't you think you should let the police handle him?" I don't want to say it, but we must both be thinking it: he's dangerous. Or at least, he could be dangerous.

"I don't know, I just don't know." His voice sounds desperate, which is hard to hear, but I can't help him. I can hear him breathing on the phone; it sounds fast, like he's scared. I want to ask if someone is there with him, but I can't: it would sound wrong, too personal.

"I saw your ex today," he says, and I feel scared.

"Oh?"

"Yes, I went to get the newspapers and saw him on the street. He asked if I was okay, if there was any news."

"Oh," I say again, because I can't think of anything else to say. I don't want him to talk to Tom. Tom knows I don't know Megan Hipwell. Tom knows I was on Blenheim Road the night she went missing.

"I didn't say anything about you. I didn't... you know. I wasn't sure if I should have said I'd met you."

"No, I don't think you should have. I don't know. It might be strange."

"Okay," he says.

Then, there's a long quiet time. I'm waiting for my heart to calm down. I think he's going to hang up, but then he says, "Did she really never talk about me?"

"Of course... of course she did," I say. "I mean, we didn't talk very often, but—"

"But you came to the house. Megan almost never has people over. She's very private and keeps to herself."

I'm trying to think of a reason. I wish I hadn't told him I'd been to the house.

"I just came over to get a book."

"Really?" He doesn't believe me. She doesn't read. I remember her house—there were no books there. "What did she say? About me?"

"Well, she was very happy," I say. "With you, I mean. Your relationship." As I say this, it sounds strange, but I can't be specific, so I try to fix it. "To be honest, I was having a bad time in my marriage, so I think I was comparing things. She seemed very happy when she talked about you." That's a bad cliché.

"Did she?" He doesn't seem to notice, his voice sounds sad. "That's good to hear." He stops, and I can hear him breathing quickly on the phone. "We had a bad fight," he says. "The night she left. I hate that she was angry with me when..." He stops talking.

"I'm sure she wasn't angry for long," I say. "Couples fight. Couples fight all the time."

"But this was bad, it was terrible, and I can't... I feel like I can't tell anyone, because they would think I was guilty."

His voice sounds different now: sad and full of guilt.

"I don't remember how it started," he says, and I don't believe him, but then I think about the fights I've forgotten and I stay quiet. "It got very intense. I was very... I was mean to her. I was awful. Completely awful. She was upset. She went upstairs and packed a bag. I don't know what she packed, but I saw later that her toothbrush was gone, so I knew she wasn't coming back. I thought she went to Tara's for the night. That happened once before. Just one time. It wasn't like this happened all the time.

"I didn't even go after her," he says, and I realize again that he's not really talking to me, he's confessing. He's telling his secrets and I'm listening, unseen. "I just let her go."

"That was on Saturday night?"

"Yes. That was the last time I saw her."

A person saw her, or "a woman who looked like her," walking toward Witney station around 7:15. I know this from the news. That was the last time anyone saw her. No one remembers seeing her on the train platform or on the train. There are no security cameras at Witney. She wasn't seen on the cameras at Corly either, but the news said that doesn't mean she wasn't there because there are "big blind spots" at that station.

"What time did you try to call her?" I ask him. He's quiet for a long time again.

"I... I went to the pub. The Rose, it's just around the corner on Kingly Road. I needed to calm down and think clearly. I had a couple of beers, then I went home. That was a little before ten. I guess I was hoping she'd be calm and back home. But she wasn't."

"So, it was around ten when you tried to call her?"

"No." His voice is very quiet now. "I didn't. I had a couple more beers at home, watched some TV. Then I went to bed."

I think about all the fights I had with Tom, all the awful things I said when I'd had too much to drink. I remember storming out, yelling at him, saying I never wanted to see him again. He always called me, calmed me down, and got me to come home.

"I just thought she was at Tara's, talking about how awful I am. So I didn't do anything."

He didn't do anything. It sounds cold and like he didn't care. I'm not surprised he hasn't told anyone this. I'm surprised he's telling me. This isn't the Scott I thought I knew, the one who stood behind Megan, his hands on her shoulders, ready to protect her.

I'm about to hang up, but Scott keeps talking. "I woke up early. There were no messages on my phone. I didn't worry. I thought she was with Tara and still mad at me. I called her then and got her voicemail, but I still didn't worry. I thought she was probably still sleeping or just ignoring me. I couldn't find Tara's number, but I had her address on a business card on Megan's desk. So, I got up and drove there."

I wondered, if he wasn't worried, why he felt he needed to go to Tara's house, but I didn't interrupt. I let him talk.

"I got to Tara's place a little after nine. It took her a while to open the door, but when she did, she looked very surprised to see me. It was clear that I was the last person she expected to see at her door that morning, and that's when I knew... That's when I knew that Megan wasn't there. And I started to think... I started..." He stopped talking, and I felt bad for doubting him.

"She told me the last time she'd seen Megan was at their Pilates class on Friday night. That's when I started to panic."

After I hung up the phone, I thought about how, if you didn't know him, if you hadn't seen how he was with her, like I have, a lot of what he'd said wouldn't seem true.

MONDAY, JULY 22, 2013

MORNING

I feel confused. I slept well but had strange dreams, and this morning I'm having trouble waking up. The hot weather is back, and the train car is very stuffy today, even though it's only half full. I woke up late this morning and didn't have time to get a newspaper or check the news online before I left the house. So, I'm trying to get the BBC website on my phone, but it's taking a long time to load. At Northcote, a man with an iPad gets on and sits next to me. He has no problem getting the news, he goes straight to the Daily Telegraph website, and there it is, in big, clear letters, the third story: MAN ARRESTED IN CONNECTION WITH MEGAN HIPWELL DISAPPEARANCE.

I get such a shock that I forget myself and lean over to get a better look. He looks up at me, annoyed, almost surprised.

"I'm sorry," I say. "I know her. The missing woman. I know her."

"Oh, how terrible," he says. He's a middle-aged man, who speaks clearly and is well-dressed. "Would you like to read the story?"

"Please. My phone isn't working."

He smiles kindly and gives me the tablet. I tap the title, and the story appears.

A man in his thirties has been arrested because of the disappearance of Megan Hipwell, twenty-nine, from Witney, who has been missing since Saturday, July 13th. The police haven't said if the man arrested is Megan Hipwell's husband, Scott Hipwell, who was questioned on Friday. This morning, a police officer said: “We have arrested a man about Megan’s disappearance. He has not been charged yet. We are still looking for Megan, and we are searching a place that we think might be a crime scene.”

We are going past the house now; the train didn't stop at the signal this time. I quickly turn my head, but it's too late. It's gone. My hands shake as I give the tablet back. He shakes his head sadly. “I’m very sorry,” he says.

“She’s not dead,” I say. My voice is rough, and I don't even believe it. Tears are coming to my eyes. I was in his house. I was there. I sat across from him, I looked at him, I felt something. I think about his big hands and how he could hurt her—small, weak Megan.

The train stops at Witney station, and I jump up.

“I have to go,” I say to the man next to me, who looks a bit surprised but nods.

“Good luck,” he says.

I run along the platform and down the stairs. I'm going against the crowd, and I almost reach the bottom when I trip, and a man says, “Watch out!” I don't look at him because I'm looking at the edge of the second-to-last step. There's a spot of blood on it. I wonder how long it's been there. Could it be a week old? Could it be my blood? Hers? Is her blood in the house, I wonder, is that why they arrested him? I try to picture the kitchen, the living room. The smell: very clean, like cleaning stuff. Was that bleach? I don't know, I can't remember now. All I can remember is the sweat on his back and the smell of beer on his breath.

I run past the underpass, tripping at the corner of Blenheim Road. I'm holding my breath as I walk quickly, looking down. I'm too scared to look up, but when I do, there's nothing to see. There are no vans or police cars outside Scott's house. Could they have finished searching the house already? If they had found something, they would still be there, right? It must take a long time to check everything. I walk faster. When I get to his house, I stop and take a deep breath. The curtains are closed, upstairs and downstairs. The curtains in the neighbor's window move a little. Someone is watching me. I step into the doorway, my hand up. I shouldn't be here. I don't know why I'm here. I just wanted to see. I wanted to know. I'm stuck for a moment, between wanting to leave and wanting to knock on the door. I turn to go, and that's when the door opens.

Before I can move, his hand quickly reaches out. He grabs my arm and pulls me close. His mouth is tight, and his eyes look crazy. He is very desperate. I feel scared and full of energy, and I see bad things about to happen. I try to yell, but it's too late. He pulls me into the house and slams the door.

MEGAN

• • •

THURSDAY, MARCH 21, 2013

MORNING

I don't lose. He should know this about me. I don't lose games like this.

My phone screen is empty. It's stubbornly, rudely empty. No messages, no missed calls. Every time I look at it, it feels like I've been hit, and I get more and more angry. What happened to me in that hotel room? What was I thinking? That we had a real connection, that there was something real between us? He doesn't plan to be with me. But I believed him for a moment—longer than a moment—and that's what makes me really mad. I was silly, too trusting. He was laughing at me the whole time.

If he thinks I'm going to sit around crying about him, he's wrong. I can live without him, I can do fine without him—but I don't like to lose. It's not like me. None of this is like me. I don't get turned down. I'm the one who leaves.

I'm making myself crazy, I can't help it. I can't stop thinking about that afternoon at the hotel and going over and over what he said, how he made me feel.

Jerk.

If he thinks I will just disappear, go away quietly, he's wrong. If he doesn't answer soon, I'm going to stop calling his cell phone and call him at home. I'm not going to be ignored.

At breakfast, Scott asks me to cancel my therapy appointment. I don't answer. I act like I didn't hear him.

"Dave wants us to come for dinner," he says. "We haven't been there in a long time. Can you change your appointment?"

He sounds relaxed, like it's a simple request, but I feel him watching me. His eyes are on my face. We're close to a fight, and I need to be careful.

"I can't, Scott, it's too late," I say. "Why don't you ask Dave and Karen to come here on Saturday instead?" Just thinking about having Dave and Karen over is tiring, but I have to give in a little.

"It's not too late," he says, putting his coffee cup on the table in front of me. He touches my shoulder for a second, says, "Cancel it, OK?" and leaves the room.

As soon as the door closes, I grab the coffee cup and throw it at the wall.

EVENING

I could tell myself that it's not really a rejection. I could try to believe that he's just trying to be good, in a moral and work way. But I know that's not true. Or at least, it's not the whole truth. If you really want someone, morals (and especially work rules) don't matter. You'll do anything to have them. He just doesn't want me that much.

I didn't answer Scott's calls all afternoon. I was late to my appointment and went straight into his office without talking to the receptionist. He was at his desk, writing. He looked up when I came in, didn't smile, and then looked back at his papers. I stood in front of his desk, waiting for him to look at me. It felt like a very long time before he did.

"Are you OK?" he finally asked. He smiled at me then. "You're late."

My breath was stuck in my throat, and I couldn't talk. I walked around the desk and leaned on it, my leg touching his leg. He moved back a little.

"Megan," he said, "are you okay?"

I shook my head. I reached my hand out to him, and he held it.

"Megan," he said again, shaking his head.

I didn't say anything.

"You can't... You should sit down," he said. "Let's talk."

I shook my head.

"Megan."

Every time he said my name, it made things worse.

He stood up and walked around the desk, away from me. He stood in the middle of the room.

"Okay," he said, his voice serious and a little rough. "Sit down."

I went with him to the center of the room. I put one hand on his side and the other on his chest. He held my wrists and stepped back.

"Don't, Megan. You can't... we can't..." He turned away.

"Kamal," I said, my voice shaky. I hated how it sounded. "Please."

"This... here. It's not right. It's normal, I know, but..."

I then told him I wanted to be with him.

"It's just a feeling, Megan," he said. "It happens sometimes. It happens to me too. I should have talked about this last time. I'm sorry."

I wanted to yell. He made it sound so ordinary, so cold, so usual.

"Are you saying you feel nothing?" I asked him. "Are you saying I'm making all this up?"

He shook his head. "You need to know, Megan, I shouldn't have let things go this far."

I moved closer to him, put my hands on his sides, and turned him around. He grabbed my arms again, his long fingers closed around my wrists. “I could lose my job,” he said, and then I got really angry.

I pulled away quickly and angrily. He tried to hold me, but he couldn't. I was shouting at him, telling him I didn't care about his job. He was trying to quiet me down—I guess he was worried about what the receptionist and other patients thought. He grabbed my shoulders, his thumbs pressing into the skin at the top of my arms, and told me to calm down and stop acting like a child. He shook me hard; for a moment, I thought he would hit me.

I kissed him on the mouth and bit his lower lip as hard as I could. I could taste his blood in my mouth. He pushed me away.

On my way home, I planned how to get back at him. I thought of all the things I could do to him. I could get him fired, or something worse. But I won't, because I like him too much. I don't want to hurt him. I'm not even that upset about him rejecting me anymore. What bothers me most is that I haven't finished my story, and I can't start over with someone else; it's too hard.

I don't want to go home now because I don't know how I'm going to explain the bruises on my arms.

RACHEL

• • •

MONDAY, JULY 22, 2013

EVENING

Now I wait. It's awful not knowing what's going to happen. Everything is moving so slowly. But there's nothing else I can do.

I was right this morning when I felt scared. I just didn't know what I should be afraid of.

It wasn't Scott. When he pulled me inside, he must have seen how scared I was. He let go of me right away. He looked wild and messy, like he was trying to hide from the light. He closed the door behind us. "What are you doing here? There are photographers and reporters everywhere. I can't have people coming to the door. Hanging around. They'll say things... They'll try anything to get pictures, to get—"

"There's no one out there," I said, even though I hadn't really looked. There might have been people in cars, waiting for something to happen.

"What are you doing here?" he asked again.

"I heard... it was on the news. I just wanted... is it him? Have they caught him?"

He nodded. “Yes, it was early this morning. The family helper came. She told me. But she couldn't... they won't say why. They must have found something, but they won't say what. It's not her, though. I know they haven't found her.”

He sits on the stairs and hugs himself. His whole body is shaking.

“I can’t take it. I can’t stand waiting for the phone to ring. When it rings, what will it be? Will it be the worst news? Will it be…” He stops talking, then looks up like he's just seeing me. “Why did you come?”

“I wanted… I thought you wouldn’t want to be by yourself.”

He looked at me like I was crazy. “I’m not by myself,” he said. He got up and walked past me into the living room. For a moment, I just stood there. I didn't know if I should follow him or leave, but then he called out, “Do you want coffee?”

There was a woman outside on the grass, smoking. She was tall, with gray hair, and wore nice black pants and a white shirt buttoned up. She was walking back and forth on the patio, but when she saw me, she stopped, threw her cigarette on the ground, and stepped on it.

“Police?” she asked, unsure, as she came into the kitchen.

“No, I’m—”

“This is Rachel Watson, Mum,” Scott said. “The woman who talked to me about Abdic.”

She nodded slowly, like Scott’s words didn’t really help. She looked me up and down quickly. “Oh.”

"I just, um..." I didn't have a good reason to be there. I couldn't say, "I just wanted to know. I wanted to see."

"Well, Scott is very thankful you came. We are waiting to learn what's happening." She walked to me, took my arm, and turned me towards the door. I looked at Scott, but he wasn't looking at me. He was staring out the window.

"Thank you for visiting, Ms. Watson. We are very grateful."

I was on the porch, the door closed behind me. I looked up and saw Tom pushing a stroller, with Anna next to him. They stopped when they saw me. Anna put her hand to her mouth and quickly grabbed her child. Like a lion protecting her baby. I wanted to laugh at her, to say, "I'm not here for you. I don't care about your daughter."

I'm kicked out. Scott's mother made that clear. I'm kicked out and I'm sad, but it shouldn't matter. They have Kamal Abdic. They have him, and I helped. I did something good. They have him, and soon they will find Megan and bring her home.

ANNA

• • •

MONDAY, JULY 22, 2013

MORNING

Tom woke me up early with a kiss and a happy smile. He has a late meeting this morning, so he suggested we take Evie to a nearby place for breakfast. It's a place we used to go when we first started dating. We would sit by the window. She was working in London, so she wouldn't see us. But it was exciting anyway. Maybe she would come home early because she was sick or forgot something important. I wished for it. I wanted her to see him with me and know he wasn't hers anymore. It's strange to think I once wanted her to show up.

Since Megan disappeared, I try not to walk this way. It makes me feel uneasy to pass that house. But it's the only way to get to the cafe. Tom walks ahead of me, pushing the stroller. He's singing to Evie, making her laugh. I love when we're out like this, the three of us. I see how people look at us. They think, What a lovely family. It makes me feel very proud, more than anything else.

I'm feeling very happy, and we're almost at number fifteen when the door opens. For a second, I think I'm seeing things because she walks out. Rachel. She comes out of the front door, sees us, and stops. It's awful. She gives us a strange smile, almost a frown. I can't help it, I quickly grab Evie from the stroller, which scares her. She starts to cry.

Rachel walks away quickly.

Tom calls after her, "Rachel! What are you doing here? Rachel!" But she keeps going, faster and faster, until she's almost running. We just stand there. Then Tom looks at my face and says, "Let's go home."

EVENING

That afternoon, we learned they arrested someone about Megan Hipwell's disappearance. It was a man I didn't know, a therapist she had been seeing. I was relieved because I had been thinking of terrible things.

"I told you it wouldn't be a stranger," Tom said. "It's never a stranger, is it? Anyway, we don't know what happened. She's probably okay. She probably ran off with someone."

"Then why did they arrest that man?"

He shrugged. He was busy putting on his jacket and fixing his tie, getting ready to see his last client of the day.

"What are we going to do?" I asked him.

"Do?" He looked confused.

"About her. Rachel. Why was she here? Why was she at the Hipwells' house? Do you think... do you think she was trying to get into our yard by going through the neighbors' yards?"

Tom laughed. "I doubt it. Come on, it's Rachel. She couldn't climb over all those fences. I don't know why she was there. Maybe she was drunk and went to the wrong house?"

"So, she meant to come here?"

He shook his head. "I don't know. Don't worry about it, okay? Keep the doors locked. I'll call her and find out what she's doing."

"I think we should call the police."

"And say what? She hasn't done anything wrong—"

"She hasn't done anything wrong recently—unless you count that she was here the night Megan Hipwell disappeared," I said. "We should have told the police about her a long time ago."

"Anna, please." He put his arms around my waist. "I don't think Rachel had anything to do with Megan Hipwell's disappearance. But I'll talk to her, okay?"

"But you said after the last time—"

"I know," he said quietly. "I know what I said." He kissed me and put his hand inside my jeans. "Let's not call the police unless we really have to."

I think we do have to. I can't stop thinking about that smile she gave us, that mean look. It was like she had won. We need to leave this place. We need to leave her.

RACHEL

• • •

TUESDAY, JULY 23, 2013

MORNING

When I wake up, it takes me a while to understand what I'm feeling. I have a sudden feeling of great happiness, but it's mixed with something else: a feeling of fear that I can't name. I know we're close to finding the truth. But I can't help feeling that the truth will be awful.

I sit up in bed and grab my laptop. I turn it on and wait impatiently for it to start. Then I log on to the internet. The whole process seems to take forever. I can hear Cathy moving around the house. She's washing her breakfast dishes and then goes upstairs to brush her teeth. She stops for a few seconds outside my door. I imagine her getting ready to knock. But she changes her mind and goes back downstairs.

The BBC news page appears. The main story is about cuts to benefits. The second story is about another TV star from the 1970s who has been accused of sexual misconduct. There's nothing about Megan or Kamal. I'm disappointed. I know that the police have twenty-four hours to charge someone, and that time has passed. But they can hold someone for an extra twelve hours in some cases.

I know all this because I spent yesterday doing research. After I left Scott's house, I came back here. I turned on the TV and spent most of the day watching the news and reading articles online. I was waiting.

By midday, the police had named their suspect. On the news, they talked about "evidence found at Dr. Abdic's home and in his car," but they didn't say what it was. Maybe blood? Or her phone, which hasn't been found yet? Maybe clothes, a bag, or her toothbrush? They kept showing pictures of Kamal, close-ups of his dark, good-looking face. The picture they used wasn't a police photo. It was a casual picture of him on vacation. He's not quite smiling, but almost. He looks too gentle and handsome to be a killer. But looks can be deceiving. They say Ted Bundy looked like Cary Grant.

I waited all day for more news, for the charges to be announced: kidnapping, assault, or something worse. I waited to hear where she is, where he's been keeping her. They showed pictures of Blenheim Road, the train station, and Scott's front door. News reporters talked about what it might mean that Megan's phone and bank cards haven't been used for more than a week.

Tom called many times. I didn't answer. I know what he wants. He wants to know why I was at Scott Hipwell's house yesterday morning. Let him wonder. It's not his business. Not everything is about him. I think he's calling because she asked him to. I don't need to explain anything to her.

I waited a long time, but there was still no charge. Instead, we heard more about Kamal. He was a trusted therapist who listened to Megan's secrets. He gained her trust and then hurt her. He seduced her, and then who knows what else?

I learned that he is a Muslim from Bosnia. He survived the war there and came to Britain as a refugee when he was fifteen. He knows about violence. He lost his father and two older brothers in Srebrenica. He has been convicted of domestic violence. The more I heard about Kamal, the more I knew I was right. I was right to tell the police about him. I was right to contact Scott.

I get up and put on my robe. I hurry downstairs and turn on the TV. I'm not going anywhere today. If Cathy comes home early, I can say I'm sick. I make coffee and sit in front of the TV. I wait.

EVENING

I got bored around three o'clock. I was tired of hearing about welfare and old TV child abusers. I was frustrated that I heard nothing about Megan or Kamal. So, I went to the store and bought two bottles of white wine.

I'm almost done with the first bottle when it happens. There's a new story on the news. It's shaky video from a building that's being built or destroyed. There are explosions in the distance. It's Syria, Egypt, or maybe Sudan. The sound is low, and I'm not really paying attention. Then I see it. The words at the bottom of the screen say that the government is dealing with a challenge to legal aid cuts. Fernando Torres will be out for four weeks with a hamstring injury. And the suspect in Megan Hipwell's disappearance has been released.

I put my glass down and grab the remote. I turn the volume up. This can't be right. The war report keeps going, and my blood pressure goes up with it. Finally, it ends, and they go back to the studio. The newsreader says, "Kamal Abdic, the man arrested yesterday about Megan Hipwell's disappearance, has been released. Abdic, who was Mrs. Hipwell's therapist, was arrested yesterday but was released this morning. The police say they don't have enough evidence to charge him."

I don't hear what she says after that. I just sit there, my eyes blurry, with a lot of noise in my ears. I'm thinking, They had him. They had him, and they let him go.

I'm going upstairs later. I've had too much to drink, and I can't see the computer screen well. Everything looks doubled or tripled. I can read if I cover one eye, but it gives me a headache. Cathy is home. She called to me, and I told her I was in bed and not feeling well. She knows I'm drinking.

My stomach feels full of alcohol. I feel sick and can't think clearly. I shouldn't have started drinking so early, or at all. I called Scott's number an hour ago and again a few minutes ago. I shouldn't have done that either. I just want to know what lies Kamal has told them and what lies they were foolish enough to believe. The police have messed everything up. They're idiots. It's that Riley woman's fault, I'm sure of it.

The newspapers haven't helped. They're saying there was no conviction for domestic violence, which is a mistake. They're making him look like the victim.

I don't want to drink anymore. I know I should pour the rest of the alcohol down the sink. If I don't, it will be there in the morning, and I'll drink it right away. Once I start, I'll want to keep going. I should pour it out, but I know I won't. It's something to look forward to in the morning.

It's dark, and I can hear someone calling her name. A voice, quiet at first, then louder. It sounds angry and desperate, calling Megan's name. It's Scott—he's upset with her. He calls her name again and again. I think it's a dream. I try to hold on to it, but the harder I try, the weaker and further away it gets.

WEDNESDAY, JULY 24, 2013

MORNING

I wake up to a soft knock at the door. Rain is hitting the windows hard. It's after eight, but it still looks dark outside. Cathy gently opens the door and looks into the room.

"Rachel? Are you okay?" She sees the bottle next to my bed, and her shoulders drop. "Oh, Rachel." She walks to my bed and picks up the bottle. I'm too embarrassed to say anything. "Are you not going to work?" she asks. "Did you go yesterday?"

She doesn't wait for me to answer. She turns to leave, saying as she goes, "You'll get fired if you keep doing this."

I should just say it now, she's already mad at me. I should go to her and tell her: I was fired months ago for being very drunk at work. This happened after a three-hour lunch with a client where I was so rude and unprofessional that I lost the company his business. When I close my eyes, I can still remember the end of that lunch, the waitress's face as she gave me my jacket, walking unsteadily into the office, people staring. Martin Miles taking me aside. I think you should go home, Rachel.

There's a loud clap of thunder, a flash of light. I wake up quickly. What did I think about last night? I look in my small notebook, but I haven't written anything since yesterday midday: notes about Kamal—age, race, and a past conviction for hurting someone at home. I take a pen and cross out that last point.

Downstairs, I make coffee and turn on the TV. The police had a press conference last night, and they're showing parts of it on Sky News. Detective Inspector Gaskill is there, looking pale, tired, and ashamed. Sad. He doesn't say Kamal's name, just that a suspect was arrested and questioned, but was let go without charges and that the investigation is still going on. The cameras move away from him to Scott, who is sitting hunched over and uncomfortable, blinking in the camera lights, his face showing pain. It hurts me to see him. He speaks quietly, looking down. He says he hasn't lost hope, that even though the police say, he still believes Megan will come home.

His words sound empty and fake, but without looking at his eyes, I can't tell why. I can't tell if he doesn't believe she's coming home because he's lost all hope, or if he really knows she's never coming home.

Then, I remember calling his number yesterday. Once, twice? I run upstairs to get my phone and find it tangled in the sheets. I have three missed calls: one from Tom and two from Scott. No messages. Tom called last night, and Scott's first call was also last night, but later, just before midnight. His second call was this morning, a few minutes ago.

My heart feels a little lighter. This is good news. Even though his mother told me to leave (Thank you for your help, now go away), Scott still wants to talk to me. He needs me. I suddenly feel affection for Cathy, and I'm grateful she poured the rest of the wine away. I need to think clearly for Scott. He needs me to be thinking straight.

I take a shower, get dressed, and make another coffee. Then, I sit in the living room with my notebook and call Scott.

"You should have told me," he says as soon as he answers, "what you are." His voice is flat and cold. My stomach feels tight. He knows. "Detective Riley talked to me after they let him go. He said he didn't have an affair with her. And the witness who said there was something going on was not reliable, she said. An alcoholic. Maybe crazy. She didn't tell me the witness's name, but I guess she was talking about you?"

"But... no," I say. "No. I wasn't... I hadn't been drinking when I saw them. It was eight thirty in the morning." As if that means anything. "And they found proof, it said so on the news. They found—"

"Not enough proof."

The phone call ends.

FRIDAY, JULY 26, 2013

MORNING

I'm not going to my pretend office anymore. I've stopped trying. I can barely get out of bed. I think I last brushed my teeth on Wednesday. I'm still pretending to be sick, but I don't think anyone believes me.

I don't want to get up, get dressed, get on the train, go to London, and walk around. It's hard enough when it's sunny, but it's impossible in this rain. It's been raining hard and cold for three days now.

I'm having trouble sleeping, and it's not just because of the drinking. I'm having nightmares. I'm stuck somewhere, and I know someone is coming. There's a way out, I know it, I saw it before, but I can't find it. When he gets me, I can't scream. I try to breathe in and out, but no sound comes out, just a rough noise, like someone dying.

Sometimes, in my nightmares, I'm in the underpass by Blenheim Road. The way back is blocked, and I can't go further because someone is there, waiting. I wake up terrified.

They're never going to find her. Every day, every hour that goes by, I'm more sure of it. She'll be one of those names, one of those stories: lost, missing, body never found. And Scott won't get justice or peace. He'll never have a body to mourn; he'll never know what happened to her. There will be no end to it, no answers. I lie awake thinking about it, and it hurts. There's no worse pain than not knowing, and it will never end.

I wrote to him. I told him about my problem, then I lied again, saying I was getting it under control and getting help. I told him I'm not crazy. I don't know if that's true anymore. I told him I was very sure about what I saw, and that I wasn't drinking when I saw it. That, at least, is true. He hasn't answered. I didn't expect him to. I'm cut off from him, shut out. I can't say the things I want to say to him. I can't write them down; they don't sound right. I want him to know how sorry I am that it wasn't enough to point at Kamal and say, Look, there he is. I should have seen something. That Saturday night, I should have been paying attention.

EVENING

I'm completely wet and very cold. My fingertips are pale and wrinkly, and my head is pounding from a hangover that started around 5:30. That makes sense since I began drinking before noon. I went to get another bottle, but the ATM stopped me, saying: You don't have enough money in your account.

After that, I started walking. I walked without a goal for over an hour in the heavy rain. The empty walking area of Ashbury was all mine. During that walk, I decided I have to do something. I have to fix my mistakes.

Now, soaked and almost sober, I'm going to call Tom. I don't want to know what I did or said on Saturday night, but I need to find out. It might help me remember something. For some reason, I'm sure I'm missing something important. Maybe this is just another lie I'm telling myself to feel like I'm not worthless. But maybe it's real.

"I've been trying to reach you since Monday," Tom says when he answers. "I called your work," he adds, letting that sink in.

I'm already feeling bad, embarrassed, and ashamed. "I need to talk to you," I say, "about Saturday night. That Saturday night."

"What are you talking about? I need to talk to you about Monday, Rachel. What were you doing at Scott Hipwell's house?"

"That's not important, Tom—"

"Yes, it is. What were you doing there? You know he could have... we don't know, do we? He could have hurt her. His wife."

"He didn't hurt his wife," I say, sure of myself. "It wasn't him."

"How do you know? Rachel, what's going on?"

"I just... You have to believe me. That's not why I called. I needed to talk about Saturday. About the message you sent. You were so mad. You said I scared Anna."

"Well, you did. She saw you walking unsteadily down the street, and you yelled at her. She was really scared, after what happened before. With Evie."

"Did she... did she do something?"

"Do something?"

"To me?"

"What?"

"I had a cut, Tom. On my head. I was bleeding."

"Are you saying Anna hurt you?" He's yelling now, he's very angry. "Seriously, Rachel. That's enough! I've talked Anna out of going to the police about you before, but if you keep doing this—bothering us, making up stories—"

"I'm not saying she did anything, Tom. I'm just trying to understand. I don't—"

"You don't remember! Of course not. Rachel doesn't remember." He sighs, sounding tired. "Look. Anna saw you—you were drunk and yelling. She came home to tell me, she was upset, so I went to find you. You were in the street. I think you might have fallen. You were very upset. You had cut your hand."

“I didn’t—”

“Well, you had blood on your hand. I don’t know how it got there. I said I would take you home, but you wouldn’t listen. You were acting crazy and not making sense. You walked away, and I went to get the car. But when I came back, you were gone. I drove past the train station, but I didn’t see you. I drove around some more. Anna was very worried that you were nearby, that you would come back, that you would try to get into the house. I was worried you would fall or get hurt. I drove all the way to Ashbury. I rang the doorbell, but you weren’t there. I called you a few times. I left a message. And yes, I was angry. I was really mad by then.”

“I’m sorry, Tom,” I say. “I’m really sorry.”

“I know,” he says. “You’re always sorry.”

“You said I yelled at Anna,” I say, feeling bad about it. “What did I say to her?”

“I don’t know,” he says quickly. “Do you want me to go get her? Maybe you want to talk to her about it?”

“Tom…”

“Well, really—why does it matter now?”

“Did you see Megan Hipwell that night?”

“No.” He sounds worried now. “Why? Did you? You didn’t do something, did you?”

"No, of course not."

He pauses. "Then why are you asking? Rachel, if you know something..."

"I don't know anything," I say. "I didn't see anything."

"Why were you at the Hipwells' house on Monday? Please tell me so I can calm Anna down. She's worried."

"I had something to tell him. Something I thought might help."

"You didn't see her, but you had something helpful to tell him?"

I pause. I'm not sure how much to say, if I should keep this just for Scott. "It's about Megan," I say. "She was having an affair."

"Wait—did you know her?"

"Just a little," I say.

"How?"

"From her art place."

"Oh," he says. "So, who is that man?"

"Her doctor," I tell him. "Kamal Abdic. I saw them together."

"Really? The man they caught? I thought they let him go."

"They did. And it's my fault, because I'm not a good witness."

Tom laughs. It's quiet and kind, he's not making fun of me. "Rachel, come on. You did the right thing by speaking up. I'm sure it's not just about you." I can hear a child talking in the background, and Tom says something away from the phone that I can't hear. "I have to go," he says. I can picture him putting down the phone, picking up his little girl, kissing her, and hugging his wife. It hurts me deeply.

MONDAY, JULY 29, 2013

MORNING

It's 8:07 and I'm on the train. Back to the fake office. Cathy was with Damien all weekend, and when I saw her last night, I didn't let her yell at me. I started saying sorry for how I acted right away. I said I was feeling very sad, but that I was getting better and starting over. She accepted, or acted like she accepted, my apologies. She hugged me. She was being very nice.

Megan is almost never in the news anymore. There was an article in the Sunday Times about the police not doing a good job. It mentioned the case briefly. Someone at the Crown Prosecution Service said it was "one of many cases where the police arrested someone too quickly based on weak proof."

We're reaching the signal. I feel the familiar shake and bump as the train slows down. I look up because I can't help it, but there's never anything to see anymore. The doors are closed and the curtains are shut. All I see is rain, lots of it, and muddy water collecting at the bottom of the garden.

I decide to get off the train at Witney. Tom couldn't help me, but maybe the other man could—the one with red hair. I wait for the other people to leave the train and then I sit on the only bench with a roof on the platform. I might get lucky. I might see him getting on the train. I could follow him and talk to him. It's the only thing I have left, my last chance. If this doesn't work, I have to give up. I just have to let it go.

Half an hour passes. Every time I hear footsteps on the stairs, my heart beats faster. Every time I hear the click of high heels, I get worried. If Anna sees me here, I could be in trouble. Tom warned me. He's convinced her not to call the police, but if I keep going...

It's 9:15. Unless he starts work very late, I've missed him. It's raining harder now, and I don't want another pointless day in London. I only have ten pounds that I borrowed from Cathy, and I need to make it last until I'm brave enough to ask my mother for money. I walk down the stairs, planning to go under the tracks to the other platform and go back to Ashbury. But then I see Scott rushing out of the newsagent across from the station, his coat pulled up around his face.

I run after him and catch him at the corner, right by the underpass. I grab his arm and he turns around, surprised.

"Please," I say, "can I talk to you?"

"Jesus Christ," he says angrily. "What do you want?"

I step back, holding my hands up. "I'm sorry," I say. "I'm sorry. I just wanted to apologize, to explain..."

The rain is pouring down. We're the only people on the street, both of us completely soaked. Scott starts to laugh. He throws his hands up and laughs loudly. "Come to the house," he says. "We're going to drown out here."

Scott goes upstairs to get me a towel while the kettle boils. The house is messier than it was a week ago. The cleaning smell is gone, replaced by a more natural smell. There's a pile of newspapers in the corner of the living room, and there are dirty mugs on the coffee table and the fireplace.

Scott comes to my side and offers me the towel. “I know it's a mess. My mom was making me crazy, always cleaning and picking up after me. We argued. She hasn't visited for a few days.” His phone rings, he looks at it, then puts it away. “Just like her. She never stops calling.”

I go with him to the kitchen.

“I’m very sorry about what happened,” I say.

He shrugs. “I know. It’s not your fault. But it might have been better if you weren’t…”

“If I wasn’t drunk?”

He turns away and makes coffee.

“Yes, maybe. But they didn't have enough proof to charge him anyway.” He gives me the coffee and we sit at the table. I see that a picture frame on the side table is turned over. Scott keeps talking. “They found things—hair, skin—in his house, but he says she was there. He first said she wasn't, then said she was.”

“Why did he lie?”

“Exactly. He said she came to his house twice, just to talk. He won't say what about—it's private. The hair and skin were downstairs. Nothing in the bedroom. He says they weren't having an affair. But he's a liar, so…” He rubs his eyes. He looks tired and sad. “They found a little blood in his car.”

“Oh my God.”

"Yes. It matches her blood type. They don't know if they can get any DNA because the sample is so small. They keep saying it might not mean anything. How can it mean nothing when her blood is on his car?" He shakes his head. "You were right. The more I learn about this guy, the more sure I am." He looks right at me, for the first time since we arrived. "He was sleeping with her, and she wanted to stop, so he... he did something. That's it. I'm sure of it."

He's lost all hope, and I understand why. It's been over two weeks, and she hasn't turned on her phone, used a credit card, or taken money from an ATM. No one has seen her. She's gone.

"He told the police she might have run away," Scott says.

"Dr. Abdic did?"

Scott nods. "He told the police she was unhappy with me and might have left."

"He's trying to make them think you did something."

"I know. But they seem to believe everything that jerk says. That Riley woman, I can tell she likes him when she talks about him. The poor, mistreated refugee." He looks down, miserable. "Maybe he's right. We did have that bad fight. But I can't believe... She wasn't unhappy with me. She wasn't. She wasn't." When he says it the third time, I wonder if he's trying to convince himself. "But if she was having an affair, she must have been unhappy, right?"

"Not necessarily," I say. "Maybe it was one of those—what do they call it?—transference things. That's the word, isn't it? When a patient starts to have feelings—or thinks they have feelings—for a therapist. But the therapist is supposed to resist them and say the feelings aren't real."

His eyes are on my face, but I don't think he's really listening to me.

"What happened?" he asks. "With you. You left your husband. Was there someone else?"

I shake my head. "It's the other way around. Anna came after."

"Sorry." He stops talking for a moment.

I know what he will ask. Before he can, I say, "It started before. When we were still married. The drinking. That's what you wanted to know, right?"

He nods again.

"We were trying to have a baby," I say, and my voice breaks. Even now, after so long, I get tears in my eyes when I talk about it. "Sorry."

"It's okay." He stands up, walks to the sink, and pours me a glass of water. He puts it on the table in front of me.

I clear my throat and try to speak calmly. "We wanted a baby, but it didn't happen. I got very sad, and I started to drink. I was very hard to live with, so Tom found comfort with someone else. And she was happy to be that person."

"I'm very sorry, that's terrible. I know... I wanted a child. Megan kept saying she wasn't ready yet." Now he wipes away his tears. "It's one of the things... we argued about it sometimes."

"Was that what you argued about the day she left?"

He sighs, pushes his chair back, and stands up. "No," he says, turning away from me. "It was something different."

EVENING

Cathy is waiting when I get home. She's in the kitchen, quickly drinking water.

"Did you have a good day at work?" she asks, tightening her lips. She knows the truth.

"Cathy..."

"Damien had a meeting near Euston today. On his way out, he saw Martin Miles. They know each other a bit, from when Damien worked at Laing Fund Management. Martin used to handle their public relations."

"Cathy..."

She raised her hand, took another drink of water. "You haven't worked there in months! Do you know how stupid I feel? How stupid Damien felt? Please, tell me you have another job you haven't told me about. Please tell me you haven't been pretending to go to work. That you haven't been lying to me every day."

"I didn't know how to tell you..."

"You didn't know how to tell me? How about: 'Cathy, I lost my job because I was drunk at work'? How about that?" I move back a little, and her face softens. "I'm sorry, but really, Rachel." She's too kind. "What have you been doing? Where do you go? What do you do all day?"

"I walk. I go to the library. Sometimes—"

"You go to the bar?"

"Sometimes. But—"

"Why didn't you tell me?" She comes closer, putting her hands on my shoulders. "You should have told me."

"I was embarrassed," I say, and I start to cry. It's terrible, awkward, but I start to weep. I cry and cry, and kind Cathy holds me, pets my hair, tells me I'll be okay, that everything will be okay. I feel awful. I hate myself almost more than ever.

Later, sitting on the couch with Cathy, drinking tea, she tells me what I need to do. I'm going to stop drinking, I'm going to fix my resume, I'm going to call Martin Miles and ask for a reference. I'm going to stop wasting money going back and forth to London on useless train trips.

"Honestly, Rachel, I don't understand how you could have done this for so long."

I shrug. "In the morning, I take the 8:04, and in the evening, I come back on the 5:56. That's my train. It's the one I take. That's just how it is."

THURSDAY, AUGUST 1, 2013

MORNING

Something is on my face, I can't breathe, I'm choking. When I wake up, I'm struggling for air and my chest hurts. I sit up, eyes wide, and see something moving in the corner of the room, a dark spot that keeps getting bigger, and I almost shout—and then I'm fully awake and there's nothing there, but I am sitting up in bed and my face is wet with tears.

It's almost morning. The outside light is starting to turn gray, and the rain from the past few days is still hitting the window. I can't go back to sleep. My heart is beating so hard it hurts.

I think there might be some wine downstairs, but I'm not sure. I don't remember finishing the second bottle. It will be warm because I can't leave it in the fridge. If I do, Cathy throws it away. She really wants me to get better, but it's not working out the way she planned. There's a small cupboard in the hallway where the gas meter is. If there was any wine left, I hid it there.

I sneak out onto the landing and walk quietly down the stairs in the dim light. I open the small cupboard and take out the bottle. It's disappointingly light, with only about a glassful left. But it's better than nothing. I pour it into a mug (in case Cathy comes down, I can pretend it's tea) and put the bottle in the trash (making sure to hide it under a milk carton and a chip bag). In the living room, I turn on the TV, immediately mute it, and sit on the sofa.

I'm flipping through channels. It's all kids' shows and commercials until I suddenly recognize Corly Wood. It's just down the road from here. You can see it from the train. Corly Wood is in heavy rain, and the fields between the trees and train tracks are flooded.

I don't know why it takes me so long to understand what's happening. For ten, fifteen, twenty seconds, I'm looking at cars, blue and white tape, and a white tent in the background. My breath gets shorter and shorter until I'm holding it and not breathing at all.

It's her. She's been in the wood the whole time, right by the railway track. I've been past those fields every day, morning and evening, not knowing.

In the wood. I imagine a grave dug under messy bushes, quickly covered up. I imagine worse things, impossible things—her body hanging from a rope, somewhere deep in the forest where no one goes.

It might not even be her. It might be something else.

I know it isn't something else.

There's a reporter on the screen now, with dark hair slicked down. I turn up the volume and listen to him tell me what I already know, what I can feel—that it wasn't me who couldn't breathe, it was Megan.

"That's right," he says, talking to someone in the studio, his hand pressed to his ear. "The police have now said that the body of a young woman was found in floodwater in a field at the bottom of Corly Wood. This is less than five miles from Megan Hipwell's house. Mrs. Hipwell went missing in early July—the thirteenth of July—and has not been seen since. The police say that the body, found by dog walkers this morning, has not been officially identified. However, they think it is Megan. Mrs. Hipwell’s husband has been told."

He stops talking for a moment. The news anchor asks him a question, but I can't hear it because my ears are ringing. I drink the rest of my mug.

The reporter speaks again. "Yes, Kay, that's right. It seems the body was buried in the woods, maybe for a while. The heavy rains we've had recently uncovered it."

It's worse than I thought. I picture her now, her ruined face in the mud, pale arms showing, reaching up as if she's trying to get out of the grave. I taste a hot, bitter liquid in my mouth, and I run upstairs to throw up.

EVENING

I stayed in bed most of the day. I tried to think clearly. I tried to remember what happened on Saturday night from my memories, flashbacks, and dreams. To understand it, I wrote it all down. The sound of my pen on paper felt like someone whispering to me. It made me nervous, like someone else was in the apartment, just outside the door. I kept imagining her.

I was scared to open the bedroom door, but when I did, no one was there. I went downstairs and turned on the TV again. The same pictures were still there: the woods in the rain, police cars on a muddy path, that awful white tent. It was all blurry and grey. Then suddenly, there was Megan, smiling at the camera, still beautiful. Then it was Scott, head down, trying to get past photographers at his front door, with Riley next to him. Then it was Kamal's office. He wasn't there.

I didn't want to hear the news, but I turned the volume up to stop the silence in my ears. The police say the woman, not yet officially identified, has been dead for some time, maybe several weeks. They say they don't know how she died. They say there's no sign of a sexual reason for the killing.

That seems like a silly thing to say. I know they mean they don't think she was raped, which is good, but that doesn't mean there wasn't a sexual reason. It seems like Kamal wanted her and couldn't have her. She must have tried to stop it, and he couldn't handle it. That's a sexual reason, isn't it?

I can't watch the news anymore, so I go back upstairs and get under my covers. I empty my purse, looking at my notes on scraps of paper. They're all the bits of information I've found. The memories shift like shadows, and I wonder, Why am I doing this? What's the point?

Okay, I understand. Please provide the text you would like me to refine.

MEGAN

• • •

THURSDAY, JUNE 13, 2013

MORNING

I can't sleep because it's so hot. It feels like tiny bugs are crawling on my skin, I have a rash on my chest, and I can't get comfortable. Scott feels very warm; being next to him is like being next to a fire. I need to get away from him and end up on the edge of the bed, with the sheets pulled back. It's too much. I thought about sleeping on the futon in the other room, but he doesn't like waking up and finding me gone. It always starts an argument about something. Usually, it's about what else the spare room could be used for, or who I was thinking about while I was alone there. Sometimes I want to yell at him, Just let me go. Let me go. Let me breathe. So I can't sleep, and I'm angry. I feel like we're already fighting, even though the fight is only in my head.

And in my head, thoughts keep going around and around.

I feel like I can't breathe.

When did this house become so small? When did my life become so boring? Is this what I really wanted? I don't remember. I just know that a few months ago I felt better, and now I can't think, I can't sleep, I can't draw, and I really want to run away. At night when I'm awake, I can hear it, quiet but constant, clear: a whisper in my head, Get away. When I close my eyes, I see images of past and future lives, the things I wanted, the things I had and gave up. I can't get comfortable, because every way I turn, I find problems: the closed art place, the houses on this street, the annoying women at Pilates, the train tracks at the end of the yard with its trains, always taking someone else somewhere else, reminding me again and again, many times a day, that I'm stuck here.

I feel like I'm going crazy.

But just a few months ago, I was feeling better, I was getting better. I was okay. I was sleeping. I wasn't afraid of nightmares. I could breathe. Yes, I still wanted to run away. Sometimes. But not every day.

Talking to Kamal helped me, that's clear. I liked it. I liked him. He made me happier. Now it all feels unfinished—I never got to the main point. It's my fault, because I acted childishly, because I didn't like being rejected. I need to learn to handle losing better. I'm embarrassed now, ashamed. My face gets hot when I think about it. I don't want that to be his last thought of me. I want him to see me again, to see me in a better way. I feel that if I went to him, he would help. He's like that.

I need to finish this story. I need to tell someone, just once. Say the words out loud. If I don't, it will hurt me. The empty feeling inside me, the one they left, will just get bigger until it takes over.

I'm going to have to forget my pride and shame and go to him. He's going to have to listen. I'll make him.

EVENING

Scott thinks I'm at the movies with Tara. I've been outside Kamal's apartment for fifteen minutes, trying to get ready to knock. I'm so scared of how he'll look at me, after last time. I have to show him that I'm sorry, so I've dressed simply: jeans and a T-shirt, hardly any makeup. This isn't about trying to attract him, he has to see that.
