My heart starts to beat fast as I walk to his door and ring the bell. No one answers. The lights are on, but no one comes. Maybe he saw me outside; maybe he's upstairs, hoping I'll go away if he ignores me. I won't. He doesn't know how determined I can be. Once I decide something, I don't give up.

I ring again, and then a third time, and finally I hear footsteps on the stairs and the door opens. He's wearing sweatpants and a white T-shirt. He's barefoot, his hair is wet, and his face is red.

"Megan." He's surprised, but not angry, which is good. "Are you okay? Is everything okay?"

"I'm sorry," I say, and he steps back to let me in. I feel so grateful, it feels almost like love.

He leads me into the kitchen. It's a mess: dirty dishes piled up, empty food containers overflowing from the trash. I wonder if he's sad. I stand in the doorway; he leans against the counter, his arms crossed.

"How can I help?" he asks. His face is very still, like he's putting on his therapist face. It makes me want to poke him, just to make him smile.

"I need to tell you something..." I begin, then I stop. I can't just jump right into it; I need to start slowly. So I try something else. "I wanted to say sorry," I say, "for what happened. Last time."

"It's okay," he says. "Don't worry about it. If you need to talk, I can send you to someone else, but I can't—"

"Please, Kamal."

"Megan, I can't be your therapist anymore."

"I know. I know that. But I can't start over with someone new. I just can't. We made so much progress. We were so close. I just need to tell you. Just once. And then I'll leave, I promise. I won't bother you again."

He tilts his head. I can tell he doesn't believe me. He thinks if he lets me in now, I'll never leave him alone.

"Please just listen. This won't take long, I just need someone to hear me."

"Your husband?" he asks, and I shake my head.

"I can't—I can't tell him. Not after all this time. He wouldn't... He wouldn't see me the same way. I'd be different to him. He wouldn't know how to forgive me. Please, Kamal. If I don't say this out loud, I feel like I'll never sleep. As a friend, not a therapist, please listen."

His shoulders slump as he turns away, and I think it's over. My heart drops. Then he opens a cupboard and takes out two glasses.

"As a friend, then. Would you like some wine?"

He leads me into the living room. It's dimly lit by lamps and looks as messy as the kitchen. We sit on opposite sides of a glass table covered with papers, magazines, and takeout menus. My hands are tight around my glass. I take a sip. The wine is red but cold and tastes old. I swallow and take another sip. He's waiting for me to start talking, but it's hard, harder than I thought. I've kept this secret for so long—ten years, more than a third of my life. It's not easy to let it go. I know I have to start talking. If I don't do it now, I might never have the courage to say the words. They might get stuck in my throat and choke me in my sleep.

"After I left Ipswich, I moved in with Mac. His cottage was outside Holkham, at the end of the road. I told you that, right? It was very lonely, a couple of miles to the closest neighbor, and a couple more to the nearest shops. At first, we had lots of parties. People would crash in the living room or sleep in the hammock outside in the summer. But we got tired of that, and Mac argued with everyone, so people stopped coming. It was just the two of us. Days would go by without us seeing anyone. We'd buy groceries at the gas station. It's strange to think about it now, but I needed it then, after everything—after Ipswich and all those men, all the things I did. I liked it, just Mac and me, the old train tracks, the grass, the dunes, and the restless gray sea."

Kamal tilts his head and gives me a small smile. I feel nervous. "It sounds nice. But are you making it sound better than it was? 'The restless gray sea'?"

"Never mind that," I say, waving him off. "And no, I'm not. Have you been to north Norfolk? It's not the Mediterranean. It is restless and always gray."

He holds up his hands, smiling. "Okay."

I feel better right away, the tension leaving my neck and shoulders. I take another sip of the wine; it tastes less bitter now.

"I was happy with Mac. I know it doesn't sound like the kind of place or life I'd want, but after Ben's death and everything that happened, it was. Mac saved me. He took me in, he loved me, he kept me safe. And he wasn't boring. And honestly, we were taking a lot of drugs, and it's hard to be bored when you're high all the time. I was happy. I was really happy."

Kamal nods. "I understand, but I'm not sure that sounds like a very real kind of happiness," he says. "Not the kind of happiness that lasts, that can support you."

I laugh. “I was seventeen. I was with a man who made me excited, who loved me. I had left my parents, left the house where everything reminded me of my dead brother. I didn’t need it to last. I just needed it for that moment.”

“So what happened?”

The room seems to get darker. Here we are, at the thing I never talk about.

“I got pregnant.”

He nods, waiting for me to continue. Part of me wants him to stop me, to ask more questions, but he doesn’t. He just waits. It gets even darker.

“It was too late when I realized I should have gotten rid of it. Of her. That’s what I would have done if I hadn’t been so stupid, so unaware. The truth is that neither of us wanted her.”

Kamal stands up, goes to the kitchen, and comes back with a paper towel for me to wipe my eyes. He gives it to me and sits down. It’s a while before I go on. Kamal sits, just like he used to in our sessions, looking at me, his hands in his lap, patient and still. It must take a lot of self-control to be so still and quiet. It must be tiring.

My legs are shaking, my knee jerking like it’s being controlled by a string. I stand up to stop it. I walk to the kitchen door and back, scratching my hands.

“We were both so stupid,” I tell him. “We didn’t even really notice what was happening. We just kept going. I didn’t see a doctor, I didn’t eat healthy food or take vitamins. I didn’t do any of the things you’re supposed to. We just kept living our lives. We didn’t even admit that anything had changed. I got bigger, slower, and more tired. We both got annoyed and argued all the time, but nothing really changed until she was born.”

He lets me cry. While I cry, he moves to the chair closest to me and sits down next to me, so his knees are almost touching my leg. He leans forward. He doesn’t touch me, but we are close. I can smell his scent, clean in this dirty room, sharp and fresh.

My voice is very quiet, and it feels strange to say these words out loud. "I had her at home," I say. "It was a bad idea, but I was afraid of hospitals at the time. The last time I was in one was when Ben died. Also, I didn't go to any of the checkups. I was smoking and drinking a little, and I didn't want to hear any lectures. I couldn't deal with any of it. I think... until the very end, it didn't feel real, like it was actually happening."

"Mac had a friend who was a nurse, or had some nursing training. She came over, and it was okay. It wasn't too bad. I mean, it was awful, of course, painful and scary, but... then she was there. She was very small. I don't remember exactly how much she weighed. That's terrible, isn't it?" Kamal doesn't say anything or move. "She was lovely. She had dark eyes and blond hair. She didn't cry much, and she slept well from the start. She was good. She was a good girl." I have to stop for a moment. "I thought it would be so hard, but it wasn't."

It's even darker now, I'm sure of it, but I look up and Kamal is there, looking at me with a gentle expression. He's listening. He wants me to tell him. My mouth is dry, so I take another sip of wine. It hurts to swallow. "We named her Elizabeth. Libby." It feels so weird to say her name out loud after so long. "Libby," I say again, liking the way her name feels in my mouth. I want to say it again and again. Kamal finally reaches out and takes my hand, his thumb on my wrist, feeling my pulse.

"One day, Mac and I had a fight. I don't remember what it was about. We did that sometimes—small arguments that became big ones. It wasn't physical or anything bad like that, but we'd yell at each other, and I'd threaten to leave, or he'd just walk out, and I wouldn't see him for a couple of days."

"It was the first time that had happened since she was born—the first time he'd just left me. She was only a few months old. The roof was leaking. I remember the sound of water dripping into buckets in the kitchen. It was freezing cold, with the wind coming off the sea. It had been raining for days. I started a fire in the living room, but it kept going out. I was so tired. I was drinking just to get warm, but it wasn't working, so I decided to take a bath. I took Libby in with me, put her on my chest, her head just under my chin."

The room gets darker and darker until I'm back there, lying in the water, her body against mine, a candle flickering behind my head. I can hear it sputtering, smell the wax, and feel the cold air on my neck and shoulders. I feel heavy, my body sinking into the warm water. I'm exhausted. Then suddenly, the candle goes out, and I'm cold. Really cold, my teeth chattering, my whole body shaking. The house feels like it's shaking too, with the wind screaming and tearing at the roof.

"I fell asleep," I say, and then I can't say anything more because I can feel her again, no longer on my chest, her body stuck between my arm and the side of the tub, her face in the water. We were both so cold.

For a moment, neither of us moves. I can barely look at him, but when I do, he doesn't pull away from me. He doesn't say a word. He puts his arm around my shoulder and pulls me close, my face against his chest. I breathe him in and wait to feel different, to feel lighter, to feel better or worse now that someone else knows. I feel relieved, I think, because I know from his reaction that I did the right thing. He isn't angry with me, and he doesn't think I'm a monster. I am safe here, completely safe with him.

I don't know how long I stay in his arms, but when I come back to myself, my phone is ringing. I don't answer it, but a moment later, it beeps to tell me I have a text. It's from Scott. Where are you? And seconds after that, the phone starts ringing again. This time it's Tara. I pull away from Kamal and answer.

"Megan, I don't know what you're doing, but you need to call Scott. He's called here four times. I told him you went out to get some wine, but I don't think he believed me. He says you're not answering your phone." She sounds annoyed, and I know I should calm her down, but I don't have the energy.

"Okay," I say. "Thanks. I'll call him now."

"Megan—" she says, but I hang up before I hear anything else.

It's after ten. I've been here for over two hours. I turn off my phone and look at Kamal.

"I don't want to go home," I say.

He nods, but he doesn't ask me to stay. Instead, he says, "You can come back, if you want. Another time."

I move closer, stand on my toes, and kiss him. He doesn't move away.

RACHEL

• • •

SATURDAY, AUGUST 3, 2013

MORNING

Last night, I dreamed I was in the woods, walking alone. It was either dusk or dawn, I'm not sure, but someone else was there. I couldn't see them, but I knew they were getting closer. I didn't want them to see me. I wanted to run, but my legs felt too heavy. When I tried to shout, no sound came out.

When I wake up, bright light comes through the blinds. The rain has stopped. The room is warm and smells bad, stale and sour. I've barely left it since Thursday. Outside, I hear the vacuum cleaner. Cathy is cleaning. She'll go out later, and then I can leave the room. I don't know what I'll do. I can't seem to get myself together. Maybe I'll drink for one more day, and then I'll get better tomorrow.

My phone buzzes, telling me the battery is low. I pick it up to plug it in and see I missed two calls last night. I check my voicemail. I have one message.

"Rachel, hi. It's Mum. I'm coming to London tomorrow, Saturday. I need to do some shopping. Could we meet for coffee? Sweetie, it's not a good time for you to stay here now. There's... well, I have a new friend, and you know how it is at the start." She giggles. "Anyway, I'm happy to lend you money for a couple of weeks. We'll talk tomorrow. Okay, sweetie. Bye."

I need to be honest with her and tell her how bad things are. I don't want to have that talk when I'm completely sober. I get out of bed. I can go to the shops now and have a couple of drinks before I go out. That will calm me down. I look at my phone again. Only one missed call is from my mother. The other is from Scott. He left a message at 12:45 AM. I sit there, holding the phone, wondering if I should call him back. Not now, it's too early. Maybe later? After one drink, not two.

I plug the phone in to charge, open the blinds and the window, then go to the bathroom and take a cold shower. I wash myself and my hair and try to ignore the voice in my head that says it's strange to call another woman in the middle of the night, less than two days after your wife's body was found.

EVENING

The ground is still drying, but the sun is almost coming through the thick white clouds. I bought a small bottle of wine—just one. I shouldn't, but lunch with my mother would be hard for anyone to resist. Still, she promised to put £300 into my bank account, so it wasn't a total waste of time.

I didn't say how bad things were. I didn't tell her I've been jobless for months, or that I was fired (she thinks her money is helping me until my unemployment money comes). I didn't tell her how much I've been drinking, and she didn't notice. Cathy did. When I saw her this morning, she looked at me and said, "Oh my God. Already?" I don't know how she does it, but she always knows. Even if I've only had a little bit, she knows just by looking at me.

"I can tell by your eyes," she says, but when I look in the mirror, I look the same. She's losing patience and sympathy. I have to stop. But not today. I can't today. It's too hard today.

I should have been ready for it, should have expected it, but I didn't. I got on the train, and she was everywhere. Her happy face was on every newspaper: beautiful, blond Megan, looking right at the camera, right at me.

Someone left their Times newspaper, so I read the story. They officially identified her last night, and the examination is today. A police officer said that "Mrs. Hipwell's cause of death might be hard to find because her body has been outside for a while and underwater for several days." It's awful to think about, with her picture right in front of me. What she looked like then, what she looks like now.

There's a short mention of Kamal, his arrest and release, and a statement from Detective Inspector Gaskill, saying they are "looking at many possibilities," which I think means they don't know anything. I close the newspaper and put it on the floor. I can't look at her anymore. I don't want to read those sad, empty words.

I lean my head against the window. Soon we'll pass number twenty-three. I look over for a second, but we're too far away to see anything. I keep thinking about the day I saw Kamal, how he kissed her, how angry I was, and how I wanted to yell at her. What would have happened if I had? What if I had gone there, knocked on the door, and asked her what she was doing? Would she still be out there on her porch?

I close my eyes. At Northcote, someone sits next to me. I don't open my eyes, but it's strange because the train is almost empty. The hairs on my neck are standing up. I smell aftershave and cigarette smoke, and I know I've smelled that before.

"Hello."

I look around and see the man with red hair, the one from the station, from that Saturday. He's smiling at me and offering his hand. I'm so surprised that I shake it. His hand feels rough and hard.

"Do you remember me?"

"Yes," I say, shaking my head. "Yes, a few weeks ago, at the station."

He nods and smiles. "I was a bit drunk," he says, then laughs. "I think you were too, weren't you?"

He's younger than I thought, maybe in his late twenties. He has a kind face, not handsome, just nice. Open, with a big smile. His accent is Cockney, or something like that. He's looking at me like he knows something about me, like he's teasing me, like we have a secret joke. We don't. I look away. I should say something, ask him, What did you see?

"Are you okay?" he asks.

"Yes, I'm fine." I look out the window again, but I can feel him looking at me. I have a strange urge to turn to him, to smell the smoke on his clothes and breath. I like the smell of cigarettes. Tom used to smoke when we first met. I would sometimes have one with him when we were out drinking or after sex. That smell is exciting to me; it reminds me of being happy. I bite my lip, wondering what he would do if I turned and kissed him. I feel him move. He leans forward, bends down, and picks up the newspaper at my feet.

"Awful, isn't it? Poor girl. It's weird, because we were there that night. It was that night, wasn't it? That she went missing?"

It's like he read my mind, and it shocks me. I quickly turn to look at him. I want to see his eyes. "I'm sorry?"

"That night when I met you on the train. That was the night that girl went missing, the one they just found. And they're saying the last time anyone saw her was outside the station. I keep thinking that I might have seen her. I don't remember, though. I was drunk." He shrugs. "You don't remember anything, do you?"

It's strange how I feel when he says that. I don't think I've ever felt this way before. I can't answer because my mind is somewhere else. It's not his words, it's his aftershave. That smell—fresh, lemony, and strong—makes me remember sitting next to him on the train. We were going the other way, and someone was laughing loudly. He has his hand on my arm, asking if I want a drink, but suddenly something feels wrong. I'm scared and confused. Someone is trying to hit me. I see a fist coming and I duck, putting my hands up to protect my head. I'm not on the train anymore, I'm on the street. I hear laughter or shouting again. I'm on the steps, then on the sidewalk. It's all confusing, and my heart is racing. I don't want to be near this man. I need to get away.

I quickly stand up, saying "Excuse me" loudly so others will hear, but there are hardly any people here, and no one looks. The man looks surprised and moves his legs so I can pass.

"Sorry," he says. "I didn't mean to upset you."

I walk away as fast as I can, but the train shakes, and I almost fall. I grab a seat to keep from falling. People are staring at me. I hurry to the next train car and then the one after that. I keep going until I reach the end of the train. I feel out of breath and scared. I can't explain it, and I can't remember what happened, but I feel the fear and confusion. I sit down, facing the way I came, so I can see him if he follows me.

I press my hands into my eyes and try to focus. I'm trying to remember what I just saw. I'm mad at myself for drinking. If only my head was clear... but there it is. It's dark, and a man is walking away from me. Or is it a woman? A woman in a blue dress. It's Anna.

My head is pounding, and my heart is racing. I don't know if what I'm seeing and feeling is real, or just my imagination or a memory. I close my eyes tightly and try to feel it and see it again, but it's gone.

ANNA

• • •

SATURDAY, AUGUST 3, 2013

EVENING

Tom is out with his army friends, and Evie is napping. I'm in the kitchen with all the doors and windows shut, even though it's hot. The rain from last week has finally stopped, and now it's very stuffy.

I'm bored and can't think of anything to do. I want to go shopping and buy something for myself, but it's impossible with Evie. She gets fussy, and I get stressed. So, I'm just stuck at home. I don't want to watch TV or read the news. I don't want to read about it, see Megan's face, or think about it.

How can I not think about it when we're so close, just four doors away?

I called some people to see if they wanted to have a playdate, but everyone is busy. I even called my sister, but you have to plan to see her a week ahead. She also said she was too hungover to be with Evie. I felt jealous then, wishing I could spend Saturdays relaxing with the newspaper and a fuzzy memory of the night before.

It's silly because what I have now is much better, and I worked hard for it. Now I just need to keep it safe. So, here I am in my hot house, trying not to think about Megan. I try not to think about her, and I jump at every noise and flinch when a shadow passes the window. It's awful.

I keep thinking about how Rachel was here the night Megan disappeared. She was stumbling around, very drunk, and then she just vanished. Tom looked for her for a long time, but he couldn't find her. I can't stop wondering what she was doing.

Rachel and Megan Hipwell are not connected. I talked to Detective Riley, the police officer, about it after we saw Rachel at the Hipwells' house. She said it was not a problem. "She's just curious," she said. "She's lonely and wants to be part of things."

She's likely correct. But then I remember her coming into my house and taking my child. I remember how scared I was when I saw her with Evie by the fence. I think about her creepy smile when I saw her outside the Hipwells' house. Detective Riley doesn't know how dangerous Rachel might be.

RACHEL

• • •

SUNDAY, AUGUST 4, 2013

MORNING

The nightmare I had this morning was different. In it, I did something wrong, but I don't know what. All I know is that it can't be fixed. I know that Tom hates me now, he won't talk to me, and he has told everyone I know about the terrible thing I did. Everyone has turned against me: old coworkers, my friends, even my mother. They look at me with disgust and no one will listen to me. No one will let me say how sorry I am. I feel awful and very guilty, but I can't think what I did. I wake up knowing the dream must be from an old memory, some past mistake—it doesn't matter which one now.

After I got off the train yesterday, I waited outside Ashbury station for fifteen or twenty minutes. I watched to see if the red-haired man got off the train with me, but he wasn't there. I kept thinking I might have missed him, that he was somewhere, waiting for me to walk home so he could follow me. I thought how much I wanted to run home and have Tom waiting for me. To have someone waiting for me.

I walked home by way of the liquor store.

The apartment was empty when I got back. It felt like someone had just left, as if I'd just missed Cathy. But the note on the counter said she was going to lunch with Damien in Henley and wouldn't be back until Sunday night. I felt uneasy and scared. I walked around the apartment, picking things up and putting them down. Something felt wrong, but I realized it was just me.

The silence sounded like voices, so I poured myself a glass of wine, then another, and then I called Scott. The phone went straight to voicemail: his message from a long time ago, the voice of a busy, confident man with a beautiful wife at home. After a few minutes, I called again. The phone was answered, but no one spoke.

"Hello?"

"Who is this?"

"It's Rachel," I answered. "Rachel Watson."

"Oh." I could hear sounds in the background, voices, a woman. Maybe his mother.

"You... I didn't answer your call," I said.

"No... no. Did I call you? Oh. It was a mistake." He seemed confused. "No, just leave it there," he said, and I realized he wasn't talking to me.

"I'm very sorry," I said.

"Yes." His voice was calm and even.

"So sorry."

"Thank you."

"Did you... did you want to talk to me?"

"No, I must have called you by accident," he said, sounding more sure of himself this time.

"Oh." I could tell he wanted to end the call. I knew I should let him be with his family and his sadness. I knew that I should, but I didn't. "Do you know Anna?" I asked him. "Anna Watson?"

"Who? You mean your ex's wife?"

"Yes."

"No. Not really. Megan... Megan watched her kids sometimes last year. Why do you ask?"

I don't know why I ask. I don't know. "Can we meet?" I asked him. "I want to talk to you about something."

"About what?" He sounded bothered. "It's really not a good time."

Hurt by his sharp tone, I was about to hang up when he said, "My house is full of people right now. Tomorrow? Come to the house tomorrow afternoon."

EVENING

He has a cut from shaving: there's blood on his cheek and collar. His hair is wet, and he smells like soap and aftershave. He nods at me and moves aside, telling me to come in, but he doesn't speak. The house is dark and stuffy. The blinds in the living room are closed, and the curtains are shut over the doors to the garden. There are plastic containers on the kitchen counters.

"Everyone is bringing food," Scott says. He points for me to sit at the table, but he stays standing. His arms hang down. "You wanted to tell me something?" He seems like he's not really there. He doesn't look at me. He looks like he's given up.

"I wanted to ask about Anna Watson. What was her relationship with Megan? Did they like each other?"

He frowns and puts his hands on the back of the chair in front of him. "No. They didn't dislike each other. They didn't know each other well. They didn't have a relationship." His shoulders drop even more. He seems tired. "Why are you asking me this?"

I have to be honest. "I saw her. I think I saw her, near the tunnel by the station. I saw her that night... the night Megan disappeared."

He shakes his head, trying to understand. "Sorry? You saw her? Where were you?"

"I was here. I was going to see Tom, my ex-husband, but I—"

He closes his eyes and rubs his forehead. "Wait—you were here—and you saw Anna Watson? I know Anna was here. She lives close by. She told the police she went to the station around seven but didn't see Megan." He grips the chair. He's losing patience. "What are you saying?"

"I had been drinking," I say, my face turning red with shame. "I don't remember exactly, but I just have this feeling—"

Scott holds up his hand. "Stop. I don't want to hear it. You have a problem with your ex and his new wife. That's clear. It has nothing to do with me or Megan. Aren't you ashamed? Do you know what I'm going through? The police questioned me this morning!" He's pushing so hard on the chair, I think it will break. "And you come here with this nonsense. I'm sorry your life is a mess, but it's nothing compared to mine. So, if you don't mind..." He points towards the door.

I stand up. I feel stupid and ashamed. "I wanted to help. I wanted—"

"You can't, okay? You can't help me. No one can help me. My wife is dead, and the police think I killed her." His voice gets louder, and his cheeks turn red. "They think I killed her."

"But... Kamal Abdic..."

The chair slams against the kitchen wall so hard that one of its legs breaks off. I jump back, scared, but Scott barely moves. His hands are by his sides, clenched into fists. I can see the veins in his skin.

"Kamal Abdic," he says, his teeth clenched, "is not a suspect anymore." His voice is calm, but he's trying hard to stay in control. I can feel his anger. I want to get to the front door, but he's in my way, blocking me and the little light in the room.

"Do you know what he's been saying?" he asks, turning away to pick up the chair. Of course I don't, I think, but I realize he's not really talking to me. "Kamal's been telling stories. Kamal says that Megan was unhappy, that I was a jealous, controlling husband, a—what was the word?—an emotional abuser." He says the words like they taste bad. "Kamal says Megan was afraid of me."

"But he's—"

"He's not the only one. That friend of hers, Tara—she says that Megan asked her to cover for her sometimes, that Megan wanted her to lie to me about where she was and what she was doing."

He puts the chair back at the table, and it falls over. I take a step toward the hallway, and he looks at me. "I am a guilty man," he says, his face full of pain. "I am as good as found guilty."

He kicks the broken chair and sits down on one of the three good ones. I stand there, unsure what to do. Should I stay or leave? He starts talking again, his voice so quiet I can barely hear him. "Her phone was in her pocket," he says. I move closer to him. "There was a message from me on it. The last thing I ever said to her, the last words she ever read, were Go to hell you lying bitch."

His head drops, and his shoulders start to shake. I'm close enough to touch him. I raise my hand and, shaking, gently put my fingers on the back of his neck. He doesn't pull away.

"I'm sorry," I say, and I mean it. I'm shocked he would speak to her like that, but I know what it's like to love someone and say awful things in anger or pain. "A text message," I say. "It's not enough. If that's all they have..."

"It's not, though, is it?" He stands up straight and moves my hand away. I walk back to my seat across from him. He doesn't look at me. "I have a reason. I didn't act right when she left. I didn't worry soon enough. I didn't call her soon enough." He laughs sadly. "And Kamal Abdic says there's a history of abuse." He looks at me then, really sees me, and seems hopeful. "You... you can talk to the police. You can say it's a lie, that he's lying. You can at least give my side, say that I loved her, that we were happy."

I feel panic rising. He thinks I can help him. He's depending on me, and all I have is a lie.

"They won't believe me," I say quietly. "They don't believe me. I'm not a reliable witness."

The silence grows in the room. A fly buzzes loudly against the doors. Scott picks at the dried blood on his cheek, I can hear his nails scratching. I push my chair back, the legs scraping on the floor, and he looks up.

"You were here," he says, like he's just now understanding what I said earlier. "You were in Witney the night Megan disappeared?"

I can barely hear him over my own heartbeat. I nod.

"Why didn't you tell the police that?" he asks. I see his jaw tighten.

"I did. I told them. But I didn't... I didn't see anything. I don't remember anything."

He stands up, walks to the doors, and opens the curtain. The sun is very bright. Scott stands with his back to me, arms crossed.

"You were drunk," he says plainly. "But you must remember something. You must—that's why you keep coming back here, right?" He turns to face me. "That's it, isn't it? Why you keep contacting me. You know something." He says this like it's a fact, not a question, not an accusation, not a guess. "Did you see his car?" he asks. "Think. A blue Vauxhall Corsa. Did you see it?" I shake my head, and he throws his arms up, annoyed. "Don't just ignore it. Really think. What did you see? You saw Anna Watson, but that doesn't mean anything. You saw—come on! Who did you see?"

Blinking in the sunlight, I try hard to remember what I saw, but nothing comes. Nothing real, nothing helpful. Nothing I could say out loud. I was in an argument. Or maybe I saw an argument. I tripped on the station steps, a man with red hair helped me up—I think he was nice to me, but now he makes me feel scared. I know I had a cut on my head, another on my lip, and bruises on my arms. I think I remember being in the underpass. It was dark. I was scared and confused. I heard voices. I heard someone call Megan's name. No, that was a dream. That wasn't real. I remember blood. Blood on my head, blood on my hands. I remember Anna. I don't remember Tom. I don't remember Kamal or Scott or Megan.

He is watching me, waiting for me to say something, to give him some comfort, but I have none.

"That night," he says, "that's the important time." He sits back down at the table, closer to me now, his back to the window. There's sweat on his forehead and lip, and he shivers like he has a fever. "That's when it happened. They think that's when it happened. They can't be sure..." He stops talking. "They can't be sure. Because of the state... of the body." He takes a deep breath. "But they think it was that night. Or soon after." He's back to talking to the room, not to me. I listen quietly as he tells the room that she died from a head injury, her skull was broken in many places. There was no sexual assault, or at least none they could prove, because of her state. Her state, which was ruined.

When he looks at me again, there is fear and desperation in his eyes.

"If you remember anything," he says, "you have to help me. Please, try to remember, Rachel." Hearing my name makes my stomach flip, and I feel awful.

On the train home, I think about what he said, and I wonder if it's true. Is the reason I can't forget this stuck in my head? Is there something I need to say? I know I feel something for him, something I can't name and shouldn't feel. But is it more than that? If there's something in my head, maybe someone can help me get it out. Someone like a psychiatrist. A therapist. Someone like Kamal Abdic.

TUESDAY, AUGUST 6, 2013

MORNING

I've barely slept. All night, I lay awake thinking about it, going over it again and again. Is this stupid, risky, pointless? Is it dangerous? I don't know what I'm doing. I made an appointment yesterday morning to see Dr. Kamal Abdic. I called his office and spoke to a receptionist, asking for him by name. I might have imagined it, but I thought she sounded surprised. She said he could see me today at four thirty. So soon? My heart pounding, my mouth dry, I said that would be fine. The session costs £75. That £300 from my mother won't last very long.

Ever since I made the appointment, I can't think about anything else. I'm scared, but also excited. I can't deny that part of me is thrilled to meet Kamal. This all started with him: seeing him changed my life. The moment I saw him kiss Megan, everything changed.

And I need to see him. I need to do something, because the police are only focused on Scott. They questioned him again yesterday. They won't say it, but there's video online: Scott walking into the police station with his mother. His tie was too tight, he looked like he was choking.

Everyone is guessing. The newspapers say the police are being careful, that they can't make another quick arrest. People are talking about a bad investigation, and that the police might need new people. Online, the talk about Scott is awful, the ideas are crazy and disgusting. There are screenshots of him crying and asking for Megan back, next to pictures of killers who also cried on TV. It's terrible, inhuman. I hope he never sees this. It would break his heart.

So, I might be stupid and reckless, but I'm going to see Kamal Abdic. Unlike everyone else, I've seen Scott up close. I know what he's like, and he's not a murderer.

EVENING

My legs are still shaking as I go up the steps to Corly station. I've been shaking for hours, it must be the adrenaline. My heart won't slow down. The train is crowded—no seats here, unlike at Euston. I have to stand in the middle of a car. It's like a hot box. I'm trying to breathe slowly, looking down at my feet. I'm trying to understand what I'm feeling.

Joy, fear, confusion, and guilt. Mostly guilt.

It wasn't what I expected.

By the time I got to the doctor's office, I was completely terrified. I thought he would look at me and know that I knew, that he would see me as a threat. I was afraid I would say the wrong thing, that I would say Megan's name. Then I walked into a boring waiting room and talked to a middle-aged receptionist, who took my information without really looking at me. I sat down and picked up a Vogue magazine, flipping through it with shaky fingers. I tried to focus on what I needed to do while also trying to look bored, like any other patient.

There were two other people there: a man in his twenties reading his phone and an older woman who stared at her feet. She didn't look up, even when the receptionist called her name. She just got up and walked away, she knew where to go. I waited for five minutes, then ten. I could feel my breathing getting shallow. The waiting room was hot and stuffy, and I felt like I couldn't get enough air. I was worried I might pass out.

Then a door opened quickly, and a man walked out. Before I could even see him well, I knew it was him. It was like when I knew Scott wasn't the right person the first time I saw him—just a tall shadow moving towards her. This man held out his hand to me.

“Ms. Watson?”

I looked up at him and felt a shock go through me. I put my hand in his. It was warm, dry, and very big, covering my whole hand.

“Please,” he said, motioning for me to follow him into his office. I did, feeling sick and dizzy. I was walking where she had walked. She had done all of this. She had sat across from him in the chair he told me to sit in. He probably put his hands together like he did earlier today. He probably nodded at her the same way, saying, “Okay, what would you like to talk about today?”

Everything about him felt warm: his hand, his eyes, his voice. I looked at his face for clues, for signs of the mean person who hurt Megan, or for the sad refugee who lost his family. I couldn't see any of that. For a moment, I forgot myself. I forgot to be afraid of him. I was just sitting there, not panicking. I took a deep breath and tried to remember what I needed to say. I told him that for four years I had struggled with alcohol. My drinking had cost me my marriage and my job. It was hurting my health, and I was afraid it would make me lose my mind.

“I don’t remember things,” I said. “I black out and can’t remember where I’ve been or what I’ve done. Sometimes I wonder if I’ve done or said bad things, and I can’t remember. And if someone tells me something I’ve done, it doesn’t feel like it was me. It’s hard to feel responsible for something you don’t remember. So I never feel bad enough. I feel bad, but the thing I’ve done feels separate from me. It’s like it didn’t happen to me.”

All of this came out, all of this truth. I just said it all in front of him in the first few minutes. I was so ready to say it; I had been waiting to tell someone. But it shouldn't have been him. He listened, his clear brown eyes on mine, his hands still. He didn’t look around or take notes. He just listened. Then he nodded slightly and said, “You want to take responsibility for what you have done, but it’s hard to feel fully responsible if you can’t remember it?”

“Yes, that’s it, exactly.”

“So, how do we take responsibility? You can apologize. Even if you don’t remember doing something wrong, your apology can still be real.”

“But I want to feel it. I want to feel… worse.”

It's strange to say, but I think this a lot. I don't feel bad enough. I know what I did wrong, I know all the bad things I've done, even if I don't remember exactly—but I feel separate from those actions. I feel like they happened to someone else.

"You think you should feel worse than you do? That you don't feel bad enough for your mistakes?"

"Yes."

Kamal shook his head. "Rachel, you've told me you lost your marriage, you lost your job—don't you think that's enough punishment?"

I shook my head.

He leaned back a little in his chair. "I think maybe you're being too hard on yourself."

"I'm not."

"Okay. Let's go back a bit. To when the problem started. You said it was... four years ago? Can you tell me about that time?"

I didn't want to. I wasn't completely fooled by his kind voice, by his gentle eyes. I wasn't completely without hope. I wasn't going to tell him everything. I wasn't going to tell him how much I wanted a baby. I told him that my marriage ended, that I was sad, and that I'd always drunk too much, but things just got worse.

"Your marriage ended, so... you left your husband, or he left you, or... you both left?"

"He cheated on me," I said. "He met another woman and fell in love with her." He nodded, waiting for me to continue. "But it wasn't his fault. It was my fault."

"Why do you say that?"

"Well, the drinking started before..."

"So, your husband's affair didn't cause it?"

"No, I had already started. My drinking pushed him away. That's why he stopped..."

Kamal waited. He didn't ask me to go on. He just let me sit there, waiting for me to say the words.

"Why he stopped loving me," I said.

I hate that I'm crying in front of him. I don't know why I couldn't stay strong. I shouldn't have talked about real things. I should have made up problems, created a fake personality. I should have been more prepared.

I hate that I looked at him and thought, for a second, that he cared about me. Because he looked at me like he did, not like he felt sorry for me, but like he understood me, like I was someone he wanted to help.

"So, Rachel, the drinking started before your marriage fell apart. Do you think you know why? I mean, not everyone does. Some people just slowly become depressed or addicted. Was there something specific for you? A death, some other loss?"

I shook my head and shrugged. I wasn't going to tell him that. I won't tell him that.

He waited a bit, then quickly looked at the clock on his desk.

"Maybe we can continue next time?" he said, then smiled, and I felt cold.

Everything about him is warm—his hands, his eyes, his voice—but not his smile. When he shows his teeth, you can see the killer in him. My stomach felt tight, my heart raced again, and I left his office without shaking his hand. I couldn't stand to touch him.

I understand why. I see what Megan liked about him, and it's not just that he's very handsome. He's also calm and makes you feel safe, and he seems very kind. Someone who is innocent, trusting, or just having problems might not see the truth, might not see that under his calm he's a wolf. I understand that. For almost an hour, I was drawn in. I let myself talk to him. I forgot who he was. I betrayed Scott and Megan, and I feel bad about that.

But most of all, I feel bad because I want to go back.

WEDNESDAY, AUGUST 7, 2013

MORNING

I had the dream again where I did something wrong, where everyone is against me and takes Tom's side. I can't explain or even say sorry because I don't know what I did. As I woke up, I thought of a real fight from a long time ago—four years ago—after our first try at IVF failed. I wanted to try again. Tom said we didn't have the money, and I didn't argue. I knew we didn't—we had a big house payment, and he had debts from a bad business deal his father pushed him into. I just had to accept it. I had to hope that one day we would have the money. In the meantime, I had to hold back tears every time I saw a pregnant woman or heard someone else's good news.

A couple of months after we found out the IVF didn't work, he told me about a trip. He was going to Vegas for four nights to watch a big fight and have fun. It was just him and some old friends I had never met. It cost a lot of money. I know because I saw the flight and hotel booking in his email. I don't know how much the boxing tickets cost, but I'm sure they weren't cheap. It wasn't enough to pay for IVF, but it would have been a start. We had a terrible fight about it. I don't remember the details because I had been drinking all afternoon, getting ready to argue with him. So, when I did, it was in the worst way. I remember how cold he was the next day, how he wouldn't talk about it. I remember him telling me, in a flat, disappointed voice, what I had done and said. How I had broken our wedding picture, how I had yelled at him for being selfish, and how I had called him a bad husband and a failure. I remember how much I hated myself that day.

I was wrong to say those things to him, but now I think I had a right to be angry. We were trying to have a baby. Shouldn't we both have been ready to give things up? I would have done anything to have a child. Couldn't he have missed one weekend in Vegas?

I lie in bed for a while, thinking. Then I get up and go for a walk. If I don't, I'll want to go to the corner shop. I haven't had a drink since Sunday, and I feel the urge to drink. I want a little buzz, to escape my thoughts. But I also feel like I've done something good, and it would be a shame to ruin it now.

Ashbury isn't a good place to walk. It's just shops and houses, with no nice park. I walk through the town center, which is okay when it's empty. The trick is to pretend you're going somewhere. Pick a place and walk towards it. I chose the church on Pleasance Road, about two miles from Cathy's place. I've been to a meeting there. I didn't go to the local one because I didn't want to see anyone I know.

When I get to the church, I turn around and walk back home. I walk like I have things to do, like a normal person. I watch the people I pass: two men running with backpacks, training for a race; a young woman in a skirt and sneakers, carrying her heels. I wonder what they're hiding. Are they trying to stop drinking? Are they running to stay in the same place? Are they thinking about someone bad they met, someone they plan to see again?

I'm not normal.

I'm almost home when I see it. I was thinking about my talks with Kamal. What are they supposed to do? Am I really going to look through his desk if he leaves the room? Am I going to try to trick him into saying something? He's probably smarter than me. He probably knows I'm trying to get information. He knows his name has been in the news. He must be careful about people trying to get stories from him.

I'm thinking about this, looking at the ground, when I pass the little shop. I try not to look at it, but I see her name. I look up, and it's there on a newspaper: WAS MEGAN A CHILD KILLER?

RACHEL

• • •

SUNDAY, AUGUST 4, 2013

MORNING

The nightmare I had this morning was different. In it, I did something wrong, but I don't know what. All I know is that it can't be fixed. I know that Tom hates me now, he won't talk to me, and he has told everyone I know about the terrible thing I did. Everyone has turned against me: old coworkers, my friends, even my mother. They look at me with disgust and no one will listen to me. No one will let me say how sorry I am. I feel awful and very guilty, but I can't think what I did. I wake up knowing the dream must be from an old memory, some past mistake—it doesn't matter which one now.

After I got off the train yesterday, I waited outside Ashbury station for fifteen or twenty minutes. I watched to see if the red-haired man got off the train with me, but he wasn't there. I kept thinking I might have missed him, that he was somewhere, waiting for me to walk home so he could follow me. I thought how much I wanted to run home and have Tom waiting for me. To have someone waiting for me.

I walked home by way of the liquor store.

The apartment was empty when I got back. It felt like someone had just left, as if I'd just missed Cathy. But the note on the counter said she was going to lunch with Damien in Henley and wouldn't be back until Sunday night. I felt uneasy and scared. I walked around the apartment, picking things up and putting them down. Something felt wrong, but I realized it was just me.

The silence sounded like voices, so I poured myself a glass of wine, then another, and then I called Scott. The phone went straight to voicemail: his message from a long time ago, the voice of a busy, confident man with a beautiful wife at home. After a few minutes, I called again. The phone was answered, but no one spoke.

"Hello?"

"Who is this?"

"It's Rachel," I answered. "Rachel Watson."

"Oh." I could hear sounds in the background, voices, a woman. Maybe his mother.

"You... I didn't answer your call," I said.

"No... no. Did I call you? Oh. It was a mistake." He seemed confused. "No, just leave it there," he said, and I realized he wasn't talking to me.

"I'm very sorry," I said.

"Yes." His voice was calm and even.

"So sorry."

"Thank you."

"Did you... did you want to talk to me?"

"No, I must have called you by accident," he said, sounding more sure of himself this time.

"Oh." I could tell he wanted to end the call. I knew I should let him be with his family and his sadness. I knew that I should, but I didn't. "Do you know Anna?" I asked him. "Anna Watson?"

"Who? You mean your ex's wife?"

"Yes."

"No. Not really. Megan... Megan watched her kids sometimes last year. Why are you asking?"

I don't know why I'm asking. I don't know. "Can we meet?" I asked him. "I want to talk to you about something."

"About what?" He sounded bothered. "It's really not a good time."

His sharp tone hurt, and I was about to hang up when he said, "I have a lot of people here. Tomorrow? Come to the house tomorrow afternoon."

EVENING

He has a cut from shaving: there's blood on his cheek and his shirt collar. His hair is wet, and he smells like soap and aftershave. He nods at me and moves aside, telling me to come in, but he doesn't speak. The house is dark and stuffy. The blinds in the living room are closed, and the curtains are shut over the doors to the garden. There are plastic food containers on the kitchen counters.

"Everyone is bringing food," Scott says. He points for me to sit at the table, but he stays standing. His arms hang down. "You wanted to tell me something?" He seems like he's not really there. He doesn't look at me. He looks like he's given up.

"I wanted to ask about Anna Watson. What was her relationship with Megan? Did they like each other?"

He frowns and puts his hands on the back of the chair in front of him. "No. They didn't dislike each other. They didn't know each other well. They didn't have a relationship." His shoulders slump even more. He seems tired. "Why are you asking me this?"

I have to be honest. "I saw her. I think I saw her, near the tunnel by the station. I saw her that night... the night Megan disappeared."

He shakes his head, trying to understand. "Sorry? You saw her? Where were you?"

"I was here. I was going to see Tom, my ex-husband, but I—"

He closes his eyes and rubs his forehead. "Wait—you were here—and you saw Anna Watson? I know Anna was here. She lives close by. She told the police she went to the station around seven but didn't see Megan." He grips the chair. He's losing his patience. "What are you saying?"

"I had been drinking," I say, my face turning red with shame. "I don't remember everything, but I have this feeling—"

Scott holds up his hand. "Stop. I don't want to hear it. You have a problem with your ex and his new wife. That's clear. It has nothing to do with me or Megan. Aren't you ashamed? Do you know what I'm going through? The police questioned me this morning!" He's pushing so hard on the chair, I think it will break. "And you come here with this nonsense. I'm sorry your life is a mess, but it's nothing compared to mine. So, if you don't mind..." He points his head toward the door.

I stand up. I feel silly and ashamed. "I wanted to help. I wanted—"

"You can't, okay? You can't help me. No one can help me. My wife is dead, and the police think I killed her." His voice gets louder, and his cheeks turn red. "They think I killed her."

"But... Kamal Abdic..."

The chair slams against the kitchen wall so hard that one of its legs breaks off. I jump back, scared, but Scott barely moves. His hands are back at his sides, clenched into fists. I can see the veins in his skin.

"Kamal Abdic," he says, his teeth clenched, "is not a suspect anymore." His voice is calm, but he's trying hard to stay in control. I can feel his anger. I want to get to the front door, but he's in my way, blocking me and the little light in the room.

"Do you know what he's been saying?" he asks, turning away to pick up the chair. Of course I don't, I think, but I know he's not really talking to me. "Kamal's been telling stories. Kamal says that Megan was unhappy, that I was a jealous, controlling husband, a—what was the word?—an emotional abuser." He says the words like they taste bad. "Kamal says Megan was scared of me."

"But he's—"

"He's not the only one. That friend of hers, Tara—she says that Megan asked her to lie for her sometimes, that Megan wanted her to lie to me about where she was and what she was doing."

He puts the chair back at the table, and it falls over. I take a step toward the hallway, and he looks at me. "I am a guilty man," he says, his face twisted in pain. "I am as good as found guilty."

He kicks the broken chair away and sits on one of the three good ones. I stand there, not sure what to do. Should I stay or leave? He starts talking again, his voice so quiet I can barely hear him. "Her phone was in her pocket," he says. I move closer to him. "There was a message from me on it. The last thing I ever said to her, the last words she ever read, were Go to hell you lying bitch."

His head is down, and his shoulders start to shake. I'm close enough to touch him. I raise my hand and, shaking, gently put my fingers on the back of his neck. He doesn't pull away.

"I'm sorry," I say, and I mean it. I'm shocked he would speak to her like that, but I know what it's like to love someone and say awful things in anger or pain. "A text message," I say. "It's not enough. If that's all they have..."

"It's not, though, is it?" He stands up straight and pulls my hand away. I walk back to my seat across from him. He doesn't look at me. "I have a reason. I didn't act right when she left. I didn't worry soon enough. I didn't call her soon enough." He laughs sadly. "And Kamal Abdic says I have a history of being abusive." He looks at me then, really sees me, and a light comes on. Hope. "You... you can talk to the police. You can tell them he's lying. You can at least give my side, tell them I loved her, that we were happy."

I feel panic rising. He thinks I can help him. He's depending on me, and all I have is a lie.

"They won't believe me," I say quietly. "They don't believe me. I'm not a reliable witness."

The silence grows between us. A fly buzzes loudly against the doors. Scott picks at the dried blood on his cheek, I can hear his nails scratching. I push my chair back, the legs scraping on the floor, and he looks up.

"You were here," he says, like he's just now understanding what I said earlier. "You were in Witney the night Megan disappeared?"

I can barely hear him over my own heartbeat. I nod.

"Why didn't you tell the police that?" he asks. I see his jaw tighten.

"I did. I told them. But I didn't see anything. I don't remember anything."

He stands up, walks to the doors, and opens the curtain. The sun is very bright for a moment. Scott stands with his back to me, arms crossed.

"You were drunk," he says plainly. "But you must remember something. You must—that's why you keep coming back here, right?" He turns to face me. "That's it, isn't it? Why you keep contacting me. You know something." He says this like it's a fact, not a question, not an accusation, not a guess. "Did you see his car?" he asks. "Think. A blue Vauxhall Corsa. Did you see it?" I shake my head, and he throws his arms up, annoyed. "Don't just ignore it. Really think. What did you see? You saw Anna Watson, but that doesn't mean anything. You saw—come on! Who did you see?"

Blinking in the sunlight, I try hard to remember what I saw, but nothing comes. Nothing real, nothing helpful. Nothing I could say out loud. I was in an argument. Or maybe I saw an argument. I tripped on the station steps, a man with red hair helped me up—I think he was nice to me, but now he makes me feel scared. I know I had a cut on my head, another on my lip, and bruises on my arms. I think I remember being in the underpass. It was dark. I was scared and confused. I heard voices. I heard someone call Megan's name. No, that was a dream. That wasn't real. I remember blood. Blood on my head, blood on my hands. I remember Anna. I don't remember Tom. I don't remember Kamal or Scott or Megan.

He is watching me, waiting for me to say something, to give him some comfort, but I have none.

"That night," he says, "that's the important time." He sits back down at the table, closer to me now, his back to the window. There's sweat on his forehead and lip, and he shivers like he has a fever. "That's when it happened. They think that's when it happened. They can't be sure..." He stops talking. "They can't be sure. Because of the state... of the body." He takes a deep breath. "But they think it was that night. Or soon after." He's back to talking to the room, not to me. I listen quietly as he tells the room that she died from a head injury, her skull was broken in many places. There was no sexual assault, or at least none they could prove, because of her state. Her state, which was ruined.

When he looks at me again, there is fear and desperation in his eyes.

"If you remember anything," he says, "you have to help me. Please, try to remember, Rachel." Hearing my name makes my stomach flip, and I feel awful.

On the train home, I think about what he said, and I wonder if it's true. Is the reason I can't forget this stuck in my head? Is there something I need to say? I know I feel something for him, something I can't name and shouldn't feel. But is it more than that? If there's something in my head, maybe someone can help me get it out. Someone like a psychiatrist. A therapist. Someone like Kamal Abdic.

TUESDAY, AUGUST 6, 2013

MORNING

I've barely slept. All night, I lay awake thinking about it, going over it again and again. Is this stupid, risky, pointless? Is it dangerous? I don't know what I'm doing. I made an appointment yesterday morning to see Dr. Kamal Abdic. I called his office and spoke to a receptionist, asking for him by name. I might have imagined it, but I thought she sounded surprised. She said he could see me today at four thirty. So soon? My heart pounding, my mouth dry, I said that would be fine. The session costs £75. That £300 from my mother won't last very long.

Ever since I made the appointment, I can't think about anything else. I'm scared, but also excited. I can't deny that part of me is thrilled to meet Kamal. This all started with him: seeing him changed my life. The moment I saw him kiss Megan, everything changed.

And I need to see him. I need to do something, because the police are only focused on Scott. They questioned him again yesterday. They won't say it, but there's video online: Scott walking into the police station with his mother. His tie was too tight, he looked like he was choking.

Everyone is guessing. The newspapers say the police are being careful, that they can't make another quick arrest. People are talking about a bad investigation, and that the police might need new people. Online, the talk about Scott is awful, the ideas are crazy and disgusting. There are screenshots of him crying and asking for Megan to come back, next to pictures of killers who also cried on TV. It's terrible, inhuman. I hope he never sees this. It would break his heart.

So, I might be stupid and reckless, but I'm going to see Kamal Abdic. Unlike everyone else, I've seen Scott up close. I know what he's like, and he's not a murderer.

EVENING

My legs are still shaking as I go up the steps to Corly station. I've been shaking for hours, it must be the adrenaline. My heart won't slow down. The train is crowded—no seats here, unlike at Euston. I have to stand in the middle of a car. It's like a hot box. I'm trying to breathe slowly, looking down at my feet. I'm trying to understand what I'm feeling.

Joy, fear, confusion, and guilt. Mostly guilt.

It wasn't what I expected.

By the time I got to the doctor's office, I was completely terrified. I thought he would look at me and know that I knew, that he would see me as a threat. I was afraid I would say the wrong thing, that I wouldn't be able to stop myself from saying Megan's name. Then I walked into a boring waiting room and talked to a middle-aged receptionist, who took my information without really looking at me. I sat down and picked up a Vogue magazine, flipping through it with shaky fingers. I tried to focus on what I needed to do while also trying to look bored, like any other patient.

There were two other people there: a man in his twenties reading his phone and an older woman who stared sadly at her feet. She didn't look up, even when the receptionist called her name. She just got up and walked away, knowing where to go. I waited for five minutes, then ten. I could feel my breathing getting shallow. The waiting room was hot and stuffy, and I felt like I couldn't get enough air. I was worried I might pass out.

Then a door opened quickly, and a man walked out. Before I could even see him well, I knew it was him. It was like when I knew Scott wasn't the right person the first time I saw him, just a tall shadow moving toward her. This man held out his hand to me.

“Ms. Watson?”

I looked up at him and felt a shock go through me. I put my hand in his. It was warm, dry, and very big, covering my whole hand.

“Please,” he said, motioning for me to follow him into his office. I did, feeling sick and dizzy. I was walking where she had walked. She had done all of this. She had sat across from him in the chair he told me to sit in. He probably put his hands together like he did earlier today. He probably nodded at her the same way, saying, “Okay, what would you like to talk about today?”

Everything about him felt warm: his hand, his eyes, his voice. I looked at his face for clues, for signs of the mean person who hurt Megan, or for the sad refugee who lost his family. I didn't see any of that. For a moment, I forgot myself. I forgot to be afraid of him. I sat there and wasn't panicking anymore. I took a deep breath and tried to remember what I needed to say, and then I said it. I told him that for four years I had struggled with alcohol. My drinking had ruined my marriage and my job. It was hurting my health, and I was afraid it would also hurt my mind.

“I don’t remember things,” I said. “I black out and can’t remember where I’ve been or what I’ve done. Sometimes I wonder if I’ve done or said bad things, and I can’t remember. And if someone tells me something I’ve done, it doesn’t feel like it was me. It doesn’t feel like I did that thing. It’s hard to feel responsible for something you don’t remember. So I never feel bad enough. I feel bad, but the thing I did feels separate from me. It’s like it doesn’t belong to me.”

All of this came out, all of this truth. I just said it all in front of him in the first few minutes. I was so ready to say it. I had been waiting to say it to someone. But it shouldn't have been him. He listened, his clear brown eyes on mine, his hands still. He didn't look around or take notes. He just listened. Then he nodded slightly and said, “You want to take responsibility for what you have done, but it’s hard to feel fully responsible if you can’t remember it?”

“Yes, that’s it, exactly.”

“So, how do we take responsibility? You can apologize. Even if you can’t remember doing something wrong, that doesn’t mean your apology isn’t real.”

“But I want to feel it. I want to feel… worse.”

It's strange to say, but I think this a lot. I don't feel bad enough. I know what I did wrong, I know all the bad things I've done, even if I don't remember exactly—but I feel separate from those actions. It's like they happened to someone else.

"You think you should feel worse than you do? That you don't feel bad enough for your mistakes?"

"Yes."

Kamal shook his head. "Rachel, you've told me you lost your marriage, you lost your job—don't you think that's enough punishment?"

I shook my head.

He leaned back a little in his chair. "I think maybe you're being too hard on yourself."

"I'm not."

"Okay. Can we go back a bit? To when the problem started. You said it was... four years ago? Can you tell me about that time?"

I didn't want to. I wasn't completely fooled by his kind voice, by his gentle eyes. I still had some hope. I wasn't going to tell him everything. I wasn't going to tell him how much I wanted a baby. I told him my marriage ended, that I was sad, and that I'd always drunk, but things just got worse.

"Your marriage ended, so... you left your husband, or he left you, or... you both left?"

"He cheated on me," I said. "He met another woman and fell in love with her." He nodded, waiting for me to continue. "But it wasn't his fault. It was my fault."

"Why do you say that?"

"Well, the drinking started before..."

"So, your husband's affair didn't cause it?"

"No, I had already started. My drinking pushed him away. That's why he stopped..."

Kamal waited. He didn't ask me to go on. He just let me sit there, waiting for me to say the words.

"Why he stopped loving me," I said.

I hate that I'm crying in front of him. I don't know why I couldn't stay strong. I shouldn't have talked about real things. I should have made up some problems, some fake story. I should have been more ready.

I hate that I looked at him and thought, for a second, that he cared about me. Because he looked at me like he did, not like he felt sorry for me, but like he understood me, like I was someone he wanted to help.

"So, Rachel, the drinking started before your marriage fell apart. Do you think you know why? I mean, not everyone does. Some people just slowly become depressed or addicted. Was there something specific for you? A death, some other loss?"

I shook my head and shrugged. I wasn't going to tell him that. I won't tell him that.

He waited a bit, then quickly looked at the clock on his desk.

"Maybe we can continue next time?" he said, then smiled, and I felt cold.

Everything about him is warm—his hands, his eyes, his voice—but not his smile. When he shows his teeth, you can see the killer in him. My stomach felt tight, my heart raced again, and I left his office without shaking his hand. I couldn't touch him.

I understand why. I see what Megan liked about him, and it's not just that he's very handsome. He's also calm and makes you feel safe, and he seems very kind. Someone who is innocent, trusting, or just having problems might not see the truth. They might not see that under his calm surface, he's like a wolf. I understand that. For almost an hour, I was drawn in. I let myself be open with him. I forgot who he was. I betrayed Scott and Megan, and I feel bad about that.

But most of all, I feel bad because I want to go back.

WEDNESDAY, AUGUST 7, 2013

MORNING

I had the dream again where I did something wrong. Everyone is against me and on Tom's side. I can't explain or even say sorry because I don't know what I did. As I woke up, I thought about a real fight we had a long time ago—four years ago—after our first try at IVF failed. I wanted to try again. Tom said we didn't have the money, and I didn't argue. I knew we didn't. We had a big house payment, and he had debts from a bad business deal his father pushed him into. I just had to accept it. I had to hope that one day we would have the money. In the meantime, I had to hold back tears every time I saw a pregnant woman or heard someone else's good news.

A couple of months after we found out the IVF didn't work, he told me about a trip. He was going to Vegas for four nights to watch a big fight and relax. It was just him and some old friends I had never met. It cost a lot of money. I know because I saw the flight and hotel booking in his email. I don't know how much the boxing tickets cost, but I'm sure they weren't cheap. It wasn't enough to pay for another round of IVF, but it would have been a start. We had a terrible fight about it. I don't remember all the details because I had been drinking all afternoon, getting ready to argue with him. So, when I did, it was in the worst way. I remember how cold he was the next day and how he wouldn't talk about it. I remember him telling me, in a flat, disappointed voice, what I had done and said. I had broken our wedding photo, yelled at him for being selfish, and called him a bad husband and a failure. I remember how much I hated myself that day.

I was wrong to say those things to him, but now I think I had a right to be angry. We were trying to have a baby. Shouldn't we both have been ready to give things up? I would have done anything to have a child. Couldn't he have missed one weekend in Vegas?

I lie in bed thinking, then get up to walk. If I don't, I'll want to go to the corner shop. I haven't had a drink since Sunday, and I feel the urge to drink, to escape my thoughts. But I also feel like I've done something good and don't want to ruin it.

Ashbury isn't good for walking. It's just shops and houses, no nice park. I walk through town, which is okay when it's empty. I pretend I'm going somewhere, pick a place and walk towards it. I chose the church on Pleasance Road, about two miles from Cathy's place. I went to a meeting there before. I didn't go to the local one because I didn't want to see people I know.

At the church, I turn around and walk home, like I have things to do. Normal. I watch people: two men running with backpacks, training for a race; a young woman in a skirt and sneakers, carrying her heels, going to work. I wonder what they're hiding. Are they trying to stop drinking? Are they running to stay still? Are they thinking about someone bad they met?

I'm not normal.

I'm almost home when I see it. I was thinking about my talks with Kamal. Am I really going to look through his desk? Try to trick him into saying something? He's probably smarter than me and knows I'm trying to get information. He knows his name is in the news, so he must be careful.

I'm thinking about this, looking at the ground, when I pass the Londis shop. I try not to look, but I see her name. I look up, and it's there on a newspaper: WAS MEGAN A CHILD KILLER?

ANNA

• • •

WEDNESDAY, AUGUST 7, 2013

MORNING

I was with the other moms from the National Childbirth Trust at Starbucks when it happened. We were in our usual spot by the window. The kids were making a mess with Lego on the floor. Beth was trying again to get me to join her book club. Then Diane arrived. She looked like she had important gossip to share. She could barely wait as she pushed her double stroller through the door.

“Anna,” she said, looking serious, “have you seen this?” She held up a newspaper with the headline: WAS MEGAN A CHILD KILLER? I couldn't speak. I just stared at it and, for some reason, started crying. Evie was scared. She cried loudly. It was terrible.

I went to the bathroom to clean myself (and Evie). When I came back, they were all talking quietly. Diane looked at me and asked, “Are you okay, sweetie?” I could tell she was enjoying this.

I had to leave. I couldn't stay. They were all acting very worried, saying how awful it must be for me. But I could see their true feelings: they were judging me. How could you let that monster take care of your child? You must be the worst mother.

I tried to call Tom on the way home, but he didn't answer. I left him a message to call me back soon. I tried to sound calm, but I was shaking and my legs felt weak.

I didn't buy the paper, but I had to read the story online. It all seemed unclear. “People close to the Hipwell investigation” said that someone claimed Megan “might have been involved in the killing of her own child” ten years ago. These “people” also said that this could be why she was murdered. The detective in charge of the investigation—Gaskill, who talked to us after she disappeared—didn't say anything about it.

Tom called me back. He was busy with work and couldn't come home. He tried to calm me down, saying all the right things. He said the story was probably not true. "You know you can't believe everything you read in the news," he said. I didn't argue much because he was the one who suggested she help with Evie. He must feel awful.

And he might be right. It might not be true. But why would someone make up a story like that? I can't help thinking, I knew it. I always felt something was wrong with her. At first, I thought she was just immature, but it was more than that. She seemed distant and only cared about herself. I'll be honest, I'm glad she's gone. Good riddance.

EVENING

I'm upstairs in the bedroom. Tom is watching TV with Evie. We're not talking. It's my fault. When he came home, I immediately started arguing with him.

I had been thinking about it all day. I couldn't stop myself. She was everywhere I looked. Here, in my house, holding my child, feeding her, changing her, playing with her while I was resting. I kept thinking about all the times I left Evie alone with her, and it made me feel sick.

Then I felt paranoid, like I was being watched, which I've felt a lot since living here. At first, I thought it was the trains. All the people staring out the windows at us made me uneasy. It was one of the reasons I didn't want to move here, but Tom didn't want to move. He said we'd lose money if we sold.

First it was the trains, then Rachel. Rachel watching us, showing up on our street, calling us all the time. And even Megan, when she was here with Evie, I felt like she was watching me, judging my parenting, like I couldn't handle things on my own. It sounds silly, I know. Then I think about the day Rachel took Evie, and I feel cold and think, Maybe I'm not being silly at all.

So when Tom came home, I was ready to fight. I told him we had to leave. I can't stay in this house, on this street, knowing what happened here. Everywhere I look, I see Rachel and Megan. I think about everything she touched. It's too much. I said I didn't care if we got a good price for the house.

"You will care when we have to live in a worse place and can't pay our mortgage," he said calmly. I asked if he could ask his parents for help since they have a lot of money. But he said he wouldn't ask them, that he'd never ask them for anything again. He got angry and said he didn't want to talk about it. It's because of how his parents treated him when he left Rachel for me. I shouldn't have mentioned them, it always makes him mad.

But I can't help it. I feel desperate. Now, every time I close my eyes, I see her sitting at the kitchen table with Evie on her lap. She'd be playing with her, smiling and talking, but it never felt real. It never seemed like she really wanted to be there. She always seemed happy to give Evie back to me when it was time to leave. It was almost like she didn't like holding a child.

RACHEL

• • •

WEDNESDAY, AUGUST 7, 2013

EVENING

The heat is terrible, it keeps getting hotter. With the apartment windows open, you can smell the bad air from the street. My throat feels scratchy. I'm taking my second shower of the day when the phone rings. I ignore it, and it rings again. And again. By the time I'm done, it's ringing for a fourth time, and I answer.

He sounds scared, breathing fast. His voice is broken. "I can't go home," he says. "There are cameras everywhere."

"Scott?"

"I know this is... this is really strange, but I just need to go somewhere, somewhere they won't be waiting for me. I can't go to my mother's, or my friends'. I'm just... driving around. I've been driving around since I left the police station..." His voice cracks. "I just need an hour or two. To sit, to think. Without them, without the police, without people asking me questions. I'm sorry, but could I come to your house?"

I say yes, of course. Not just because he sounds scared and desperate, but because I want to see him. I want to help him. I tell him my address and he says he'll be here in fifteen minutes.

The doorbell rings ten minutes later: short, quick, urgent rings.

"I'm sorry to do this," he says as I open the door. "I didn't know where else to go." He looks scared: he's shaking, pale, and sweaty.

"It's okay," I say, moving aside to let him in. I lead him to the living room and tell him to sit. I get him a glass of water from the kitchen. He drinks it quickly, then sits hunched over, with his forearms on his knees and his head down.

I stand there, not sure if I should talk or stay quiet. I take his glass and fill it again, saying nothing. Finally, he starts to speak.

"You probably think the worst has happened," he says quietly. "I mean, you would, right?" He looks up at me. "My wife is dead, and the police think I killed her. What could be worse than that?"

He's talking about the news, about what people are saying about her. This story in the papers, supposedly from someone in the police, says Megan was involved in a child's death. It's unclear and made up, a way to hurt a dead woman's reputation. It's awful.

"It's not true, though," I say to him. "It can't be."

He looks blank and confused. "Detective Riley told me this morning," he says. He coughs and clears his throat. "The news I always wanted to hear. You can't imagine," he continues, his voice very soft, "how much I've wished for it. I used to dream about it, imagine how she'd look, how she'd smile at me, shy and knowing, how she'd take my hand and kiss it..." He's lost in his thoughts, and I don't know what he's talking about. "Today," he says, "today I found out that Megan was pregnant."

He starts to cry, and I'm crying too, for a baby that never was, the child of a woman I never knew. But the sadness is almost too much. I don't understand how Scott is still alive. This should have killed him, taken all his energy. But somehow, he's still here.

I can't talk or move. The living room is hot and stuffy, even with the windows open. I can hear sounds from the street: a police car, girls laughing, loud music from a car. Normal life. But in here, the world is ending. For Scott, the world is ending, and I can't speak. I just stand there, quiet, helpless, and useless.

Then I hear footsteps outside, and the sound of Cathy looking for her keys in her bag. It makes me snap back to reality. I have to do something: I grab Scott's hand, and he looks up at me, surprised.

"Come with me," I say, and pull him up. He lets me lead him into the hall and up the stairs. Cathy opens the door. I close the bedroom door behind us.

"My roommate," I say to explain. "She might ask questions. I know you don't want that right now."

He nods. He looks around my small room. He sees the messy bed, the clean and dirty clothes on my chair, the empty walls, and the cheap furniture. I feel ashamed. This is my life: messy, worn, and small. Not good. As I think this, I realize it's silly to think Scott would care about my life right now.

I tell him to sit on the bed. He does, wiping his eyes. He sighs.

"Can I get you anything?" I ask.

"A beer?"

"I don't have alcohol," I say, and I feel my face turn red. But Scott doesn't notice. He doesn't even look up. "I can make tea?" He nods again. "Lie down," I say. "Rest." He does what I say, taking off his shoes and lying on the bed, like a sick child.

Downstairs, while the water boils, I talk to Cathy. She talks about a new lunch place in Northcote ("really good salads") and how annoying her new coworker is. I smile and nod, but I'm not really listening. I'm waiting to hear him, for any sounds. It feels strange to have him here, in my bed, upstairs. It makes me feel dizzy, like I'm dreaming.

Cathy stops talking and looks at me, confused. "Are you okay?" she asks. "You look... out of it."

"I'm just tired," I say. "I don't feel well. I think I'll go to bed."

She looks at me. She knows I haven't been drinking (she always knows), but she probably thinks I'm about to start. I don't care, I can't think about it now. I pick up the cup of tea for Scott and tell her I'll see her in the morning.
