I stop outside my bedroom door and listen. It's quiet. Carefully, I turn the doorknob and open the door. He's lying there, in the same position I left him, his hands at his sides, his eyes closed. I can hear his breathing, soft and uneven. He takes up half the bed, but I want to lie down next to him, to put my arm around him, to comfort him. Instead, I cough a little and hold out the cup of tea.

He sits up. "Thank you," he says roughly, taking the mug. "Thank you for... giving me a safe place. It's been... I can't explain how it's been, since that story came out."

"The one about what happened a long time ago?"

"Yeah, that one."

How the newspapers got that story is argued about a lot. There's been a lot of guessing, with people blaming the police, Kamal Abdic, and Scott.

"It's a lie," I say to him. "Right?"

"Of course it is, but it gives someone a reason, doesn't it? That's what they're saying: Megan killed her baby, which would give someone—the baby's father, I guess—a reason to kill her. Years and years later."

"That's crazy."

"But you know what everyone's saying. That I made up this story, not just to make her look bad, but to move suspicion away from me, to some unknown person. Some guy from her past that no one even knows about."

I sit next to him on the bed. Our legs almost touch.

“What are the police saying?”

He shrugs. “Not much. They asked me what I knew. Did I know she had a child before? Did I know what happened? Did I know who the father was? I said no, it was all lies, she’d never been pregnant…” His voice breaks again. He stops and drinks some tea. “I asked them where the story came from, how it got into the newspapers. They said they couldn’t tell me. It’s from him, I guess. Abdic.” He sighs deeply. “I don’t understand why. I don’t understand why he would say those things about her. I don’t know what he’s trying to do. He’s clearly crazy.”

I think about the man I met: calm, soft-spoken, and kind. The opposite of crazy. But that smile… “It’s awful that this was printed. There should be rules…”

“You can’t lie about dead people,” he says. He’s quiet for a moment, then says, “They said they won’t share the information about this… about her pregnancy. Not yet. Maybe not ever. But not until they’re sure.”

“Until they’re sure?”

“It’s not Abdic’s child,” he says.

“They did DNA tests?”

He shakes his head. “No, I just know. I can’t explain it, but I know. The baby is—was—mine.”

“If he thought it was his baby, it gives him a reason, right?” He wouldn’t be the first man to get rid of a child by getting rid of its mother—but I don’t say that. And—I also don’t say this—it gives Scott a reason, too. If he thought his wife was pregnant with another man’s child… but he couldn’t have done it. His shock, his sadness—it has to be real. No one can act that well.

Scott doesn't seem to be listening anymore. His eyes are staring blankly at the bedroom door, and he looks like he's sinking into the bed as if it were quicksand.

"You should stay here for a bit," I tell him. "Try to sleep."

He looks at me and almost smiles. "You don't mind?" he asks. "I would be grateful. It's hard for me to sleep at home. It's not just the people outside, the feeling that they're trying to get to me. It's not only that. It's her. She's everywhere, I can't stop seeing her. I walk down the stairs and I try not to look, but when I pass the window, I have to go back and check if she's not out there, on the patio." I feel tears welling up as he tells me this. "She liked to sit out there, you know—on our little patio. She liked to sit there and watch the trains."

"I know," I say, putting my hand on his arm. "I used to see her there sometimes."

"I keep hearing her voice," he says. "I keep hearing her calling my name. I lie in bed and I can hear her calling me from outside. I keep thinking she's out there." He's shaking.

"Lie down," I say, taking the mug from his hand. "Rest."

When I'm sure he's asleep, I lie down behind him, my face close to his shoulder. I close my eyes and listen to my heart beating, the pulse in my neck. I smell his sad, stale scent.

When I wake up, hours later, he's gone.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 8, 2013

MORNING

I feel like I'm betraying someone. He left just a few hours ago, and now I'm going to see Kamal. This is the man he thinks killed his wife and child. I feel sick. I wonder if I should have told him my plan, that I'm doing this for him. But I'm not sure if I'm only doing it for him, and I don't really have a plan.

I will share something personal. That's my plan for today. I will talk about something real. I will talk about wanting a child. I'll see if that causes anything—a strange reaction, any kind of response. I'll see what happens.

It doesn't get me anywhere.

He starts by asking how I'm feeling and when I last had a drink.

"Sunday," I tell him.

"Good. That's good." He puts his hands in his lap. "You look well." He smiles, and I don't see a killer. I wonder what I saw the other day. Did I imagine it?

"Last time, you asked how the drinking started." He nods. "I became sad," I say. "We were trying... I was trying to get pregnant. I couldn't, and I became sad. That's when it started."

Soon, I'm crying again. It's hard to resist kindness from strangers. Someone who looks at you, who doesn't know you, who says it's okay, no matter what you did: you suffered, you hurt, you deserve forgiveness. I tell him my feelings and I forget, again, why I'm here. I don't watch his face for a reaction, I don't look at his eyes for any sign of guilt or suspicion. I let him comfort me.

He is kind and makes sense. He talks about ways to cope, and reminds me that I'm still young.

So maybe it does get me somewhere, because I leave Kamal Abdic's office feeling better and more hopeful. He has helped me. I sit on the train and try to remember the killer I saw, but I can't see him anymore. I'm having trouble seeing him as a man who could beat a woman and crush her skull.

A bad, embarrassing picture comes to mind: Kamal with his gentle hands, his calming way, his soft speech, compared to Scott, who is big and strong, wild, and hopeless. I have to remember that this is Scott now, not how he used to be. I have to keep thinking about what he was like before all of this. And then I have to admit that I don't know what Scott was like before all of this.

FRIDAY, AUGUST 9, 2013

EVENING

The train stops at the light. I take a drink from the cold can of gin and tonic and look up at his house, her balcony. I was doing so well, but I need this. A little boost of bravery. I'm going to see Scott, and I'll have to face all the dangers of Blenheim Road first: Tom, Anna, police, reporters. The tunnel, with its blurry memories of fear and blood. But he asked me to come, and I couldn't say no.

They found the little girl last night. What was left of her. Buried on the land of a farm near the East Anglian coast, just where someone had told them to look. It was in the news this morning:

Police have started looking into the death of a child after they found human remains buried in the yard of a house near Holkham, north Norfolk. The discovery happened after police were told about a possible illegal killing while they were looking into the death of Megan Hipwell, from Witney, whose body was found in Corly Woods last week.

I called Scott this morning when I saw the news. He didn't answer, so I left a message, telling him I was sorry. He called back this afternoon.

"Are you okay?" I asked him.

"Not really." His voice was slurred from drinking.

"I'm so sorry . . . do you need anything?"

"I need someone who won't say, 'I knew it.'"

"I'm sorry?"

"My mother was here all afternoon. She says she knew all along—'that girl wasn't right, something was off, no family, no friends, came from nowhere.' I wonder why she never told me." The sound of glass breaking, and then swearing.

"Are you okay?" I asked again.

"Can you come over?" he asked.

"To the house?"

"Yes."

"I... the police, reporters... I'm not sure..."

"Please. I just want someone with me. Someone who knew Megs, who liked her. Someone who doesn't believe all of this..."

He was drunk, and I knew it, but I said yes anyway.

Now, on the train, I'm also drinking and thinking about what he said. Someone who knew Megs, who liked her. I didn't know her, and I'm not sure I like her now. I quickly finish my drink and open another.

I get off at Witney. I'm with the other Friday evening commuters, just another worker among the hot, tired people, looking forward to going home and sitting outside with a cold beer, eating dinner with my kids, and going to bed early. Maybe it's the gin, but it feels really good to be with the crowd, everyone checking their phones and looking for their train passes. I'm reminded of the first summer we lived on Blenheim Road, when I used to rush home from work every night, eager to get out of the station and run down the street. Tom would be working from home, and he would start taking my clothes off as soon as I walked in. I smile thinking about it now, the excitement of it: my face getting hot as I hurried down the road, holding back a grin, my breath getting faster, thinking of him and knowing he was also waiting for me to get home.

My head is so full of those memories that I forget to worry about Tom and Anna, the police and the photographers. Before I know it, I'm at Scott's door, ringing the bell. The door opens, and I feel excited, even though I shouldn't. But I don't feel guilty because Megan isn't who I thought she was. She wasn't that beautiful, happy girl on the terrace. She wasn't a loving wife. She wasn't even a good person. She was a liar and a cheater.

She was a killer.

MEGAN

• • •

THURSDAY, JUNE 20, 2013

EVENING

I'm on the couch in his living room, holding a glass of wine. The house is still very messy. I wonder if he always lives like this, like a teenage boy? Then I remember he lost his family when he was a teenager, so maybe that's why. I feel sorry for him. He comes from the kitchen and sits close to me. If I could, I would come here every day, for just an hour or two. I'd just sit here, drink wine, and feel his hand touch mine.

But I can't. There's a reason for this, and he wants me to get to it.

"Okay, Megan," he says. "Do you feel ready now? To finish telling me what you were saying before?"

I lean back a little against him, against his warm body. He lets me. I close my eyes, and it's easy to go back there, back to the bathroom. It's strange, because I've tried so hard not to think about it, about those days and nights. But now I can close my eyes and it's like falling asleep, right into the middle of a dream.

It was dark and very cold. I wasn't in the bath anymore. "I don't know exactly what happened. I remember waking up, knowing something was wrong. Then, the next thing I knew, Mac was home. He was calling for me. I could hear him downstairs, shouting my name, but I couldn't move. I was sitting on the bathroom floor, she was in my arms. The rain was loud, and the roof was creaking. I was so cold. Mac came up the stairs, still calling my name. He came to the doorway and turned on the light." I can feel it now, the light hurting my eyes, everything bright and white, and scary.

"I remember yelling at him to turn the light off. I didn't want to see, I didn't want to look at her like that. I don't know—I don't know what happened then. He was yelling at me, screaming in my face. I gave her to him and ran. I ran out of the house into the rain, I ran to the beach. I don't remember what happened after that. It was a long time before he came for me. It was still raining. I was in the sand dunes, I think. I thought about going in the water, but I was too scared. He came for me eventually. He took me home."

We buried her in the morning. I wrapped her in a sheet, and Mac dug the hole. We put her at the edge of our land, near the old train tracks. We put rocks on top to mark the spot. We didn't talk about it, or anything else. We didn't even look at each other. That night, Mac left. He said he had to meet someone. I thought he might go to the police. I didn't know what to do. I just waited for him, for someone to come. He didn't come back. He never came back.

I'm sitting in Kamal's warm living room, his warm body next to me, and I'm shaking. "I can still feel it," I tell him. "At night, I can still feel it. It's what I'm scared of, what keeps me awake: the feeling of being alone in that house. I was so scared—too scared to sleep. I'd just walk around the dark rooms and hear her crying, smell her skin. I saw things. I'd wake up and think someone else—something else—was in the house with me. I thought I was going crazy. I thought I was going to die. I thought maybe I'd just stay there, and someone would find me one day. At least that way I wouldn't have left her."

I sniff and reach for a tissue from the box on the table. Kamal's hand goes down my back and rests there.

"But I didn't have the courage to stay. I think I waited about ten days, and then there was no food left—not even a can of beans. I packed my things and left."

"Did you see Mac again?"

"No, never. The last time I saw him was that night. He didn't kiss me or even say goodbye. He just said he had to go out for a bit." I shrug. "That was it."

"Did you try to call him?"

I shook my head. "No. I was too scared at first. I didn't know what he would do if I got in touch. And I didn't know where he was—he didn't even have a cell phone. I lost contact with the people who knew him. His friends were all like travelers. A few months ago, after we talked about him, I looked him up online. But I couldn't find him. It's strange..."

"What is?"

"At first, I used to see him all the time. Like, on the street, or I'd see a man in a bar and think it was him, and my heart would race. I used to hear his voice in crowds. But that stopped a long time ago. Now, I think he might be dead."

"Why do you think that?"

"I don't know. He just... he feels like he's not alive to me."

Kamal sits up and moves slightly away from me. He turns to face me.

"I think that's probably just your mind playing tricks, Megan. It's normal to think you see people who were important to you after you stop seeing them. When it first happened, I used to think I saw my brother all the time. As for him 'feeling dead,' that's probably just because he's been gone from your life for so long. In a way, he doesn't feel real to you anymore."

He's acting like a therapist now, not just a friend sitting on the couch. I want to pull him closer, but I don't want to do anything wrong. I remember the last time, when I kissed him before I left—the look on his face, wanting something, but also frustrated and angry.

"I wonder if, now that we've talked about this, now that you've told me your story, it might help if you tried to contact Mac. To help you move on, to finish that part of your life."

I thought he might say that. "I can't," I say. "I can't."

"Just think about it for a little bit."

"I can't. What if he still hates me? What if it just makes everything bad again, or if he tells the police?" What if—I can't say this, can't even whisper it—what if he tells Scott what I really am?

Kamal shakes his head. "Maybe he doesn't hate you at all, Megan. Maybe he never hated you. Maybe he was scared, too. Maybe he feels bad. From what you've told me, he didn't act like a responsible person. He took in a very young, very helpless girl and left her alone when she needed help. Maybe he knows that what happened is both of your faults. Maybe that's why he ran away."

I don't know if he really thinks that or if he's just trying to make me feel better. I only know it's not true. I can't blame him. This is my responsibility.

"I don't want to force you to do something you don't want to do," Kamal says. "I just want you to think about how talking to Mac might help you. And it's not because I think you owe him anything. Do you understand? I think he owes you. I know you feel guilty, I do. But he left you. You were alone, scared, worried, and sad. He left you by yourself in that house. It's no wonder you can't sleep. Of course, the idea of sleeping scares you: you fell asleep, and something bad happened to you. And the one person who should have helped you left you alone."

When Kamal says these things, they don't sound so bad. His words are smooth and comforting, and I almost believe them. I almost believe I can leave all this behind, forget it, go home to Scott, and live a normal life, not looking back or waiting for something better. Is that what normal people do?

"Will you think about it?" he asks, touching my hand. I smile and say I will. Maybe I even mean it, I don't know. He walks me to the door, his arm around my shoulders. I want to kiss him again, but I don't.

Instead, I ask, "Is this the last time I'll see you?" and he nods. "Could we...?"

"No, Megan. We can't. We have to do what's right."

I smile at him. "I'm not very good at that," I say. "I never have been."

"You can be. You will be. Go home now. Go home to your husband."

I stand on the sidewalk outside his house for a long time after he closes the door. I feel lighter, I think, and freer—but also sadder, and suddenly I just want to go home to Scott.

I'm about to walk to the station when a man comes running down the sidewalk, with headphones on and his head down. He's coming right at me, and as I step back to get out of his way, I slip off the edge of the sidewalk and fall.

The man doesn't say sorry or even glance at me. I'm too surprised to yell. I stand up, leaning on a car, trying to breathe normally. The calm I felt at Kamal's house is now gone.

When I get home, I see that I cut my hand when I fell. I must have touched my mouth with my hand because my lips have blood on them.

RACHEL

• • •

SATURDAY, AUGUST 10, 2013

MORNING

I wake up early. I hear the recycling truck going down the street and the soft rain hitting the window. The blinds are partly open—we didn't close them last night. I smile. I feel him behind me, warm and sleepy, and hard. I move my hips, pressing closer to him. He will wake up soon, grab me, and turn me over.

“Rachel,” he says, “don’t.” I feel cold. I'm not at home, this isn't home. This is wrong.

I turn over. Scott is sitting up now. He puts his legs over the side of the bed, his back to me. I close my eyes tightly and try to remember, but it's all unclear. When I open my eyes, I can think clearly because this room is the one I've woken up in many times: the bed is here, this is the view—if I sit up, I can see the tops of the oak trees across the street; over there, on the left, is the bathroom, and to the right are the closets. It's just like the room I shared with Tom.

“Rachel,” he says again, and I reach out to touch his back, but he stands up quickly and turns to face me. He looks empty, like the first time I saw him up close, at the police station—as if someone has taken out his insides, leaving only a shell. This is like the room I shared with Tom, but it is the one he shared with Megan. This room, this bed.

“I know,” I say. “I’m sorry. I’m so sorry. This was a mistake.”

“Yes, it was,” he says, not looking at me. He goes into the bathroom and closes the door.

I lie back and close my eyes and feel myself sink into fear, that awful twisting in my stomach. What have I done? I remember him talking a lot when I first got here, a flood of words. He was angry—angry at his mother, who never liked Megan; angry at the newspapers for what they wrote about her, suggesting she deserved what happened; angry at the police for messing up the case, for failing her, failing him. We sat in the kitchen drinking beers and I listened to him talk, and when the beers were gone we sat outside on the patio and he stopped being angry. We drank and watched the trains go by and talked about normal things: TV and work and where he went to school, just like regular people. I forgot to feel what I was supposed to be feeling, we both did, because I remember it now. I remember him smiling at me, touching my hair.

It hits me hard, I can feel my face getting hot. I remember admitting it to myself. Thinking the thought and not pushing it away, accepting it. I wanted it. I wanted to be with Jason. I wanted to feel what Jess felt when she sat out there with him, drinking wine in the evening. I forgot what I was supposed to be feeling. I ignored the fact that at best, Jess is just something I made up, and at worst, Jess is not nothing, she is Megan—she is dead, a body hurt and left to rot. Worse than that: I didn’t forget. I didn’t care. I didn’t care because I’ve started to believe what they’re saying about her. Did I, for even a moment, think she deserved what happened, too?

Scott comes out of the bathroom. He’s taken a shower, washed me off his skin. He looks better, but he won’t look me in the eye when he asks if I want coffee. This isn’t what I wanted: none of this is right. I don’t want to do this. I don’t want to lose control again.

I get dressed quickly and go into the bathroom, splash cold water on my face. My mascara is smeared, dark at the corners of my eyes, and my lips are dark. Bitten. My face and neck are red where his beard has rubbed my skin. I quickly remember last night, his hands on me, and my stomach flips. Feeling dizzy, I sit down on the edge of the bathtub. The bathroom is dirtier than the rest of the house: dirt around the sink, toothpaste on the mirror. A mug, with only one toothbrush in it. There’s no perfume, no lotion, no makeup. I wonder if she took it when she left, or if he’s thrown it all away.

Back in the bedroom, I look around for signs of her—a robe on the door, a hairbrush on the dresser, a lip balm, earrings—but there’s nothing. I walk to the closet and am about to open it, my hand on the handle, when I hear him call out, “Coffee’s ready!” and I jump.

He hands me the mug without looking at me, then turns away and stands with his back to me, looking at the train tracks or something else. I look to my right and see that the pictures are gone, all of them. I feel a tingle on my scalp, the hairs on my arms standing up. I sip my coffee and have trouble swallowing. None of this is right.

Maybe his mother did it: took everything away, took the pictures. His mother didn’t like Megan, he’s said that many times. Still, who does what he did last night? Who sleeps with a stranger in the bed where his wife slept when she’s been dead less than a month? He turns then, he looks at me, and I feel like he knows what I’m thinking because he has a strange look on his face—disgust, or hate—and I’m disgusted by him, too. I put the mug down.

“I should go,” I say, and he doesn’t argue.

The rain has stopped. It’s bright outside, and I’m squinting in the hazy morning sun. A man comes up to me—he’s right in my face as soon as I’m on the sidewalk. I put my hands up, turn sideways and push him out of the way. He’s saying something but I don’t hear it. I keep my hands up and my head down, so I’m very close to her when I see Anna, standing by her car, hands on her hips, watching me. When she sees me she shakes her head, turns away and walks quickly towards her own front door, almost running. I stand still for a second, watching her small figure in black pants and a red shirt. I feel like I’ve seen this before. I’ve watched her run away like this before.

It was right after I moved out. I’d come to see Tom, to get something I’d left behind. I don’t even remember what it was, it wasn’t important, I just wanted to go to the house, to see him. I think it was a Sunday, and I’d moved out on Friday, so I’d been gone about two days. I stood in the street and watched her carrying things from a car into the house. She was moving in, two days after I’d left, my bed still warm. Talk about being too quick. She saw me and I went towards her. I don’t know what I was going to say to her—nothing smart, I’m sure. I was crying, I remember that. And she, like now, ran away. I didn’t know the worst of it then—she wasn’t showing yet. Thank goodness. I think it might have killed me.

I'm on the platform, waiting for the train, and I feel dizzy. I sit on the bench and think it's just a hangover. I haven't had a drink in five days, then I drank too much, that's all. But I know it's more than that. It's about Anna. Seeing her and how I felt when she walked away like that. It was fear.

ANNA

• • •

SATURDAY, AUGUST 10, 2013

MORNING

This morning, I drove to the gym in Northcote for my spin class. Afterward, I stopped at the Matches store and bought a cute Max Mara mini dress (Tom will like it when he sees me in it). I was having a great morning, but when I parked the car, there was a lot of noise outside the Hipwells' house. There are always photographers there now. And there she was. Again! I couldn't believe it. Rachel, rushing past a photographer, looking messy. I think she had just left Scott's house.

I wasn't even upset. I was just shocked. When I told Tom about it calmly, he was just as confused as I was.

"I'll talk to her," he said. "I'll find out what's happening."

"You've tried that," I said gently. "It doesn't change anything." I suggested we should get legal advice, maybe get a restraining order.

"But she's not really bothering us, is she?" he said. "The phone calls have stopped, she hasn't come near us or the house. Don't worry, honey. I'll handle it."

He's right, she's not really harassing us. But I don't care. Something is wrong, and I'm not going to ignore it. I'm tired of being told not to worry. I'm tired of him saying he'll handle it, that he'll talk to her, that she'll eventually leave. I think it's time for me to do something myself. The next time I see her, I'm calling that police officer—the woman, Detective Riley. She seemed nice and understanding. I know Tom feels bad for Rachel, but I think it's time I dealt with her myself.## MONDAY, AUGUST 12, 2013

MORNING

We're in the parking lot at Wilton Lake. We used to come here sometimes to swim on very hot days. Today, we're just sitting next to each other in Tom's car, with the windows open, letting the warm air in. I want to lean my head back, close my eyes, smell the pine trees, and listen to the birds. I want to hold his hand and stay here all day.

He called me last night and asked if we could meet. I asked if it was about seeing Anna on Blenheim Road. I said it had nothing to do with them—I wasn't there to bother them. He believed me, or at least he said he did, but he still seemed worried and a little nervous. He said he needed to talk to me.

"Please, Rach," he said, and the way he said it, like the old days, made my heart feel like it would burst. "I'll come and pick you up, okay?"

I woke up before the sun came up and was making coffee at five. I washed my hair, shaved my legs, put on makeup, and changed my clothes four times. And I felt bad. I know it's silly, but I thought about Scott—about what we did and how it felt—and I wished I hadn't done it, because it felt like I was being unfaithful to Tom. The man who left me for another woman two years ago. I can't help how I feel.

Tom arrived a little before nine. I went downstairs, and he was there, leaning on his car, wearing jeans and an old gray T-shirt—so old that I remember exactly how the material felt against my cheek when I laid on his chest.

"I have the morning off work," he said when he saw me. "I thought we could go for a drive."

We didn't talk much on the drive to the lake. He asked how I was and said I looked good. He didn't mention Anna until we were sitting in the parking lot and I was thinking about holding his hand.

"Yeah, um, Anna said she saw you... and she thought you might have been coming from Scott Hipwell's house. Is that right?" He turned to face me, but he wasn't really looking at me. He seemed almost shy to ask me the question.

"You don't have to worry about it," I told him. "I've been seeing Scott... I mean, not like that, not dating him. We've become friends. That's all. It's hard to explain. I've just been helping him out a bit. You know—of course you know—that he's been having a very hard time."

Tom nodded, but he still didn't look at me. Instead, he chewed on his left index finger, a clear sign that he was worried.

"But Rach..."

I wish he'd stop calling me that, because it made me feel dizzy, it made me want to smile. It had been so long since I'd heard him say my name like that, and it was making me hope. Maybe things weren't going well with Anna, maybe he remembered some of the good things about us, maybe a part of him missed me.

"I'm just... I'm really worried about this."

He finally looked up at me, his big brown eyes met mine, and he moved his hand a little, as if he was going to take mine, but then he changed his mind and stopped. "I know—well, I don't really know much about it, but Scott... I know that he seems like a nice guy, but you can't be sure, can you?"

"You think he did it?"

He shook his head and swallowed hard. "No, no. I'm not saying that. I know... Well, Anna says that they argued a lot. That Megan sometimes seemed a little scared of him."

"Anna said?" I want to ignore anything that woman says, but I can't forget how I felt at Scott's house on Saturday, that something was not right, something was wrong.

He nods. "Megan used to babysit for us when Evie was very small. It's awful to think about that now, after what's been in the news. But it shows that you think you know someone and then..." He sighs deeply. "I don't want anything bad to happen. To you." He smiles at me, then shrugs a little. "I still care about you, Rach," he says, and I have to look away because I don't want him to see my tears. He knows, of course, and he puts his hand on my shoulder and says, "I'm so sorry."

We sit quietly for a while. I bite my lip to stop myself from crying. I don't want to make this harder for him, I really don't.

"I'm okay, Tom. I'm getting better. I am."

"I'm really glad to hear that. You're not—"

"Drinking? Less. It's getting better."

"That's good. You look good. You look... pretty." He smiles at me and I can feel myself blush. He looks away quickly. "Are you... um... are you okay, you know, with money?"

"I'm fine."

"Really? Are you really, Rachel, because I don't want you to—"

"I'm okay."

"Will you take a little? I don't want to sound stupid, but will you just take a little? To help you get by?"

"Honestly, I'm fine."

He leans over, and I can barely breathe. I want to touch him so much. I want to smell his neck, put my face in the wide, strong space between his shoulders. He opens the glove box. "Let me write you a check, just in case, okay? You don't even have to use it."

I start laughing. "You still keep a checkbook in the glove box?"

He starts laughing, too. "You never know," he says.

"You never know when you'll have to help out your crazy ex-wife?"

He rubs his thumb on my cheek. I lift my hand and take his in mine and kiss his palm.

"Promise me," he says roughly, "you'll stay away from Scott Hipwell. Promise me, Rach."

"I promise," I say, and I mean it. I can hardly see because I'm so happy. I realize that he's not just worried about me, he's jealous.

TUESDAY, AUGUST 13, 2013

EARLY MORNING

I'm on the train, looking at a pile of clothes by the tracks. Dark-blue cloth. A dress, I think, with a black belt. I don't know how it got there. The train workers didn't leave it. We're moving very slowly, so I have time to look. It seems like I've seen that dress before, someone wearing it. I can't remember when. It's very cold. Too cold for a dress like that. I think it might snow soon.

I'm excited to see Tom's house—my house. I know he'll be there, sitting outside. I know he'll be alone, waiting for me. He'll stand up when we pass, he'll wave and smile. I know all of this.

But first, we stop in front of number fifteen. Jason and Jess are there, drinking wine on the porch, which is strange, because it's not even eight thirty in the morning. Jess is wearing a dress with red flowers, and small silver earrings with birds on them. I can see them moving as she talks. Jason is standing behind her, his hands on her shoulders. I smile at them. I want to wave, but I don't want people to think I'm strange. I just watch, and I wish I had a glass of wine, too.

We've been here for a long time and the train still isn't moving. I wish we'd go, because if we don't, Tom won't be there and I'll miss him. I can see Jess's face now, more clearly than usual. It's because of the light, which is very bright, shining right on her like a spotlight. Jason is still behind her, but his hands aren't on her shoulders now, they're on her neck, and she looks uncomfortable, upset. He's choking her. I can see her face turning red. She's crying. I stand up, I'm hitting the window and screaming at him to stop, but he can't hear me. Someone grabs my arm—the guy with the red hair. He tells me to sit down, says we're almost at the next stop.

"It'll be too late by then," I tell him, and he says, "It's already too late, Rachel," and when I look back at the porch, Jess is standing and Jason has a handful of her blond hair and he's going to hit her head against the wall.

MORNING

It's been hours since I woke up, but I'm still shaky. My legs are trembling as I sit down. I woke up from the dream feeling scared, like everything I thought I knew was wrong, that everything I'd seen—of Scott, of Megan—I'd made up, that none of it was real. But if my mind is playing tricks, isn't it more likely that the dream is fake? Those things Tom said to me in the car, mixed with guilt about what happened with Scott the other night: the dream was just my brain sorting all that out.

Still, that familiar feeling of dread grows when the train stops at the signal, and I'm almost too scared to look up. The window is closed, there's nothing there. It's quiet, peaceful. Or it's empty. Megan's chair is still on the porch, empty. It's warm today, but I can't stop shivering.

I have to remember that the things Tom said about Scott and Megan came from Anna, and I know better than anyone that she can't be trusted.

Dr. Abdic's greeting this morning seems a little weak to me. He's almost bent over, like he's in pain, and when he shakes my hand his grip is not as strong as before. I know that Scott said they wouldn't share any news about the pregnancy, but I wonder if they've told him. I wonder if he's thinking about Megan's child.

I want to tell him about the dream, but I can't think of a way to explain it without giving myself away, so instead I ask him about getting back memories, about hypnosis.

"Well," he says, putting his fingers flat on the desk, "some therapists think that hypnosis can be used to get back hidden memories, but it's very debated. I don't do it, and I don't suggest it to my patients. I'm not sure it helps, and sometimes I think it can be harmful." He gives me a small smile. "I'm sorry. I know this isn't what you want to hear. But with the mind, I think, there are no easy solutions."

"Do you know therapists who do this kind of thing?" I ask.

He shakes his head. "I'm sorry, but I couldn't suggest one. You have to remember that people under hypnosis are very open to suggestions. The memories that are 'found'"—he makes quote marks with his fingers—"cannot always be trusted. They are not real memories at all."

I can't risk it. I couldn't stand to have other images in my head, more memories that I can't trust, memories that mix and change, tricking me into believing that what is is not, telling me to look one way when I should be looking another way.

"So what do you suggest, then?" I ask him. "Is there anything I can do to try to get back what I've lost?"

He rubs his long fingers back and forth over his lips. "It's possible, yes. Just talking about a certain memory can help you to make things clearer, going over the details in a place where you feel safe and relaxed . . ."

"Like here, for example?"

He smiles. "Like here, if you do feel safe and relaxed here." His voice goes up, he's asking a question that I don't answer. The smile goes away. "Focusing on senses other than sight often helps. Sounds, the feel of things . . . smell is very important when it comes to remembering. Music can be powerful, too. If you are thinking of a certain situation, a certain day, you might think about going back to the place, returning to the scene, as it were." It's a common saying, but the hairs on the back of my neck are standing up, my head tingling. "Do you want to talk about a certain event, Rachel?"

I do, of course, but I can’t tell him that, so I tell him about the time with the golf club, when I hit Tom after we had a fight.

I remember waking up that morning feeling worried, knowing right away that something bad had happened. Tom wasn’t in bed with me, and I felt relieved. I lay on my back, thinking about it. I remembered crying and crying and telling him that I loved him. He was angry, telling me to go to bed; he didn’t want to hear it anymore.

I tried to think back to earlier that night, to where the argument started. We were having such a good time. I’d made grilled shrimp with lots of chili and cilantro, and we were drinking this tasty white wine that he’d been given by a happy customer. We ate outside on the patio, listening to the Killers and Kings of Leon, albums we used to play when we first started dating.

I remember us laughing and kissing. I remember telling him a story about something—he didn’t think it was as funny as I did. I remember feeling sad. Then I remember us yelling at each other, stumbling through the sliding doors as I went inside, being mad that he didn’t rush to help me up.

But here’s the thing: “When I got up that morning, I went downstairs. He wouldn’t talk to me, barely even looked at me. I had to beg him to tell me what I had done. I kept telling him how sorry I was. I was very worried. I can’t explain why, I know it doesn’t make sense, but if you can’t remember what you’ve done, your mind just makes up things and you think the worst possible things . . .”

Kamal nods. “I can imagine. Go on.”

“So finally, just to get me to stop talking, he told me. I had gotten upset about something he’d said, and then I kept going on about it, being annoying and mean, and I wouldn’t let it go, and he tried to get me to stop, he tried to kiss and make up, but I wouldn’t. And then he decided to just leave me, to go upstairs to bed, and that’s when it happened. I chased him up the stairs with a golf club in my hand and tried to hit him in the head. I missed, luckily. I just made a hole in the wall in the hall.”

Kamal’s face doesn’t change. He isn’t surprised. He just nods. “So, you know what happened, but you can’t really feel it, is that right? You want to be able to remember it yourself, to see it and feel it in your own memory, so that—how did you say it?—so that it belongs to you? And that way, you’ll feel fully responsible?”

“Well.” I shrug. “Yes. I mean, that’s part of it. But there’s something more. And it happened later, much later—weeks, maybe months after. I kept thinking about that night. Every time I walked past that hole in the wall I thought about it. Tom said he was going to fix it, but he didn’t, and I didn’t want to bother him about it. One day I was standing there—it was evening and I was coming out of the bedroom and I just stopped, because I remembered. I was on the floor, my back to the wall, crying and crying, Tom standing over me, asking me to calm down, the golf club on the carpet next to my feet, and I felt it, I felt it. I was very scared. The memory doesn’t match what really happened, because I don’t remember being angry, being very mad. I remember being scared.”

I've been thinking about what Kamal said, about going back to where something happened, so instead of going home I've come to Witney. Instead of quickly walking past the underpass, I walk slowly right up to it. I put my hands on the cold, rough brick at the entrance and close my eyes, feeling it with my fingers. Nothing comes to mind. I open my eyes and look around. The road is very quiet: just one woman walking towards me a little ways off, no one else. No cars driving by, no children yelling, just a very quiet siren far away. The sun goes behind a cloud and I feel cold, stuck at the entrance of the tunnel, unable to go any further. I turn to leave.

The woman I saw walking towards me a moment ago is just turning the corner; she's wearing a dark blue coat. She looks up at me as she passes and then I remember. A woman . . . blue . . . the way the light looked. I remember: Anna. She was wearing a blue dress with a black belt and was walking away from me, walking fast, almost like she did the other day, only this time she did look back, she looked over her shoulder and then she stopped. A car pulled up next to her on the sidewalk—a red car. Tom's car. She leaned down to talk to him through the window and then opened the door and got in, and the car drove away.

I remember that. On that Saturday night I stood here, at the entrance to the underpass, and watched Anna getting into Tom's car. But I can't be remembering right, because that doesn't make sense. Tom came to look for me in the car. Anna wasn't in the car with him—she was at home. That's what the police told me. It doesn't make sense, and I could scream because I'm so frustrated, not knowing, and my brain feels useless.

I cross the street and walk along the left side of Blenheim Road. I stand under the trees for a while, across from number twenty-three. They've painted the front door again. It was dark green when I lived there; it's black now. I don't remember noticing that before. I liked the green better. I wonder what else is different inside? The baby's room, of course, but I wonder if they still sleep in our bed, if she puts on her lipstick in front of the mirror that I hung. I wonder if they've repainted the kitchen, or fixed that hole in the wall upstairs.

I want to cross over and bang the knocker against the black paint. I want to talk to Tom, to ask him about the night Megan went missing. I want to ask him about yesterday, when we were in the car and I kissed his hand, I want to ask him what he felt. Instead, I just stand there for a bit, looking up at my old bedroom window until I feel tears welling up, and I know it's time to go.

ANNA

• • •

TUESDAY, AUGUST 13, 2013

MORNING

I watched Tom getting ready for work this morning, putting on his shirt and tie. He seemed a little distracted, probably thinking about his schedule for the day—meetings, appointments, who, what, where. I felt jealous. For the first time ever, I actually wanted to be him, getting dressed up and leaving the house and rushing around all day, with a purpose, all to get paid.

It’s not the work I miss—I was a real estate agent, not a brain surgeon, it’s not really a job you dream about as a kid—but I did like being able to walk around the really expensive houses when the owners weren’t there, touching the marble counters, sneaking a look into the big closets. I used to imagine what my life would be like if I lived like that, the kind of person I would be. I know that raising a child is very important, but the problem is that it's not valued. Not in the way that matters to me right now, which is money. I want us to have more money so that we can leave this house, this street. It’s that simple.

Maybe not quite that simple. After Tom left for work, I sat down at the kitchen table to argue with Evie about breakfast. Two months ago, she would eat anything. Now, if it’s not strawberry yogurt, she won’t eat it. I know this is normal. I keep telling myself this while I’m trying to get egg out of my hair, while I’m crawling around on the floor picking up spoons and bowls. I keep telling myself this is normal.

Still, when we were finally done and she was playing by herself, I let myself cry for a minute. I only cry when Tom’s not here, just a few moments to let it all out. It was when I was washing my face afterwards, when I saw how tired I looked, how messy and awful, that I felt it again—that need to put on a dress and high heels, to dry my hair and put on some makeup and walk down the street and have men look at me.

I miss work, but I also miss what work meant to me in my last year of working, when I met Tom. I miss being a mistress.

I enjoyed it. I loved it, actually. I never felt guilty. I pretended I did. I had to, with my married friends, the ones who are scared of the young nanny or the pretty, funny girl at work who can talk about sports and spends a lot of time at the gym. I had to tell them that of course I felt terrible about it, of course I felt bad for his wife, I never wanted any of this to happen, we fell in love, what could we do?

The truth is, I never felt sorry for Rachel, even before I knew about her drinking and how difficult she was, how she was making his life awful. She didn't seem real to me, and I was enjoying myself too much anyway. Being the other woman is exciting, there's no denying it: you're the one he can't help but cheat on his wife with, even though he loves her. That's how attractive you are.

I was selling a house. Number thirty-four Cranham Road. It was hard to sell, because the latest buyer couldn't get a loan. Something about the bank's check. So we decided to get another surveyor to check it, just to be sure everything was okay. The sellers had already left, the house was empty, so I had to be there to let him in.

It was clear from the moment I opened the door to him that it was going to happen. I'd never done anything like that before, never even thought about it, but there was something in the way he looked at me, the way he smiled at me. We couldn't stop ourselves—we did it there in the kitchen, against the counter. It was crazy, but that's how we were. That's what he always used to say to me. Don't expect me to be normal, Anna. Not with you.

I pick Evie up and we go out into the garden together. She's pushing her little cart around, laughing to herself as she does it, this morning's bad mood forgotten. Every time she smiles at me I feel like my heart's going to burst. No matter how much I miss working, I would miss this more. And it's never going to happen anyway. I'm not leaving her with a babysitter again, no matter how good they are. I'm not leaving her with anyone else ever again, not after Megan.

EVENING

Tom texted me to say he was going to be a bit late this evening, he had to take a client out for a drink. Evie and I were getting ready for our evening walk. We were in the bedroom, Tom's and mine, and I was changing her. The light was beautiful, a bright orange color filling the house, turning suddenly gray-blue when the sun went behind a cloud. I'd pulled the curtains halfway across to stop the room getting too hot, so I went to open them, and that's when I saw Rachel, standing on the other side of the road, looking at our house. Then she just left, walking back towards the station.

I'm sitting on the bed and I'm shaking with anger, digging my nails into my hands. Evie's kicking her feet in the air, and I'm so angry, I don't want to pick her up because I'm afraid I would hurt her.

He told me he'd fixed this. He told me that he called her, they talked, she admitted that she had become friends with Scott Hipwell, but that she wasn't going to see him anymore, that she wouldn't be hanging around anymore. Tom said she promised him, and that he believed her. Tom said she was being reasonable, she didn't seem drunk, she wasn't upset, she didn't threaten him or beg him to come back to her. He told me he thought she was getting better.

I take a few deep breaths and pull Evie up onto my lap, I lay her back against my legs and hold her hands with mine.

"I think I've had enough of this, don't you, sweetie?"

It's just so tiring: every time I think things are getting better, that we're finally done with the Rachel problem, there she is again. Sometimes I feel like she's never going to leave.

Deep inside me, a bad feeling has started. When Tom tells me it's OK, everything's alright, she's not going to bother us anymore, and then she does, I can't help wondering if he's trying his best to get rid of her, or if there's some part of him, deep down, that likes that she can't let go.

I go downstairs and search in the kitchen drawer for the card that Detective Riley left. I call her number quickly, before I have time to change my mind.

WEDNESDAY, AUGUST 14, 2013

MORNING

In bed, his hands on my hips, his breath hot against my neck, his skin sweaty against mine, he says, "We don't do this enough anymore."

"I know."

"We need to make more time for ourselves."

"We do."

"I miss you," he says. "I miss this. I want more of this."

I turn over and kiss him on the lips, my eyes closed tightly, trying to hide the guilt I feel for going to the police without him knowing.

“I think we should go somewhere,” he says quietly, “just the two of us. Get away for a while.”

And who would take care of Evie? I want to ask. Your parents, who you don't talk to? Or my mother, who is so weak, she can barely take care of herself?

I don't say that, I don't say anything, I just kiss him again, more intensely. His hand moves down to the back of my leg and he holds it tightly.

“What do you think? Where would you like to go? Mauritius? Bali?”

I laugh.

“I’m serious,” he says, pulling away from me, looking at me. “We deserve it, Anna. You deserve it. It’s been a tough year, hasn’t it?”

“But . . .”

“But what?” He smiles perfectly at me. “We’ll find a way to take care of Evie, don’t worry.”

“Tom, the money.”

"We'll be okay."

"But..." I don't want to say this, but I have to. "We don't have enough money to even think about moving, but we have enough money for a vacation in Mauritius or Bali?"

He puffs out his cheeks, then breathes out slowly, turning away from me. I shouldn't have said it. The baby monitor makes noise: Evie's waking up.

"I'll get her," he says, and gets up and leaves the room.

At breakfast, Evie is doing her usual thing. It's a game to her now, not eating, shaking her head, chin up, lips closed tight, her little fists pushing at the bowl in front of her. Tom gets impatient quickly.

"I don't have time for this," he says to me. "You'll have to do it." He stands up, holding out the spoon for me to take, his face looking upset.

I take a deep breath.

It's okay, he's tired, he has a lot of work, he's annoyed because I didn't agree with his vacation idea this morning.

But it isn't okay, because I'm tired, too, and I'd like to talk about money and our situation here without him just leaving the room. Of course, I don't say that. Instead, I break my promise to myself and I mention Rachel.

"She's been coming around again," I say, "so what you said to her the other day didn't work."

He looks at me sharply. "What do you mean, coming around?"

"She was here last night, standing in the street across from the house."

"Was she with anyone?"

"No. She was by herself. Why do you ask?"

"Damn it," he says, and his face gets dark like it does when he's really mad. "I told her to stay away. Why didn't you say anything last night?"

"I didn't want to make you upset," I say quietly, already wishing I hadn't said anything. "I didn't want to worry you."

"Jesus!" he says, and he drops his coffee cup loudly in the sink. The noise scares Evie, and she starts to cry. This makes things worse. "I don't know what to say, I really don't. When I talked to her, she was okay. She listened to me and promised not to come here anymore. She seemed okay. She seemed healthy, actually, back to normal—"

"She seemed okay?" I ask him, and before he turns away I can see in his face that he knows he's been caught. "I thought you said you talked to her on the phone?"

He takes a deep breath, sighs, then turns back to me, his face showing nothing. "Yeah, well, that's what I told you, honey, because I knew you'd get upset if I saw her. So I admit it—I lied. I just wanted things to be easy."

"Are you serious?"

He smiles at me, shaking his head as he walks towards me, his hands still up as if asking for forgiveness. "I'm sorry, I'm sorry. She wanted to talk in person and I thought it might be best. I'm sorry, okay? We just talked. We met in a bad coffee shop in Ashbury and talked for twenty minutes—maybe thirty minutes at most. Okay?"

He puts his arms around me and pulls me close. I try to push him away, but he's stronger than me, and he smells good, and I don't want to argue. I want us to agree. "I'm sorry," he says again, into my hair.

"It's okay," I say.

I let him get away with it, because I'm dealing with this now. I talked to Detective Riley last night, and I knew right away that I'd done the right thing by calling her. When I told her that I'd seen Rachel leaving Scott Hipwell's house "a few times" (a little bit of an exaggeration), she seemed very interested. She wanted to know dates and times (I could give her two; I was unclear about the other times), if they'd been together before Megan Hipwell disappeared, and if I thought they were having a sexual relationship now. I have to say I hadn't really thought about it—I can't imagine him going from Megan to Rachel. Also, his wife has barely been buried.

I also told her about Evie—the attempted kidnapping—just in case she'd forgotten.

"She's very unstable," I said. "You might think I'm overreacting, but I can't take any chances with my family."

"Not at all," she said. "Thank you for calling me. If you see anything else that seems suspicious, let me know."

I don't know what they'll do about her—maybe just warn her? It will help if we start looking into things like restraining orders. Hopefully, for Tom's sake, it won't come to that.

After Tom leaves for work, I take Evie to the park. We play on the swings and the little wooden rocking horses, and when I put her back in her stroller she falls asleep right away. This means it's time for me to go shopping. We go through the back streets towards the big Sainsbury's. It's a longer way to get there, but it's quiet, with not much traffic, and we get to go past number thirty-four Cranham Road.

It still makes me feel a little nervous walking past that house. I get butterflies in my stomach, and I smile and my cheeks turn red. I remember rushing up the front steps, hoping the neighbors wouldn't see me letting myself in. I'd get ready in the bathroom, put on perfume, and the kind of underwear you wear just to take off. Then I'd get a text message and he'd be at the door, and we'd spend an hour or two in the bedroom upstairs.

He'd tell Rachel he was with a client, or meeting friends for a beer. "Aren't you worried she'll find out?" I'd ask him, and he'd shake his head, ignoring the idea. "I'm a good liar," he once told me with a grin. Once, he said, "Even if she did find out, Rachel won't remember what happened tomorrow anyway." That's when I started to realize how bad things were for him.

But thinking about those conversations makes me stop smiling. Thinking about Tom laughing while he touched my belly, smiling at me, saying, "I'm a good liar." He is a good liar, it comes naturally to him. I've seen him do it: convincing hotel staff that we were on our honeymoon, or getting out of extra work hours by saying there was a family emergency. Everyone lies sometimes, but when Tom does it, you believe him.

I think about breakfast this morning—but the point is that I caught him in a lie, and he admitted it right away. I don't have anything to worry about. He isn't seeing Rachel behind my back! That's silly. She might have been attractive once—she was very pretty when he met her, I've seen pictures: with big dark eyes and a curvy figure—but now she's just overweight. And he would never go back to her, not after everything she did to him, to us—all the bothering, all those late-night phone calls, hang-ups, text messages.

I'm standing in the canned food aisle, Evie is still sleeping in the stroller, and I start thinking about those phone calls, and about the time—or maybe times?—when I woke up and the bathroom light was on. I could hear his voice, quiet and gentle, behind the closed door. He was calming her down, I know he was. He told me that sometimes she'd be so angry, she'd threaten to come to the house, go to his work, or hurt herself. He might be a very good liar, but I know when he's telling the truth. He doesn't fool me.

EVENING

But, thinking about it, he did fool me, didn't he? When he told me that he'd spoken to Rachel on the phone, that she sounded fine, better, almost happy, I believed him completely. And when he came home on Monday night and I asked him about his day and he told me about a boring meeting that morning, I listened without suspecting that there was no meeting, that he was in a coffee shop in Ashbury with his ex-wife.

This is what I'm thinking about while I'm carefully unloading the dishwasher, because Evie is sleeping and the noise of the silverware might wake her up. He does fool me. I know he's not always completely honest about everything. I think about that story about his parents—how he invited them to the wedding but they didn't come because they were so angry with him for leaving Rachel. I always thought that was strange, because when I've talked to his mom she seemed so happy to talk to me. She was nice, and interested in me and Evie.

"I hope we can see her soon," she said, but when I told Tom about it he didn't care.

"She's trying to get me to invite them over," he said, "just so she can say no. It's a power game." She didn't seem like someone playing power games to me, but I didn't argue. Other people's families are always hard to understand. He'll have his reasons for keeping them away, I know he will, and they'll be about protecting me and Evie.

So why am I wondering now if that was true? It's this house, this situation, all the things that have been happening here—they're making me doubt myself, doubt us. If I'm not careful, they'll make me crazy, and I'll end up like her. Like Rachel.

I'm just sitting here, waiting to take the sheets out of the dryer. I think about turning on the TV to see if there's an episode of Friends on that I haven't watched three hundred times. I think about doing my yoga, and I think about the book on my nightstand, which I've only read twelve pages of in the last two weeks. I think about Tom's laptop, which is on the coffee table in the living room.

And then I do things I never thought I would. I grab the bottle of red wine we opened last night with dinner and pour myself a glass. Then I get his laptop, turn it on, and start trying to guess the password.

I'm doing the things she did: drinking alone and looking through his things. The things she did and he hated. But recently—as recently as this morning—things have changed. If he's going to lie, then I'm going to check up on him. That's fair, right? I feel like I deserve some fairness. So I try to guess the password. I try names in different ways: mine and his, his and Evie's, mine and Evie's, all three of us together, forwards and backwards. Our birthdays, in different ways. Anniversaries: the first time we met, the first time we had sex. Number thirty-four, for Cranham Road; number twenty-three, this house. I try to think differently—most men use football teams as passwords, I think, but Tom doesn't like football; he likes cricket, so I try Boycott and Botham and Ashes. I don't know any of the recent names. I finish my glass and pour another half. I'm actually enjoying myself, trying to solve the puzzle. I think of bands he likes, movies he enjoys, actresses he finds attractive. I type password; I type 1234.

There's a loud screeching outside as the London train stops at the signal, like nails on a chalkboard. I clench my teeth and take another long drink of wine. As I do, I see the time—it's almost seven and Evie's still sleeping, and he'll be home any minute. I'm literally thinking that he'll be home any minute when I hear the key in the door, and my heart stops.

I quickly close the laptop and jump up, knocking my chair over. Evie wakes up and starts to cry. I put the computer back on the table before he comes into the room, but he knows something's wrong. He just stares at me and says, "What's going on?" I tell him, "Nothing, nothing, I knocked over a chair by mistake." He picks Evie up to hug her, and I see myself in the hallway mirror. My face is pale, and my lips are dark red from the wine.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 15, 2013

MORNING

Cathy got me a job interview. A friend of hers started her own public relations business and needs a helper. It's mostly a secretary job and doesn't pay much, but I don't mind. This woman will meet me without checking my work history—Cathy told her I had a mental health problem but am better now. The interview is tomorrow afternoon at her house—she works from an office in her backyard—which is in Witney. So, I was going to spend the day improving my resume and interview skills. I was—but then Scott called me.

"I wanted to talk," he said.

"We don't need to... I mean, you don't need to say anything. It was... We both know it was a mistake."

"I know," he said, sounding sad, not like the angry Scott I remember, but like the sad one who sat on my bed and told me about his child who died. "But I really want to talk to you."

"Of course," I said. "Of course we can talk."

"In person?"

“Oh,” I said. I really didn't want to go back to that house. “I’m sorry, I can’t today.”

“Please, Rachel? It’s important.” He sounded like he was in trouble, and I felt sorry for him even though I didn't want to. I was trying to think of a reason not to go when he asked again. “Please?” So I said yes, and I wished I hadn't as soon as I said it.

There's a story in the newspapers about Megan's child—her first child who died. Actually, it's about the child's father. They found him. His name is Craig McKenzie, and he died from a drug overdose in Spain four years ago. So he can't be involved. It never seemed like a good reason to me anyway—if someone wanted to hurt her for what she did back then, they would have done it a long time ago.

So who could it be? It could be the people you would expect: the husband, the boyfriend. Scott, Kamal. Or some random man who grabbed her from the street—a killer who is just starting out? Will she be the first of many, like Wilma McCann, or Pauline Reade? And who says the killer has to be a man? She was a small woman, Megan Hipwell. Very small, like a bird. It wouldn't take much strength to hurt her.

AFTERNOON

The first thing I notice when he opens the door is the smell. It smells like sweat and beer, bad and sour, and something else underneath, something worse. Something rotting. He's wearing sweatpants and a dirty grey T-shirt, his hair is greasy, and his skin is shiny, like he has a fever.

“Are you okay?” I ask him, and he smiles at me. He's been drinking.

“I’m fine, come in, come in.” I don't want to, but I do.

The curtains on the side of the house facing the street are closed, and the living room is filled with a reddish light that seems to fit the heat and the smell.

Scott walks into the kitchen, opens the fridge, and takes out a beer.

"Come and sit down," he says. "Have a drink." The smile on his face is stuck, not happy, but harsh. There's something mean about how his face looks. The dislike I saw on Saturday morning, after we slept together, is still there.

"I can't stay long," I tell him. "I have a job interview tomorrow, I need to get ready."

"Really?" He raises his eyebrows. He sits down and pushes a chair out towards me. "Sit down and have a drink," he says, like an order, not a nice offer. I sit down across from him and he pushes the beer bottle towards me. I pick it up and take a sip. Outside, I can hear loud noises—kids playing in a backyard somewhere—and beyond that, the quiet and familiar sound of the train.

"They got the DNA results yesterday," Scott says to me. "Detective Riley came to see me last night." He waits for me to say something, but I'm scared of saying the wrong thing, so I stay quiet. "It's not mine. It wasn't mine. The funny thing is, it wasn't Kamal's, either." He laughs. "So she was seeing someone else. Can you believe it?" He's smiling that awful smile. "You didn't know anything about that, did you? About another guy? She didn't tell you about another man, did she?" The smile is fading from his face and I'm getting a bad feeling about this, a very bad feeling. I stand up and take a step towards the door, but he's there in front of me, his hands grabbing my arms, and he pushes me back into the chair.

"Sit the fuck down." He grabs my bag from my shoulder and throws it into the corner of the room.

"Scott, I don't know what's going on—"

"Come on!" he shouts, leaning over me. "You and Megan were such good friends! You must have known about all her boyfriends!"

He knows. And as I think that, he must see it in my face because he leans in closer, his breath smelling bad in my face, and says, "Come on, Rachel. Tell me."

I shake my head and he swings a hand out, hitting the beer bottle in front of me. It falls off the table and breaks on the floor.

"You never even fucking met her!" he yells. "Everything you said to me—everything was a lie."

I lower my head and stand up, saying quietly, “I’m sorry, I’m sorry.” I try to walk around the table to get my purse and phone, but he grabs my arm again.

“Why did you do this?” he asks. “What made you do this? What is wrong with you?”

He looks at me, his eyes staring into mine, and I’m scared of him. But I also know that his question makes sense. I need to explain. So I don’t pull my arm away. I let him hold my arm tightly and I try to speak clearly and calmly. I try not to cry or panic.

“I wanted you to know about Kamal,” I tell him. “I saw them together, like I said, but you wouldn’t have believed me if I was just some girl on the train. I needed—”

“You needed!” He lets go of me and turns away. “You’re telling me what you needed . . .” His voice is quieter, he’s calming down. I take a deep breath, trying to slow my heart.

“I wanted to help you,” I say. “I knew that the police always think the husband did it, and I wanted you to know—to know there was someone else . . .”

“So you made up a story about knowing my wife? Do you know how crazy that sounds?”

“Yes, I do.”

I walk to the kitchen counter to get a cloth, then get on my hands and knees and clean up the spilled beer. Scott sits with his elbows on his knees and his head down. “She wasn’t who I thought she was,” he says. “I don’t know who she was.”

I squeeze the water out of the cloth over the sink and run cold water over my hands. My purse is nearby, in the corner of the room. I start to go get it, but Scott looks up at me, so I stop. I stand there with my back to the counter, holding the edge to stay steady and feel better.

"Detective Riley told me," he says. "She was asking me about you. If I was going out with you." He laughs. "Going out with you! Jesus. I asked her, 'Have you seen what my wife looked like? My standards aren't that low.'" My face is hot, I'm sweating under my arms and at the bottom of my back. "It seems Anna has been complaining about you. She's seen you around. So that's how it came up. I said, 'We're not going out, she's just an old friend of Megan's, she's helping me.'" He laughs again, quietly and without humor. "She said, 'She doesn't know Megan. She's just a sad little liar with no life.'" The smile left his face. "You're all liars. Every single one of you."

My phone makes a sound. I move towards the bag, but Scott gets there first.

"Wait a minute," he says, picking it up. "We're not done yet." He dumps my bag onto the table: phone, wallet, keys, lipstick, tampons, credit card slips. "I want to know how much of what you told me was a lie." He picks up the phone and looks at the screen. He looks at me, his eyes are suddenly cold. He reads out loud: "This confirms your appointment with Dr. Abdic at four thirty P.M. on Monday, August nineteenth. If you can't make it, please let us know twenty-four hours before."

"Scott—"

"What is going on?" he asks, his voice rough. "What have you been doing? What have you been saying to him?"

"I haven't been saying anything..." He drops the phone on the table and walks towards me, his hands in fists. I move back into the corner of the room, between the wall and the glass door. "I was trying to find out... I was trying to help." He raises his hand and I flinch, lowering my head, waiting for the pain. I know I've felt this before, but I can't remember when and I don't have time to think about it now. He hasn't hit me, but he's put his hands on my shoulders and is holding them tightly, his thumbs digging into my collarbones, and it hurts so much I cry out.

"All this time," he says through his teeth, "all this time I thought you were on my side, but you were against me. You were giving him information, weren't you? Telling him things about me, about Megs. It was you, trying to get the police to come after me. It was you—"

"No. Please don't. It wasn't like that. I wanted to help you." His right hand goes up, he grabs my hair at the back of my neck and twists. "Scott, please don't. Please. You're hurting me. Please." He's pulling me now, towards the front door. I feel relieved. He's going to throw me out into the street. Thank God.

But he doesn't throw me out, he keeps pulling me, yelling and swearing. He's taking me upstairs and I'm trying to stop him, but he's so strong, I can't. I'm crying, "Please don't. Please," and I know something bad is going to happen. I try to scream, but I can't, the sound won't come.

I can't see because of tears and fear. He pushes me into a room and slams the door. The key turns in the lock. I feel sick and throw up on the carpet. I wait, I listen. Nothing happens, and no one comes.

I'm in the spare room. In my house, this room used to be Tom's office. Now it's their baby's room, the room with the soft pink blind. Here, it's a small room, filled with papers and files, a fold-up treadmill and an old Apple Mac. There is a box of papers with numbers—maybe accounts from Scott's business—and another filled with old postcards—blank ones, with bits of sticky tack on the back, as if they were once stuck onto a wall: the roofs of Paris, children skateboarding in an alley, old wooden railway ties covered in moss, a view of the sea from inside a cave. I look through the postcards—I don't know why or what I'm looking for, I'm just trying to stay calm. I'm trying not to think about that news report, Megan's body being pulled out of the mud. I'm trying not to think of her injuries, of how scared she must have been when she saw it coming.

I'm searching through the postcards, and then something cuts me and I jump back with a shout. The tip of my finger is cut across the top, and blood is dripping onto my jeans. I stop the blood with the bottom of my T-shirt and look more carefully through the cards. I see what did it right away: a framed picture, broken, with a piece of glass missing from the top, the sharp edge covered with my blood.

It's not a photo I've seen before. It's a picture of Megan and Scott together, their faces close to the camera. She's laughing, and he's looking at her lovingly. Or is it jealousy? The glass is broken in a star shape from the corner of Scott's eye, so it's hard to see his expression. I sit there on the floor with the picture in front of me and think about how things get broken all the time by accident, and how sometimes you just don't fix them. I think about all the plates that were smashed when I argued with Tom, about that hole in the wall in the hallway upstairs.

Somewhere on the other side of the locked door, I can hear Scott laughing, and my whole body goes cold. I quickly get to my feet and go to the window, open it and lean right out, then with just the tips of my toes on the ground, I shout for help. I call out for Tom. It's hopeless. Sad. Even if he was, by some chance, out in the garden a few doors down, he wouldn't hear me, it's too far away. I look down and lose my balance, then pull myself back inside, feeling sick, sobs catching in my throat.

"Please, Scott!" I call out. "Please..." I hate the sound of my voice, the begging tone, the desperation. I look down at my bloody T-shirt and I remember that I have other choices. I pick up the photo frame and turn it over onto the carpet. I choose the longest piece of glass and put it carefully into my back pocket.

I can hear footsteps coming up the stairs. I move back against the wall opposite the door. The key turns in the lock.

Scott has my bag in one hand and throws it at my feet. In the other hand he is holding a piece of paper. "Well, if it isn't Nancy Drew!" he says with a smile. He puts on a silly voice and reads aloud: "Megan has run off with her boyfriend, who from now on, I will call B." He laughs quietly. "B has hurt her... Scott has hurt her..." He crumples up the paper and throws it at my feet. "Jesus Christ. You really are sad, aren't you?" He looks around, seeing the sick on the floor, the blood on my T-shirt. "What the hell have you been doing? Trying to kill yourself? Going to do my job for me?" He laughs again. "I should break your neck, but you know what, you're just not worth the trouble." He stands to one side. "Get out of my house."

I grab my bag and go to the door, but just as I do, he steps out in front of me like a boxer, and for a moment I think he's going to stop me, touch me again. There must be fear in my eyes because he starts to laugh, he laughs loudly. I can still hear him when I slam the front door behind me.

FRIDAY, AUGUST 16, 2013

MORNING

I barely slept. I drank a bottle and a half of wine to try to fall asleep, to stop my hands from shaking, to calm my jumpiness, but it didn't really help. Every time I started to doze off, I'd wake up suddenly. I felt like I could feel him in the room with me. I turned the light on and sat there, listening to the street noises and people moving around in the building. I only relaxed enough to sleep when it started to get light. I dreamed I was in the woods again. Tom was with me, but I still felt scared.

I left Tom a note last night. After I left Scott's, I ran to number twenty-three and banged on the door. I was so panicked, I didn't even care if Anna was there or if she'd be angry at me for showing up. No one answered, so I wrote a note on a piece of paper and pushed it through the mail slot. I don't care if she sees it—I think I actually want her to see it. I kept the note simple—I told him we needed to talk about the other day. I didn't say Scott's name, because I didn't want Tom to go there and fight him—who knows what might happen.

I called the police almost as soon as I got home. I had a couple of glasses of wine first to calm down. I asked to talk to Detective Inspector Gaskill, but they said he wasn't there, so I talked to Riley instead. It wasn't what I wanted—I know Gaskill would have been nicer.

"He trapped me in his house," I told her. "He threatened me."

She asked how long I was "trapped" for. I could hear her using air quotes over the phone.

"I don't know," I said. "Maybe half an hour."

There was a long pause.

"And he threatened you. Can you tell me exactly what he said?"

"He said he'd break my neck. He said... he said he should break my neck..."

"He should break your neck?"

"He said he would if he felt like it."

Silence. Then, "Did he hit you? Did he hurt you at all?"

"Bruises. Just bruises."

"He hit you?"

"No, he grabbed me."

More silence.

Then: "Ms. Watson, why were you at Scott Hipwell's house?"

"He asked me to come see him. He said he needed to talk to me."

She sighed. "You were told to stay away from this. You've been lying to him, saying you were a friend of his wife, you've been telling many stories and—let me finish—this is a person who, at the very least, is under a lot of stress and is very upset. At the very least. At worst, he could be dangerous."

"He is dangerous, that's what I'm telling you, for goodness sake."

"This isn't helpful—you going over there, lying to him, making him angry. We're in the middle of a murder investigation here. You need to understand that. You could mess up our progress, you could—"

"What progress?" I said sharply. "You haven't made any progress at all. He killed his wife, I'm telling you. There's a picture, a photo of the two of them—it's broken. He's angry, he's not stable—"

"Yes, we saw the photo. The house has been searched. It's not really proof of murder."

"So you're not going to arrest him?"

She sighed deeply. "Come to the station tomorrow. Give a statement. We'll go from there. And Ms. Watson? Stay away from Scott Hipwell."

Cathy came home and found me drinking. She wasn't happy. What could I tell her? There was no way to explain it. I just said I was sorry and went upstairs to my room, like a teenager being moody. And then I lay awake, trying to sleep, waiting for Tom to call. He didn't.

I wake up early, check my phone (no calls), wash my hair and get dressed for my interview, hands shaking, stomach upset. I'm leaving early because I have to stop at the police station first, to give them my statement. Not that I think it will help. They never took me seriously and they certainly aren't going to start now. I wonder what it would take for them to see me as anything other than someone who makes things up.

On the way to the station I can't stop looking behind me; the loud sound of a police siren makes me jump in fear. On the station platform I walk as close to the railings as I can, my fingers touching the iron fence, just in case I need to hold on tight. I know it's silly, but I feel so scared now that I've seen what he is; now that there are no secrets between us.

AFTERNOON

The matter should be finished for me now. All this time, I've been thinking that there was something to remember, something I was missing. But there isn't. I didn't see anything important or do anything bad. I just happened to be on the same street. I know this now, thanks to the red-haired man. And yet there's a feeling at the back of my mind that I just can't get rid of.

Neither Gaskill nor Riley were at the police station; I gave my statement to a police officer who looked bored. I guess it will be filed away and forgotten, unless I die suddenly. My interview was on the other side of town from Scott's place, but I took a taxi from the police station. I'm not taking any risks. It went as well as it could: the job is really below my skills, but I feel like I've also gone down in the world over the past year or two. I need to get back on track. The big problem (besides the bad pay and the low-level job) will be having to come to Witney all the time, to walk these streets and risk seeing Scott or Anna and her child.

Because running into people is all I seem to do around here. It's one of the things I used to like about this place: the small-town feel near London. You might not know everyone, but you recognize faces.

I'm almost at the station, just passing the Crown, when I feel a hand on my arm and I turn around quickly, stepping off the sidewalk and into the road.

"Hey, hey, I'm sorry. I'm sorry." It's him again, the red-haired man, holding a drink in one hand, the other raised to show he means no harm. "You're easily startled, aren't you?" He smiles. I must look really scared, because the smile disappears. "Are you okay? I didn't mean to frighten you."

He finished work early, he says, and asks me to have a drink with him. I say no, and then I change my mind.
