"I need to apologize," I say, when he—Andy, as it turns out—brings me my gin and tonic, "for how I acted on the train. Last time, I mean. I was having a bad day."

"It's okay," Andy says. His smile is slow and relaxed, I don't think this is his first drink. We're sitting across from each other in the outdoor area of the pub; it feels safer here than on the street. Maybe it's the safe feeling that makes me brave. I take my chance.

"I wanted to ask you about what happened," I say. "The night I met you. The night that Meg—The night that woman disappeared."

"Oh. Right. Why? What do you mean?"

I take a deep breath. I can feel my face getting red. No matter how many times you have to say this, it's always embarrassing, it always makes you feel ashamed. "I was very drunk and I don't remember. There are some things I need to figure out. I just want to know if you saw anything, if you saw me talking to anyone else, anything like that..." I'm looking down at the table, I can't look him in the eye.

He touches my foot with his. “It’s okay, you didn’t do anything wrong.” I look up and he’s smiling. “I was angry too. We talked a little on the train, I don’t remember what about. Then we both got off here, at Witney, and you were a little wobbly. You fell on the steps. You remember? I helped you up and you were embarrassed, blushing like you are now.” He laughs. “We walked out together, and I asked if you wanted to go to the bar. But you said you had to meet your husband.”

“That’s all?”

“No. Do you really not remember? It was a little later—I don’t know, maybe half an hour? I went to the Crown, but a friend called and said he was at a bar on the other side of the train tracks, so I was going to the underpass. You had fallen again. You were hurt. You had cut yourself. I was worried, I offered to walk you home, but you didn’t want to. You were very upset. I think you had argued with your boyfriend. He was walking down the street, and I said I would go after him if you wanted, but you said no. He drove off after that. He was with someone.”

“A woman?”

He nods, looks down a little. “Yeah, they got in a car together. I thought that was what the fight was about.”

“And then?”

“Then you walked away. You seemed a little confused, and you walked away. You kept saying you didn’t need help. I was a little drunk myself, so I just let it go. I went through the underpass and met my friend at the bar. That was it.”

Walking up the stairs to the apartment, I think I see shadows above me, hear footsteps ahead. Someone waiting at the top. There’s no one there, of course, and the apartment is empty too: it feels untouched, it smells empty, but I still check every room—under my bed and under Cathy’s, in the closets and the small cupboard in the kitchen that couldn't hide a child.

Finally, after checking the apartment three times, I can stop. I go upstairs and sit on the bed and think about the talk I had with Andy, and that it matches what I remember. There is no big surprise: Tom and I argued in the street, I fell and hurt myself, he left and got in his car with Anna. Later he came back looking for me, but I was already gone. I got in a taxi, I guess, or back on the train.

I sit on my bed looking out the window and wonder why I don’t feel better. Maybe it’s just because I still don’t have any answers. Maybe it’s because, even though what I remember matches what other people remember, something still feels wrong. Then I realize: Anna. It’s not just that Tom never said he went anywhere in the car with her, it’s that when I saw her, walking away, getting in the car, she wasn’t holding the baby. Where was Evie while all this was happening?

SATURDAY, AUGUST 17, 2013

EVENING

I need to talk to Tom, to understand things better, because the more I think about it, the less it makes sense, and I can't stop thinking about it. I'm worried, because it's been two days since I left him that note and he hasn't replied. He didn't answer his phone last night, and he hasn't been answering it all day. Something's wrong, and I feel like it has to do with Anna.

I know that he'll want to talk to me, too, after he hears about what happened with Scott. I know that he'll want to help. I can't stop thinking about how he was that day in the car, about how we felt together. So I pick up the phone and call him, feeling nervous, just like I always used to, excited to hear his voice as much as I was years ago.

"Yeah?"

"Tom, it's me."

"Yes."

Anna must be there with him, he doesn't want to say my name. I wait a moment, to let him go to another room, away from her. I hear him sigh. "What is it?"

"Um, I wanted to talk to you... Like I said in my note, I—"

"What?" He sounds annoyed.

"I left you a note a few days ago. I thought we should talk—"

"I didn't get a note." Another, big sigh. "Damn it. That's why she's angry with me." Anna must have taken it, she didn't give it to him. "What do you need?"

I want to hang up, call again, start over. Tell him how good it was to see him on Monday, when we went to the woods.

"I just wanted to ask you something."

"What?" he says sharply. He sounds really annoyed.

"Is everything okay?"

"What do you want, Rachel?" It's gone, all the kindness that was there a week ago. I'm angry at myself for leaving that note, I've clearly gotten him into trouble at home.

"I wanted to ask you about that night—the night Megan Hipwell disappeared."

"Oh, man. We've talked about this—you can't have forgotten already."

"I just—"

"You were drunk," he says, his voice loud and rough. "I told you to go home. You wouldn't listen. You walked away. I drove around looking for you, but I couldn't find you."

"Where was Anna?"

"She was at home."

"With the baby?"

"With Evie, yes."

"She wasn't in the car with you?"

"No."

"But—"

"Oh, come on. She was going to go out, and I was going to watch the baby. Then you showed up, so she stayed home and canceled her plans. And I wasted even more time looking for you."

I wish I hadn't called. To have my hopes go up and then down again, it feels like a cold knife twisting in my stomach.

"Okay," I say. "It's just, I remember it differently... Tom, when you saw me, was I hurt? Was I... Did I have a cut on my head?"

Another big sigh. "I'm surprised you remember anything at all, Rachel. You were very drunk. Really, really drunk. Stumbling all over the place." My throat starts to feel tight, hearing him say these words. I've heard him say things like this before, in the bad old days, the worst days, when he was tired of me, sick of me, disgusted by me. Tiredly, he goes on. "You'd fallen over in the street, you were crying, you were a total mess. Why is this important?" I can't find the words right away, I take too long to answer. He goes on: "Look, I have to go. Don't call anymore, please. We've talked about this. How many times do I have to ask you? Don't call, don't leave notes, don't come here. It upsets Anna. Okay?"

The phone hangs up.

SUNDAY, AUGUST 18, 2013

EARLY MORNING

I've been downstairs in the living room all night, with the TV on for company, fear coming and going. Strength coming and going. It feels like I've gone back in time, the hurt he caused years ago opened up again, new and fresh. It's silly, I know. I was stupid to think that I had a chance with him again, just because of one conversation, a few moments that I thought were kind but were probably just him feeling sorry and guilty. Still, it hurts. And I just have to let myself feel the pain, because if I don't, if I keep ignoring it, it'll never really go away.

And I was stupid to let myself think that there was a connection between me and Scott, that I could help him. So, I'm stupid. I'm used to that. I don't have to keep being stupid, do I? Not anymore. I lay here all night and I promised myself that I'll get things under control. I'll move away from here, far away. I'll get a new job. I'll go back to my old name, break ties with Tom, make it hard for anyone to find me. If anyone comes looking.

I haven't had much sleep. Lying here on the sofa, making plans, every time I started to fall asleep I heard Tom's voice in my head, as clear as if he were right there, right next to me, his lips against my ear— You were very drunk. Really, really drunk —and I woke up suddenly, shame covering me like a wave. Shame, but also a very strong feeling of having been here before, because I've heard those words before, those exact words.

And then I couldn't stop thinking about the scenes: waking with blood on the pillow, the inside of my mouth hurting, like I'd bitten my cheek, dirty fingernails, terrible headache, Tom coming out of the bathroom, that look he had—half hurt, half angry—fear rising in me like a flood.

"What happened?"

Tom showed me the bruises on his arm and chest, where I had hit him.

“I don’t believe it, Tom. I would never hit you. I’ve never hit anyone.”

“You were very drunk, Rachel. Do you remember anything you did last night? Anything you said?” Then he would tell me, and I still couldn’t believe it, because nothing he said sounded like me. And the thing with the golf club, that hole in the wall, grey and empty like a blind eye staring at me every time I walked by. I couldn't connect the violence he talked about with the fear I remembered.

Or thought I remembered. After a while, I learned not to ask what I had done, or to argue when he told me, because I didn’t want to know the details. I didn’t want to hear the worst of it, the things I said and did when I was that drunk. Sometimes he threatened to record me and play it back for me. He never did. That was a small kindness.

After a while, I learned that when you wake up like that, you don’t ask what happened. You just say that you’re sorry for what you did and who you are, and that you’re never going to act like that again.

And now I’m not, I’m really not. I can thank Scott for this: I’m too afraid now to go out in the middle of the night to buy alcohol. I’m too afraid to let myself lose control, because that’s when I become weak.

I’m going to have to be strong, that’s all there is to it.

My eyelids start to feel heavy again and my head falls forward. I turn the TV down so it’s almost silent, turn over to face the back of the sofa, get comfortable, and pull the blanket over me. I’m falling asleep, I can feel it, and then—bang, I suddenly wake up, my heart pounding. I saw it. I saw it.

I was in the underpass and he was coming towards me, one slap across the mouth and then his fist raised, keys in his hand, sharp pain as the jagged metal hit my head.

ANNA

• • •

SATURDAY, AUGUST 17, 2013

EVENING

I hate myself for crying, it's so sad. But I feel very tired, these past few weeks have been so hard on me. And Tom and I had another fight about—of course—Rachel.

It's been building up, I guess. I've been worrying a lot about the note, about him lying to me about them meeting. I keep telling myself it's silly, but I can't help feeling that they are seeing each other. I've been thinking about it over and over: after everything she did to him—to us—how could he? How could he even think about being with her again? I mean, if you compare us, there isn't a man who would choose her over me. And that's not even talking about all her problems.

But then I think, this happens sometimes, right? People you used to know, they won't let you go, and no matter how hard you try, you can't get away, can't be free. Maybe after a while you just stop trying.

She came by on Thursday, banging on the door and calling for Tom. I was very angry, but I didn't open the door. Having a child makes you weak. If I was alone I would have faced her, I would have dealt with her easily. But with Evie here, I couldn't risk it. I don't know what she might do.

I know why she came. She was angry that I talked to the police about her. I bet she went crying to Tom to tell me to leave her alone. She left a note— We need to talk, please call me as soon as possible, it's important (important underlined three times)—which I threw away. Later, I took it out and put it in my bedside drawer, with the copy of that mean email she sent and the list I've been keeping of all the calls and times I've seen her. The list of her bothering me. My proof, if I need it. I called Detective Riley and left a message saying that Rachel had come by again. She still hasn't called back.

I should have told Tom about the note, I know I should have, but I didn't want him to be mad at me for talking to the police, so I just put it in that drawer and hoped that she'd forget about it. She didn't, of course. She called him tonight. He was very angry when he got off the phone with her.

"What the heck is this about a note?" he asked angrily.

I told him I had thrown it away. "I didn't know you wanted to read it," I said. "I thought you wanted her out of our lives as much as I do."

He rolled his eyes. "That's not the point, and you know it. Of course, I want Rachel gone. What I don't want is for you to start listening to my phone calls and throwing away my mail. You're..." He sighed.

"I'm what?"

"Nothing. It's just... it's the kind of thing she used to do."

It was a hard blow, a cheap shot. I started crying and ran upstairs to the bathroom. I waited for him to come up to comfort me, to kiss and make up like he usually does, but after about half an hour he called out to me, "I'm going to the gym for a couple of hours," and before I could answer, I heard the front door slam.

And now I'm acting exactly like she used to: finishing the half bottle of red wine left over from dinner last night and looking through his computer. It's easier to understand her actions when you feel like I feel right now. There's nothing as painful, as damaging, as suspicion.

I finally got the laptop password: it's Blenheim. As simple and boring as that—the name of the road we live on. I haven't found any bad emails, no dirty pictures or love letters. I spend half an hour reading through work emails so boring that they even make the pain of jealousy feel less, then I shut down the laptop and put it away. I'm feeling pretty good, thanks to the wine and the boring stuff on Tom's computer. I've told myself I was just being silly.

I go upstairs to brush my teeth—I don't want him to know that I've been drinking again—and then I decide that I'll take the sheets off the bed and put on clean ones. I'll spray some Acqua di Parma on the pillows and put on that black silk nightgown he got me for my birthday last year, and when he comes back, I'll make things right with him.

As I'm taking the sheets off the bed, I almost trip over a black bag pushed under the bed: his gym bag. He's forgotten his gym bag. He's been gone an hour, and he hasn't come back for it. My stomach drops. Maybe he just thought, forget it, and decided to go to the bar instead. Maybe he has some extra stuff in his locker at the gym. Maybe he's in bed with her right now.

I feel sick. I get on my knees and look through the bag. All his things are there, clean and ready, his iPod shuffle, the only shoes he runs in. And something else: a mobile phone. A phone I've never seen before.

I sit on the bed, the phone in my hand, my heart beating fast. I'm going to turn it on, I can't stop myself, but I'm sure I'll regret it, because this can only mean something bad. You don't keep extra phones in gym bags unless you're hiding something. A voice in my head says, "Just put it back, just forget about it," but I can't. I press the power button and wait for the screen to turn on. And wait. And wait. It's off. I feel very relieved.

I'm relieved because now I can't find out anything, but I'm also relieved because a dead phone seems like an unused phone, a phone he doesn't want, not the phone of a man having an affair. That man would want his phone with him all the time. Maybe it's an old phone of his, maybe it's been in his gym bag for months and he hasn't thrown it away. Maybe it's not even his: maybe he found it at the gym and was going to give it to the desk and forgot?

I leave the bed messy and go downstairs to the living room. The coffee table has drawers with junk that we've collected over time: tape, plug adapters for trips, tape measures, sewing kits, old phone chargers. I grab all three chargers; the second one I try fits. I plug it in on my side of the bed, phone and charger hidden behind the bedside table. Then I wait.

Times and dates, mostly. Not dates. Days. "Monday at 3? Friday, 4:30." Sometimes, a no. "Can't tomorrow. Not Weds." There's nothing else: no love messages, no clear suggestions. Just texts, about ten of them, all from a hidden number. There are no names in the phone book and the call history is gone.

I don't need dates, because the phone shows them. The meetings go back months. They go back almost a year. When I saw that the first one was from September last year, I felt a lump in my throat. September! Evie was six months old. I was still big, tired, and not interested in sex. But then I start to laugh, because this is silly, it can't be true. We were very happy in September, in love with each other and our new baby. There's no way he was seeing her, no way he's been seeing her all this time. I would have known. It can't be true. The phone isn't his.

Still. I get my list of calls from the bedside table and look at the calls, comparing them with the meetings on the phone. Some of them match. Some calls are a day or two before, some a day or two after. Some don't match at all.

Could he really have been seeing her all this time, telling me that she was bothering him, when they were really planning to meet, to sneak around behind my back? But why would she call him on the house phone if she had this phone to call? It doesn't make sense. Unless she wanted me to know. Unless she was trying to cause problems between us?

Tom has been gone almost two hours, he'll be back soon. I make the bed, put the list and the phone in the bedside table, go downstairs, pour myself a last glass of wine and drink it fast. I could call her. I could face her. But what would I say? I don't have the right to be angry. And I'm not sure I could handle it, the joy she would have in telling me that all this time, I've been the fool. If he cheats with you, he'll cheat on you.

I hear footsteps outside and I know it's him, I know how he walks. I put the wineglass in the sink and stand there, leaning against the kitchen counter, my heart pounding.

"Hello," he says when he sees me. He looks embarrassed and is swaying a little.

"They have beer at the gym now, do they?"

He smiles. "I forgot my things. I went to the bar."

Just like I thought. Or just like he thought I would think?

He comes a little closer. "What have you been doing?" he asks me, smiling. "You look guilty." He puts his arms around my waist and pulls me close. I can smell the beer on his breath. "Have you been doing something bad?"

"Tom..."

"Shhh," he says, and he kisses me, starts unbuttoning my jeans. He turns me around. I don't want to, but I don't know how to say no, so I close my eyes and try not to think of him with her. I try to think of when we first started, running to the empty house on Cranham Road, out of breath, wanting each other so much.

SUNDAY, AUGUST 18, 2013

EARLY MORNING

I wake up suddenly; it's still dark. I think I hear Evie crying, but when I go to check on her, she's sleeping soundly, holding her blanket tightly. I go back to bed, but I can't fall back asleep. All I can think about is the phone in the drawer next to the bed. I look at Tom, lying with his left arm out, his head back. I can tell from his breathing that he's fast asleep. I get out of bed, open the drawer, and take out the phone.

Downstairs in the kitchen, I turn the phone in my hand, getting ready. I want to know, but I don't. I want to be sure, but I really want to be wrong. I turn it on. I press one and hold it, I hear the voice mail message. I hear that I have no new messages and no saved messages. Would I like to change my greeting? I end the call, but then I get a strange fear that the phone could ring, that Tom would hear it from upstairs, so I open the glass doors and go outside.

The grass is wet under my feet, the air cool, smelling like rain and roses. I can hear a train far away, a slow rumbling sound. I walk almost to the fence before I dial the voice mail again: would I like to change my greeting? Yes, I would. There's a beep and a pause and then I hear her voice. Her voice, not his. Hi, it's me, leave a message.

My heart has stopped.

It's not his phone, it's hers.

I play it again.

Hi, it's me, leave a message.

It's her voice.

I can't move, can't breathe. I play it again, and again. My throat is tight, I feel like I'm going to pass out, and then the light turns on upstairs.

RACHEL

• • •

SUNDAY, AUGUST 18, 2013

EARLY MORNING

One memory led to another. It felt like I had been lost in the dark for days, weeks, or months. Then, I finally grabbed onto something, like feeling my way along a wall to get from one room to another. The blurry shadows started to come together, and after a while, my eyes got used to the darkness, and I could see.

Not right away. At first, even though it felt like a memory, I thought it must be a dream. I sat on the sofa, almost unable to move from shock. I told myself that it wouldn't be the first time I had remembered something wrong. It wouldn't be the first time I thought things happened one way when they actually happened differently.

Like the time we went to a party at Tom's coworker's house. I was very drunk, but we had a good night. I remember kissing Clara goodbye. Clara was the coworker's wife, a nice, friendly woman. I remember her saying we should hang out again. I remember her holding my hand.

I remembered that so clearly, but it wasn't true. I knew it wasn't true the next morning when Tom ignored me when I tried to talk to him. I know it isn't true because he told me he was disappointed and embarrassed that I had accused Clara of flirting with him, and that I had been out of control and mean.

When I closed my eyes, I could feel her hand, warm against my skin, but that didn't really happen. What really happened was that Tom had to almost carry me out of the house, with me crying and yelling the whole time, while poor Clara hid in the kitchen.

So when I closed my eyes, when I was half dreaming and found myself in that underpass, I might have felt the cold and smelled the bad, old air. I might have seen a person walking towards me, full of anger, with a fist raised, but it wasn't true. The fear I felt wasn't real. And when the shadow hit me, leaving me on the ground, crying and bleeding, that wasn't real either.

It happened, and I saw it. It's so surprising that I can hardly believe it, but as I watch the sun come up, it feels like fog clearing. What he told me was not true. I didn't make up him hitting me. I remember it. Just like I remember saying goodbye to Clara after that party and her holding my hand. Just like I remember being scared when I was on the floor next to that golf club—and I know now, I know for sure that I wasn't the one using it.

I don't know what to do. I run upstairs, put on jeans and sneakers, and run back downstairs. I call their house phone, let it ring a few times, then hang up. I don't know what to do. I make coffee, let it get cold, call Detective Riley's number, then hang up right away. She won't believe me. I know she won't.

I go to the train station. It's a Sunday schedule, the first train isn't for thirty minutes, so I just sit there on a bench, thinking over and over, from not believing it to feeling hopeless and back again.

Everything is a lie. I didn't make up him hitting me. I didn't make up him walking away from me fast, with his fists tight. I saw him turn, yell. I saw him walking down the street with a woman, I saw him getting into the car with her. I didn't make it up. And I realize then that it's all very easy, so very easy. I do remember, it's just that I had mixed up two memories. I had put the picture of Anna, walking away from me in her blue dress, into another situation: Tom and a woman getting into a car. Because that woman wasn't wearing a blue dress, she was wearing jeans and a red T-shirt. She was Megan.

ANNA

• • •

SUNDAY, AUGUST 18, 2013

EARLY MORNING

I throw the phone over the fence as far as I can. It lands on the edge of the loose rocks at the top of the slope. I think I can hear it rolling down towards the train tracks. I think I can still hear her voice. Hi. It’s me. Leave a message. I think I might be hearing her voice for a long time.

He’s at the bottom of the stairs when I get back to the house. He’s watching me, blinking, sleepy, just waking up.

“What’s going on?”

“Nothing,” I say, but my voice is shaking.

“What were you doing outside?”

“I thought I heard someone,” I tell him. “Something woke me up. I couldn’t go back to sleep.”

"The phone rang," he says, rubbing his eyes.

I put my hands together to stop them from shaking. "What? What phone?"

"The phone." He looks at me like I'm crazy. "The phone rang. Someone called and then hung up."

"Oh. I don't know. I don't know who that was."

He laughs. "Of course you don't. Are you okay?" He comes over to me and puts his arms around my waist. "You're acting strange." He holds me for a bit, his head against my chest. "You should have woken me if you heard something," he says. "You shouldn't go out there alone. That's my job."

"I'm fine," I say, but I have to tighten my jaw to stop my teeth from chattering. He kisses my lips, and puts his tongue in my mouth.

"Let's go back to bed," he says.

"I think I'm going to have coffee," I say, trying to get away from him.

He's not letting me go. His arms are tight around me, his hand holding the back of my neck.

"Come on," he says. "Come with me. I'm not going to take no for an answer."

RACHEL

• • •

SUNDAY, AUGUST 18, 2013

MORNING

I'm not sure what to do, so I just ring the doorbell. I wonder if I should have called first. It's not nice to show up early on a Sunday morning without calling, is it? I start to giggle. I feel a little crazy. I don't really know what I'm doing.

No one answers the door. The crazy feeling gets stronger as I walk around the side of the house, down the small path. I feel like I've been here before. That morning, when I came to the house, when I took the little girl. I never wanted to hurt her. I'm sure of that now.

I can hear her talking as I walk along the path in the cool shade of the house, and I wonder if I'm making things up. But no, there she is, and Anna, too, sitting on the patio. I call out to her and climb over the fence. She looks at me. I expect her to be shocked or angry, but she doesn't even look surprised.

"Hello, Rachel," she says. She stands up, taking her child by the hand, pulling her close. She looks at me, not smiling, calm. Her eyes are red, her face pale, clean, with no makeup.

"What do you want?" she asks.

"I rang the doorbell," I tell her.

"I didn't hear it," she says, lifting the child onto her hip. She turns slightly away from me, like she's going to go inside, but then she just stops. I don't understand why she's not shouting at me.

"Where's Tom, Anna?"

"He went out. Army guys meeting."

"We need to leave, Anna," I say, and she starts to laugh.

ANNA

• • •

SUNDAY, AUGUST 18, 2013

MORNING

For some reason, the whole thing seems very funny now. Poor fat Rachel standing in my garden, all red and sweaty, telling me we need to leave. We need to leave.

“Where are we going?” I ask her when I stop laughing, and she just looks at me, blank, not knowing what to say. “I’m not leaving with you.” Evie wiggles and complains and I put her back down. My skin still feels hot and sore from where I washed myself in the shower this morning; the inside of my mouth, my cheeks, my tongue, they feel bitten.

“When will he be back?” she asks me.

“Not for a while yet, I don’t think.”

I have no idea when he’ll be back, actually. Sometimes he can spend whole days at the climbing wall. Or I thought he spent whole days at the climbing wall. Now I don’t know.

I do know that he’s taken the gym bag; it can’t be long before he finds out that the phone is gone.

I was thinking of taking Evie and going to my sister’s for a while, but the phone is bothering me. What if someone finds it? There are workers on this part of the track all the time; one of them might find it and give it to the police. It has my fingerprints on it.

Then I was thinking that maybe it wouldn’t be too hard to get it back, but I’d have to wait until night so no one would see me.

I know that Rachel is still talking, she’s asking me questions. I haven’t been listening to her. I feel so tired.

“Anna,” she says, coming closer to me, her dark eyes looking closely at mine. “Have you ever met any of them?”

“Met who?”

“His friends from the army. Have you ever actually met any of them?” I shake my head. “Don’t you think that’s strange?” It seems strange to me that she’s in my garden early on a Sunday morning.

“Not really,” I say. “They’re part of another life. Another of his lives. Like you are. Like you were supposed to be, anyway, but we can’t seem to get rid of you.” She looks hurt. “What are you doing here, Rachel?”

“You know why I’m here,” she says. “You know that something . . . something has been happening.” She looks serious, like she’s worried about me. If things were different, it might be nice.

“Would you like a cup of coffee?” I say, and she nods.

I make the coffee and we sit next to each other on the patio in quiet that feels almost friendly. “What were you saying?” I ask her. “That Tom’s friends from the army aren’t real? That he made them up? That he’s actually with another woman?”

"I don't know," she says.

"Rachel?" She looks at me, and I can see she's scared. "Is there something you want to tell me?"

"Have you ever met Tom's family?" she asks. "His parents?"

"No. They aren't talking to him. They stopped talking to him when he left with me."

She shakes her head. "That's not true," she says. "I've never met them either. They don't even know me, so why would they care about him leaving me?"

There's a dark feeling in my head, at the back of my skull. I've been trying to stop it since I heard her voice on the phone, but now it starts to grow and spread.

"I don't believe you," I say. "Why would he lie about that?"

"Because he lies about everything."

I stand up and walk away from her. I'm annoyed that she told me this. I'm annoyed with myself, because I think I do believe her. I think I've always known that Tom lies. It's just that before, his lies were helpful to me.

"He is a good liar," I say to her. "You didn't know anything for a long time, did you? All those months we were meeting, having sex in that house on Cranham Road, and you never suspected anything."

She swallows and bites her lip hard. “Megan,” she says. “What about Megan?”

“I know. They had a relationship.” The words sound strange to me—this is the first time I’ve said them out loud. He cheated on me. He cheated on me. “I’m sure that makes you laugh,” I say to her, “but she’s gone now, so it doesn’t matter, right?”

“Anna . . .”

The darkness gets bigger; it’s pushing at the edges of my head, making it hard to see. I grab Evie’s hand and start to pull her inside. She complains loudly.

“Anna . . .”

“They had a relationship. That’s it. Nothing else. It doesn’t have to mean—”

“That he killed her?”

“Don’t say that!” I yell at her. “Don’t say that in front of my child.”

I give Evie her morning snack, which she eats without complaining for the first time in weeks. It’s almost like she knows that I have other things to worry about, and I love her for it. I feel much calmer when we go back outside, even if Rachel is still there, standing at the bottom of the garden by the fence, watching one of the trains go by. After a while, when she sees that I’m back outside, she walks towards me.

“You like them, don’t you?” I say. “The trains. I hate them. I really hate them.”

She gives me a small smile. I see that she has a deep dent on the left side of her face. I've never seen that before. I guess I haven't seen her smile very often. Ever.

"Another thing he lied about," she says. "He told me you loved this house, loved everything about it, even the trains. He told me that you wouldn't want to find a new place, that you wanted to move in here with him, even if I had been here first."

I shake my head. "Why would he tell you that?" I ask her. "It's completely false. I've been trying to get him to sell this house for two years."

She shrugs. "Because he lies, Anna. All the time."

The darkness grows. I pull Evie onto my lap and she sits there happily, she's getting sleepy in the sunshine. "So all those phone calls . . ." I say. It's only now starting to make sense. "They weren't from you? I mean, I know some of them were, but some—"

"Were from Megan? Yes, I think so."

It's strange, because I know now that all this time I've been hating the wrong woman, and yet knowing this doesn't make me dislike Rachel any less. If anything, seeing her like this, calm, worried, not drunk, I'm starting to see what she used to be and I dislike her more, because I'm starting to see what he must have seen in her. What he must have loved.

I look down at my watch. It's after eleven. He left around eight, I think. It might even have been earlier. He must know about the phone by now. He must have known for a while. Maybe he thinks it fell out of the bag. Maybe he thinks it's under the bed upstairs.

"How long have you known?" I ask her. "About the affair."

"I didn't," she says. "Until today. I mean I don't know what was going on. I just know . . ." Thankfully she stops talking, because I'm not sure I can stand hearing her talk about my husband being unfaithful. The thought that she and I—heavy, sad Rachel and I—are now in the same situation is too much to bear.

"Do you think it was his?" she asks me. "Do you think the baby was his?"

I'm looking at her, but I'm not really seeing her. I only see darkness and hear a loud noise in my ears, like the sea or a plane flying very low.

"What did you say?"

"The... I'm sorry." Her face is red and she's upset. "I shouldn't have... She was pregnant when she died. Megan was pregnant. I'm so sorry."

But I'm sure she's not sorry at all, and I don't want to fall apart in front of her. I look down at Evie, and I feel a sadness that is stronger than any I've ever felt. It hits me like a wave, making it hard to breathe. Evie's brother, Evie's sister. Gone. Rachel sits next to me and puts her arm around me.

"I'm sorry," she says again, and I want to hit her. The feeling of her skin touching mine makes me feel uncomfortable. I want to push her away and yell at her, but I can't. She lets me cry for a while, and then she says clearly, "Anna, I think we should leave. I think you should pack some things for you and Evie, and then we should go. You can stay at my place for now. Until... until we figure things out."

I dry my eyes and move away from her. "I'm not leaving him, Rachel. He had an affair, he... It's not the first time, is it?" I start to laugh, and Evie laughs too.

Rachel sighs and stands up. "You know this isn't just about an affair, Anna. I know that you know."

"We don't know anything," I say quietly.

"She got in the car with him that night. I saw her. I didn't remember it at first—I thought it was you," she says. "But I remember. I remember now."

"No." Evie's sticky little hand presses against my mouth.

"We have to talk to the police, Anna." She takes a step towards me. "Please. You can't stay here with him."

Even though the sun is out, I'm shivering. I'm trying to remember the last time Megan came to the house, the look on his face when she said she couldn't work for us anymore. I'm trying to remember if he looked happy or sad. Suddenly, a different picture comes to mind: one of the first times she came to look after Evie. I was supposed to go out with my friends, but I was so tired, so I went upstairs to sleep. Tom must have come home while I was up there, because they were together when I came downstairs. She was leaning against the counter, and he was standing too close to her. Evie was in the high chair, she was crying and neither of them were looking at her.

I feel very cold. Did I know then that he wanted her? Megan was blonde and beautiful—she was like me. So yes, I probably knew that he wanted her, just like I know when I walk down the street that there are married men with their wives and children who look at me and think about it. So maybe I did know. He wanted her, he took her. But not this. He couldn't do this.

Not Tom. A lover, husband twice over. A father. A good father, who never complained and provided for us.

"You loved him," I remind her. "You still love him, don't you?"

She shakes her head, but she doesn't sound sure.

"You do. And you know... you know that this isn't possible."

I stand up, pulling Evie up with me, and move closer to her. "He couldn't have, Rachel. You know he couldn't have done this. You couldn't love a man who would do that, could you?"

"But I did," she says. "We both did." There are tears on her cheeks. She wipes them away, and as she does her face changes and loses all color. She's not looking at me, but over my shoulder, and as I turn around to see what she's looking at, I see him at the kitchen window, watching us.

MEGAN

• • •

FRIDAY, JULY 12, 2013

MORNING

She's made me do this. Or maybe he has. I feel like it's her. Or my heart tells me so, I don't know. I can feel her, like before, curled up, like a seed in a shell, but this seed is smiling. Waiting for the right time. I can't hate her. And I can't get rid of her. I can't. I thought I could, I thought I would really want to get her out, but when I think about her, I just see Libby's face, her dark eyes. I can smell her skin. I can feel how cold she was at the end. I can't get rid of her. I don't want to. I want to love her.

I can't hate her, but she scares me. I'm afraid of what she'll do to me, or what I'll do to her. That fear woke me up just after five this morning, covered in sweat even though the windows were open and I was alone. Scott's at a meeting, somewhere in Hertfordshire or Essex or somewhere. He's coming back tonight.

Why am I like this? I want to be alone when he's here, but when he's gone I can't stand it. I can't stand the quiet. I have to talk out loud to make it go away. In bed this morning, I kept thinking, what if it happens again? What's going to happen when I'm alone with her? What's going to happen if he doesn't want me, doesn't want us? What happens if he figures out that she isn't his?

She might be, of course. I don't know, but I just feel like she isn't. The same way I feel like she's a girl. But even if she isn't, how would he know? He won't. He can't. I'm being silly. He'll be so happy. He'll be very excited when I tell him. The thought that she might not be his won't even come to his mind. Telling him would be mean, it would break his heart, and I don't want to hurt him. I've never wanted to hurt him.

I can't help the way I am.

"You can help what you do, though." That's what Kamal says.

I called Kamal just after six. The quiet was heavy, and I was starting to panic. I thought about calling Tara—I knew she’d come quickly—but I didn’t think I could handle it, she’d be too worried and protective. Kamal was the only person I could think of. I called him at home. I told him I was in trouble, I didn’t know what to do, I was really upset. He came over right away. He asked a few questions, but not many. Maybe I made things sound worse than they were. Maybe he was afraid I was going to do something bad.

We’re in the kitchen. It’s still early, just after seven thirty. He has to leave soon if he’s going to make his first appointment. I look at him, sitting across from me at our kitchen table, his hands folded neatly in front of him, his kind eyes on mine, and I feel love. I really do. He’s been so good to me, even though I’ve acted badly.

He’s forgiven everything I did before, just like I hoped he would. He forgot all of it, all my mistakes. He told me that if I didn't forgive myself, this would keep happening and I would never stop running away. And I can’t run anymore, can I? Not now she’s here.

“I’m scared,” I tell him. “What if I mess up again? What if there’s something wrong with me? What if things go wrong with Scott? What if I end up alone again? I don’t know if I can do it, I’m so afraid of being alone again—I mean, alone with a child . . .”

He leans forward and puts his hand over mine. “You won’t mess up. You won’t. You’re not a sad, lost child anymore. You’re a completely different person. You’re stronger. You’re an adult now. You don’t have to be afraid of being alone. It’s not the worst thing, is it?”

I don’t say anything, but I can’t help wondering if it is, because if I close my eyes I can remember the feeling I get when I’m almost asleep, which wakes me up. It’s the feeling of being alone in a dark house, listening for her cries, waiting to hear Mac’s football on the wooden floors downstairs and knowing that they’re never going to come.

“I can’t tell you what to do about Scott. Your relationship with him . . . Well, I’ve told you my worries, but you have to decide what to do for yourself. Decide if you trust him, if you want him to take care of you and your child. That must be your decision. But I think you can trust yourself, Megan. You can trust yourself to do the right thing.”

Outside, on the grass, he brings me a cup of coffee. I put it down and hug him, pulling him closer. Behind us a train is going up to the signal. The noise is like a wall around us, and I feel like we are truly alone. He hugs me back and kisses me.

“Thank you,” I say. “Thank you for coming, for being here.”

He smiles, pulling away from me, and rubs his thumb across my cheek. “You’ll be fine, Megan.”

"Couldn't I just leave with you? You and I . . . couldn't we just leave together?"

He laughs. "You don't need me. And you don't need to keep leaving. You'll be okay. You and your baby will be okay."

SATURDAY, JULY 13, 2013

MORNING

I know what I need to do. I thought about it all day yesterday, and all night, too. I barely slept at all. Scott came home tired and in a bad mood; all he wanted was to eat, have sex, and sleep, with no time for anything else. It was definitely not the right time to talk about this.

I stayed awake most of the night, with him hot and restless next to me, and I made my decision. I'm going to do the right thing. I'm going to do everything right. If I do everything right, then nothing can go wrong. Or if it does, it won't be my fault. I will love this child and raise her knowing that I did the right thing from the start. Okay, maybe not from the very start, but from the moment I knew she was coming. I owe it to this baby, and I owe it to Libby. I owe it to her to do everything differently this time.

I lay there and I thought of what that teacher said, and of all the things I'd been: child, rebellious teenager, runaway, prostitute, lover, bad mother, bad wife. I'm not sure if I can become a good wife, but I have to try to be a good mother.

It's going to be hard. It might be the hardest thing I've ever had to do, but I'm going to tell the truth. No more lies, no more hiding, no more leaving, no more nonsense. I'm going to be honest about everything, and then we'll see. If he can't love me then, that's how it is.

EVENING

My hand is against his chest and I'm pushing as hard as I can, but I can't breathe and he's so much stronger than I am. His arm presses against my throat, I can feel the blood pounding in my head, my vision getting blurry. I try to yell, my back against the wall. I grab a piece of his T-shirt and he lets go. He turns away from me and I slide down the wall onto the kitchen floor.

I cough and spit, with tears running down my face. He's standing close to me, and when he turns back, I quickly put my hand to my throat to protect it. I see he looks ashamed and I want to tell him that it's okay. I'm okay. I try to speak but can't, I just cough more. The pain is awful. He's talking to me but I can't hear him. It's like we're underwater, the sound is unclear and comes in waves. I can't understand anything.

I think he's saying he's sorry.

I get up, push past him, and run up the stairs. I slam the bedroom door and lock it. I sit on the bed and wait, listening for him, but he doesn't come. I stand up and grab my bag from under the bed. I go to the dresser to get some clothes and see myself in the mirror. I touch my face: it looks very pale compared to my red skin, purple lips, and bloodshot eyes.

I'm partly shocked because he's never hurt me like that before. But another part of me expected it. I always knew it could happen, that we were heading this way. That I was leading him to this. Slowly, I start taking things out of the drawers—underwear, a couple of T-shirts—and put them in the bag.

I haven't even told him anything yet. I had just started. I wanted to tell him the bad things first, before the good news. I couldn't tell him about the baby and then say it might not be his. That would be too mean.

We were outside on the patio. He was talking about work and noticed I wasn't really listening.

"Am I boring you?" he asked.

"No. Well, maybe a little." He didn't laugh. "No, I'm just distracted. Because I need to tell you something. Actually, a few things. Some you won't like, but some—"

"What won't I like?"

I should have known then it wasn't the right time. He was in a bad mood. He was immediately suspicious, looking at my face for clues. I should have known it was a terrible idea. I guess I did, but it was too late to stop. And I had already decided to do the right thing.

I sat next to him on the edge of the ground and put my hand in his.

“What won’t I like?” he asked again, but he didn’t let go of my hand.

I told him I loved him and I felt his whole body tighten, like he knew what was coming and was getting ready for it. You do that, don’t you, when someone tells you they love you like that. I love you, I do, but . . . But.

I told him that I’d made some errors and he let go of my hand. He stood up and walked a little ways toward the path before turning to look at me. “What kind of errors?” he asked. His voice was steady, but I could hear that it was hard for him to keep it that way.

“Come and sit with me,” I said. “Please?”

He shook his head. “What kind of errors, Megan?” Louder that time.

“There was . . . it’s done now, but there was . . . someone else.” I looked down, I couldn’t look at him.

He said something quietly, but I couldn’t hear it. I looked up then, but he’d turned away and was facing the path again, his hands on his head. I stood up and went to him, stood behind him and put my hands on his sides, but he jumped away from me. He turned to go into the house and, without looking at me, said, “Don’t touch me, you little whore.”

I should have let him go then, given him time to understand, but I couldn’t. I wanted to get past the bad things so that I could get to the good, so I followed him into the house.

“Scott, please, just listen, it’s not as bad as you think. It’s over now. It’s completely over, please listen, please—”

He grabbed the picture of us that he loves—the one I had put in a frame as a gift for our second wedding anniversary—and threw it as hard as he could at my head. It broke against the wall behind me, and he jumped at me, grabbing my arms and pulling me across the room, throwing me against the other wall. My head hit the wall hard. Then he leaned in, his arm across my throat, pushing harder and harder, saying nothing. He closed his eyes so he didn't have to see me struggle to breathe.

As soon as my bag is packed, I start taking everything out again, putting it back in the drawers. If I try to leave with a bag, he won't let me go. I have to leave with nothing but a purse and a phone. Then I change my mind again and start putting everything back in the bag. I don't know where I'm going, but I know I can't stay here. I close my eyes and can feel his hands around my throat.

I know I decided not to run or hide anymore, but I can't stay here tonight. I hear slow, heavy footsteps on the stairs. It takes him a long time to get to the top—usually he runs, but today he walks like he's going to his death. I don't know if he's the one who's going to be hurt or the one who's going to hurt me.

“Megan?” He doesn't try to open the door. “Megan, I'm sorry I hurt you. I'm so sorry that I hurt you.” I can hear him crying. It makes me angry, I want to go out there and scratch his face. Don't you dare cry, not after what you just did. I'm furious with him, I want to yell at him, tell him to get away from the door, away from me, but I don't say anything because I'm not stupid. He has a reason to be angry. And I have to think clearly. I'm thinking for two now. This fight has made me stronger, it's made me more determined. I can hear him outside the door, begging for forgiveness, but I can't think about that now. Right now, I have other things to do.

At the very back of the closet, in the bottom of three rows of shoe boxes, there is a dark-grey box labeled red wedge boots , and in that box there is an old cell phone, a cheap phone I bought years ago and kept just in case. I haven't used it for a while, but today's the day. I'm going to be honest. I'm going to tell the truth. No more lies, no more hiding. It's time for Daddy to take responsibility.

I sit on the bed and turn the phone on, hoping it still has some power. It turns on and I can feel the excitement in my blood, it's making me dizzy, a little sick, and it's making me feel like I'm on drugs. I'm starting to enjoy myself, enjoy the idea of telling everyone the truth, facing him—all of them—with what we are and where we're going. By the end of the day, everyone is going to know where they stand.

I call his number. As expected, it goes straight to voice mail. I hang up and send a text: I need to talk to you. URGENT. Call me back. Then I sit there, and I wait.

I look at the call history. The last time I used this phone was April. Lots of calls, all of them unanswered, in early April and late March. I called and called and called, and he ignored me, he didn't even respond to the threats I made—I'd go to his house, I'd talk to his wife. I think he'll listen to me now, though. I'm going to make him listen to me now.

When we started all this, it was just a game. Something to pass the time. I used to see him sometimes. He'd come to the art gallery and smile and flirt, and it was harmless—there were many men who came to the gallery and smiled and flirted. But then the gallery closed and I was home all the time, bored and restless. I just needed something else, something different. Then one day, when Scott was away, I saw him in the street, we started talking and I invited him in for coffee. The way he looked at me, I could see what he was thinking, and so it just happened. And then it happened again, and I never meant for it to go anywhere, I didn't want it to go anywhere. I just liked feeling wanted; I liked feeling in control. It was as simple and stupid as that. I didn't want him to leave his wife; I just wanted him to want to leave her. To want me that much.

I don't remember when I started thinking that it could be more, that we should be more, that we were right for each other. But the moment I did, I could feel him start to pull away. He stopped texting, stopped answering my calls, and I've never felt that kind of rejection before, never. I hated it. So then it became something else: something I couldn't stop thinking about. I can see that now. In the end I really thought I could just walk away from it, a little hurt, but not really harmed. But it's not that simple anymore.

Scott is still outside the door. I can’t hear him, but I can feel he’s there. I go into the bathroom and call the number again. I get voice mail again, so I hang up and call again, and again. I whisper a message. “Answer the phone, or I’m coming to your place. I mean it this time. I have to talk to you. You can’t just ignore me.”

I stand in the bathroom for a while, the phone on the edge of the sink. Hoping it will ring. The screen stays gray and blank. I brush my hair and my teeth, and put on some makeup. My face is starting to look normal again. My eyes are still red, and my throat still hurts, but I look okay. I start counting. If the phone doesn’t ring before I get to fifty, I’m going to go down there and knock on the door. The phone doesn’t ring.

I put the phone into my jeans pocket, walk quickly through the bedroom and open the door. Scott is sitting on the floor, his arms around his knees, his head down. I can hear him getting up, and he calls, “Megan! Where are you going? Are you going to him?”

At the bottom of the stairs, I turn. “There is no him , OK? It’s finished.”

“Please wait, Megan. Please don’t go.”

I don’t want to hear him beg, don’t want to listen to his sad voice, feeling sorry for himself. Not when my throat still feels like someone’s poured acid down it.

“Don’t follow me,” I say to him in a rough voice. “If you follow me, I’ll never come back. Do you understand? If I turn around and see you behind me, that’ll be the last time you ever see me.”

I can hear him calling my name as I slam the door behind me.

I wait on the sidewalk outside for a few moments to make sure he isn’t following me, then I walk, quickly at first, then slower, and slower, along Blenheim Road. I get to number twenty-three and then I get scared. I’m not ready for this yet. I need a minute to calm down. A few minutes. I walk on, past the house, past the underpass, past the station. I keep going until I get to the park and then I call his number one more time.

I tell him that I’m in the park, that I’ll wait for him there, but if he doesn’t come, that’s it, I’m going to the house. This is his last chance.

It's a nice evening, a little after seven but still warm and bright. Some kids are still playing on the swings and the slide, their parents standing to the side, talking excitedly. It looks good, normal, and as I watch them I have a bad feeling that Scott and I won't bring our daughter here to play. I just can't see us happy and relaxed like that. Not now. Not after what I've just done.

I was so sure this morning that saying everything openly would be the best way—not just the best way, the only way. No more lying, no more hiding. And then when he hurt me, it only made me more sure. But now, sitting here alone, with Scott not just angry but heartbroken, I don't think it was the right thing at all. I wasn't being strong, I was being careless, and I don't know how much harm I've done.

Maybe the bravery I need has nothing to do with telling the truth and everything to do with leaving. It's not just being restless—this is more than that. For her sake and mine, now is the time to go, to leave them both, to leave everything. Maybe running and hiding is exactly what I need to do.

I get up and walk around the park once. I'm half hoping the phone will ring and half scared it will ring, but in the end I'm happy when it stays quiet. I'll take it as a sign. I go back the way I came, towards home.

I've just passed the station when I see him. He's walking fast, coming out of the underpass, his shoulders bent over and his fists tight, and before I can stop myself, I call out.

He turns to face me. "Megan! What the heck . . ." The look on his face is pure anger, but he motions for me to come to him.

"Come on," he says, when I get closer. "We can't talk here. The car's over there."

"I just need—"

"We can't talk here!" he says sharply. "Come on." He pulls my arm. Then, more gently, "We'll drive somewhere quiet, OK? Somewhere we can talk."

As I get into the car, I look over my shoulder, back the way he came. The underpass is dark, but I feel like I can see someone in there, in the shadows—someone watching us go.

SUNDAY, AUGUST 18, 2013

AFTERNOON

Anna turns quickly and runs into the house as soon as she sees him. My heart beating fast, I follow slowly, stopping before the sliding doors. Inside, they are hugging, his arms around her, the child between them. Anna’s head is down, her shoulders shaking. His mouth is on the top of her head, but he is looking at me.

“What’s happening?” he asks, with a small smile. “I didn't expect to find you two talking in the garden when I got home.”

He sounds happy, but I know he is not. I open my mouth to talk, but I can't find the words. I don't know where to begin.

“Rachel? Are you going to tell me what's going on?” He lets go of Anna and walks towards me. I step back, and he starts to laugh.

“What's wrong with you? Are you drunk?” he asks, but I can see that he knows I'm not drunk and I think he wishes I was. I put my hand in my back pocket—my phone is there, hard and small and makes me feel safe, but I wish I had called someone already. If I had told them I was with Anna and her child, the police would have come, even if they didn't believe me.

Tom is now very close to me—he's just inside the door and I'm just outside it.

"I saw you," I finally say, and I feel very happy, for a short time but clearly, when I say the words out loud. "You think I don't remember anything, but I do. I saw you. After you hit me, you left me there, in the tunnel..."

He starts to laugh, but I can see it now and I wonder how I never understood him this easily before. There's fear in his eyes. He looks quickly at Anna, but she doesn't look back at him.

"What are you talking about?"

"In the tunnel. On the day Megan Hipwell disappeared..."

"Oh, that's not true," he says, waving his hand at me. "I did not hit you. You fell." He reaches for Anna's hand and pulls her closer to him. "Honey, is this why you're so upset? Don't listen to her, she's talking complete nonsense. I didn't hit her. I've never touched her like that in my life." He puts his arm around Anna's shoulders and pulls her even closer. "Come on. I've told you how she is. She doesn't know what happens when she drinks, she makes up the most—"

"You got into the car with her. I watched you leave." He's still smiling, but it doesn't seem real anymore, and I don't know if I'm imagining it, but he looks a little more pale to me now. He loosens his hold on Anna, letting her go again. She sits down at the table, her back to her husband, her daughter moving around on her lap.

Tom puts his hand over his mouth and leans back against the kitchen counter, crossing his arms. "You saw me get into the car with who?"

"With Megan."

"Oh, right!" He starts laughing again, a loud, fake laugh. "Last time we talked about this, you told me you saw me get into the car with Anna. Now it's Megan, is it? Who will it be next week? Princess Diana?"

Anna looks up at me. I can see the doubt, the hope, quickly appear on her face. "You're not sure?" she asks.

Tom kneels down next to her. “Of course she isn’t sure. She’s making this up—she always does. Sweetheart, please. Why don’t you go upstairs for a little while, OK? I’ll talk to Rachel about this. And this time”—he looks at me—“I promise I’ll make sure she won’t bother us again.”

I can see Anna is unsure—the way she’s looking at him, trying to see if he’s telling the truth, his eyes focused on hers. “Anna!” I shout, trying to get her attention back. “You know. You know he’s lying. You know that he was sleeping with her.”

For a moment, no one speaks. Anna looks from Tom to me and back. She starts to say something, but no words come out.

“Anna! What does she mean? There’s . . . there was nothing between me and Megan Hipwell.”

“I found the phone, Tom,” she says quietly, her voice so soft, it’s hard to hear. “So please, don’t. Don’t lie. Just don’t lie to me.”

The child starts to cry and whine. Tom gently takes her from Anna’s arms. He walks to the window, rocking his daughter back and forth, talking to her quietly. I can’t hear what he’s saying. Anna’s head is down, tears falling from her chin onto the kitchen table.

“Where is it?” Tom says, turning to us, his face no longer smiling. “The phone, Anna. Did you give it to her?” He points his head towards me. “Do you have it?”

“I don’t know anything about a phone,” I tell him, wishing that Anna had said something about this before.

Tom ignores me. “Anna? Did you give it to her?”

Anna shakes her head.

"Where is it?"

"I threw it away," she says. "Over the fence. By the track."

"Good girl. Good girl," he says, not really paying attention. He's trying to think, figure out what to do next. He looks at me quickly and then looks away. For a second, he looks like he's lost.

He turns to Anna. "You were always so tired," he says. "You just weren't interested. Everything was about the baby. Right? It was all about you, wasn't it? All about you!" And just like that, he's back to his old self, happy, making faces at his daughter, tickling her, making her smile. "And Megan was so... well, she was available."

"At first, it was at her place," he says. "But she was really worried about Scott finding out. So we started meeting at the Swan. It was... Well, you remember what it was like, right, Anna? At the beginning, when we used to go to that house on Cranham Road. You understand." He looks back at me and winks. "That's where Anna and I used to meet, back in the good old days."

He moves his daughter to his other arm, letting her rest on his shoulder. "You think I'm being mean, but I'm not. I'm telling the truth. That's what you want, right, Anna? You asked me not to lie."

Anna doesn't look up. Her hands are holding the edge of the table tightly, her whole body stiff.

Tom sighs loudly. "It's a relief, to be honest." He's talking to me, looking right at me. "You have no idea how hard it is, dealing with people like you. And, damn, I tried. I tried so hard to help you. To help both of you. You're both... I mean, I loved you both, I really did, but you can both be really weak."

"Damn you, Tom," Anna says, getting up from the table. "Don't put me in the same group as her."

I look at her and see how well they fit together, Anna and Tom. She's a much better match than I am, because this is what bothers her: not that her husband is a liar and a killer, but that he just compared her to me.

Tom goes to her side and says gently, “I’m sorry, honey. I was wrong.” She pushes him away and he looks at me. “I tried my best, you know. I was a good husband to you, Rach. I dealt with a lot—your drinking and your sadness. I dealt with all that for a long time before I gave up.”

“You lied to me,” I say. “You told me everything was my fault. You made me think I was worthless. You watched me suffer, you—”

He shrugs. “Do you know how boring you became, Rachel? How ugly? Too sad to get out of bed, too tired to shower or wash your hair? Seriously. It’s no wonder I got tired of it, right? It’s no wonder I had to find ways to have fun. You can only blame yourself.”

His face changes from anger to worry as he turns to talk to his wife. “Anna, it was different with you, I promise. That thing with Megan, it was just . . . just a bit of fun. That’s all it was supposed to be. I know it wasn't good, but I just needed a break. That’s all. It was never going to last. It was never going to hurt us, our family. You have to understand that.”

“You . . .” Anna is trying to say something, but she can’t speak.

Tom puts his hand on her shoulder and squeezes it. “What, love?”

“You had her watching Evie,” she says angrily. “Were you sleeping with her while she was working here? While she was watching our child?”

He takes his hand away, his face showing he is sorry and ashamed. “That was awful. I thought . . . I thought it would be . . . Honestly, I don’t know what I thought. I wasn’t thinking clearly. It was wrong. It was very wrong of me.” And his face changes again—now he looks innocent, begging her: “I didn’t know then, Anna. You have to believe that I didn’t know what she was like. I didn’t know about the baby she killed. I would never have let her watch Evie if I’d known that. You have to believe me.”

Suddenly, Anna jumps up, pushing her chair back—it falls to the floor, making a loud noise and scaring their daughter. “Give her to me,” Anna says, holding her arms out. Tom moves back a little. “Now, Tom, give her to me. Give her to me. ”

But he doesn’t, he walks away from her, holding the child, whispering to her again, trying to get her back to sleep, and then Anna starts to scream. At first she’s saying give her to me, give her to me , but then it’s just a loud cry of anger and pain. The child is screaming, too. Tom is trying to quiet her, he’s ignoring Anna, so I have to take hold of her. I pull her outside and talk to her, quietly and quickly.

"You need to calm down, Anna. Do you understand? I need you to calm down. I need you to talk to him, to keep him busy for a moment while I call the police. Okay?"

She's shaking her head—she's shaking all over. She grabs my arms, her nails digging into my skin. "How could he do this?"

"Anna! Listen to me. You need to keep him busy for a moment."

Finally, she looks at me, really looks at me, and nods. "Okay."

"Just... I don't know. Get him away from the door, try to keep him busy for a bit."

She goes back inside. I take a deep breath, then turn and walk a few steps away from the sliding door. Not too far, just onto the grass. I turn and look back. They're still in the kitchen. I walk a little farther away. The wind is picking up now; the heat is about to end. Birds are flying low in the sky, and I can smell the rain coming. I love that smell.

I put my hand in my back pocket and take out my phone. My hands are shaking, and I can't unlock the phone the first two times—I get it on the third try. For a moment I think about calling Detective Riley, someone who knows me. I look through my call list but can't find her number, so I give up—I'll just dial 999. I'm on the second nine when I feel his foot kick my lower back and I fall forward onto the grass, the air knocked out of me. The phone flies out of my hand—he has it before I can get on my knees, before I can breathe.

"Now, now, Rach," he says, grabbing my arm and pulling me to my feet easily. "Let's not do anything stupid."

He leads me back into the house, and I let him, because I know I can't fight now, I won't get away from him here. He pushes me through the doorway, sliding the glass door closed behind us and locking it. He throws the key onto the kitchen table. Anna is standing there. She gives me a small smile, and I wonder if she told him that I was about to call the police.

Anna starts making lunch for her daughter and puts the kettle on to make us tea. In this very strange copy of real life, I feel like I could just say goodbye, walk across the room and out into the safety of the street. It's so tempting, I actually take a few steps that way, but Tom blocks me. He puts a hand on my shoulder, then runs his fingers under my throat, putting just a little pressure.

"What am I going to do with you, Rach?"

SATURDAY, JULY 13, 2013

EVENING

I don't see the blood on his hand until we get in the car.

"You cut yourself," I say.

He doesn't answer; his hands are tight on the steering wheel.

"Tom, I needed to talk to you," I say. I'm trying to be nice, trying to act like an adult, but I guess it's too late for that. "I'm sorry for bothering you, but come on! You just ignored me. You—"

"It's OK," he says quietly. "I'm not... I'm angry about something else. It's not you." He turns and tries to smile at me, but can't. "Problems with my ex," he says. "You know how it is."

"What happened to your hand?" I ask him.

"Problems with the ex," he says again, and his voice sounds mean. We drive the rest of the way to Corly Wood without talking.

We drive into the parking lot, all the way to the end. We've been here before. There are usually not many people in the evenings—sometimes a few teenagers with beers, but that's it. Tonight, we're alone.

Tom turns off the car and looks at me. "Okay. What did you want to talk about?" He's still angry, but it's not as bad now. Still, after what just happened, I don't want to be in a small space with an angry man, so I say we should walk. He rolls his eyes and sighs, but he agrees.

It's still warm; there are lots of small flies under the trees, and the sun is shining through the leaves, making the path look strangely like it's underground. Above us, magpies are making loud, angry noises.

We walk a little without talking, me in front, Tom a few steps behind. I'm trying to think of what to say, how to explain things. I don't want to make things worse. I have to remember that I'm trying to do the right thing.

I stop walking and turn to face him—he's standing very close to me.

He puts his hands on my hips. "Here?" he asks. "Is this what you want?" He looks like he doesn't care.

"No," I say, moving away from him. "Not that."

The path goes down a little here. I walk slower, but he walks at the same speed as me.

"What then?"

Deep breath. My throat still hurts. “I’m pregnant.”

There’s no reaction at all—his face shows nothing. I could be telling him that I need to go to the store on the way home, or that I have a dentist appointment.

“Congratulations,” he says after a while.

Another deep breath. “Tom, I’m telling you this because . . . well, because it’s possible that the child could be yours.”

He looks at me for a few moments, then laughs. “Oh? Lucky me. So what—are we going to run away, the three of us? You, me and the baby? Where were we going? Spain?”

“I thought you should know, because—”

“Have an abortion,” he says. “I mean, if it’s your husband’s, do what you want. But if it’s mine, get rid of it. Seriously, let’s not be silly about this. I don’t want another kid.” He touches my face. “And I’m sorry, but I don’t think you’d be a good mother, would you, Megs?”

“You can be as involved as you want—”

“Did you hear what I just said?” he says sharply, turning away from me and walking back to the car. “You’d be a terrible mother, Megan. Just get rid of it.”

I go after him, walking fast at first and then running, and when I get close enough I push him in the back. I’m yelling at him, screaming, trying to scratch his face, and he’s laughing, easily pushing me away. I start saying the worst things I can think of. I insult his manhood, his boring wife, his ugly child.

I don't even know why I'm so angry, because what did I think would happen? Maybe anger, worry, or being upset. But not this. It's not even being turned down, it's being ignored. All he wants is for me to leave—me and my child—so I tell him, I yell at him, "I'm not leaving. I am going to make you pay for this. For the rest of your life, you're going to pay for this."

He's not laughing anymore.

He's walking towards me. He has something in his hand.

I've fallen. I must have slipped. I hit my head on something. I think I'm going to throw up. Everything is red. I can't get up.

One for sorrow, two for joy, three for a girl . . . Three for a girl. I'm stuck on three, I just can't go any further. My head is full of sounds, my mouth is full of blood. Three for a girl. I can hear the magpies—they're laughing, making fun of me, a loud cackling. A warning. Bad warnings. I can see them now, black against the sun. Not the birds, something else. Someone's coming. Someone is talking to me. Now look. Now look what you made me do.

SUNDAY, AUGUST 18, 2013

AFTERNOON

In the living room, we sit in a small triangle: Tom on the sofa, the loving father and good husband, daughter on his lap, wife next to him. And the ex-wife across from them, drinking her tea. Very polite. I’m sitting in the leather chair that we bought from Heal’s right after we got married—it was the first thing we bought as a married couple: soft tan leather, expensive, fancy. I remember how happy I was when it arrived. I remember sitting in it, feeling safe and happy, thinking, This is what marriage is—safe, warm, comfortable.

Tom is looking at me, his forehead wrinkled. He’s trying to figure out what to do, how to make things better. He’s not worried about Anna, I can tell. I’m the problem.

“She was a little like you,” he says suddenly. He leans back on the sofa, moving his daughter to a better position on his lap. “Well, she was and she wasn’t. She had that thing . . . messy, you know. I can’t help liking that.” He smiles at me. “I’m like a hero, me.”

“You’re not a hero,” I say quietly.

“Ah, Rach, don’t be like that. Don’t you remember? You were sad, because your dad died, and just wanting someone to come home to, someone to love you? I gave you all that. I made you feel safe. Then you decided to ruin it all, but you can’t blame me for that.”

“I can blame you for a lot of things, Tom.”

"No, no." He shakes his finger at me. "Let's not change the past. I was good to you. Sometimes... well, sometimes you made me do things. But I was good to you. "I need to change her," she says.

"Not now."

"She's wet, Tom. She needs changing. Don't be mean."

Her face shows no emotion. “I've made a good life for myself, even with you, even with Megan—even with everything.

“It was after I'd seen you that Megan came along. She was heading down towards Blenheim Road. I couldn't let her go to the house. I couldn't let her talk to Anna, could I?
