There's no crying and complaining. She was yelling at me, cursing, saying all sorts of bad things, telling me she'd go straight to Anna, she wasn't going to be ignored, her child wasn't going to be ignored . . . God, she just wouldn't shut up. So . . . I don't know, I just needed her to stop. So I picked up a rock”—he stares down at his right hand, as if he can see it now—“and I just . . .” He closes his eyes and sighs deeply. “It was just one hit, but she was . . .” He puffs out his cheeks, breathes out slowly. “I didn't mean for this. I just wanted her to stop. She was bleeding a lot. She was crying, making a horrible noise. She tried to crawl away from me. There was nothing I could do. I had to finish it.”

The sun is gone, the room is dark. It's quiet, except for the sound of Tom's breathing, rough and shallow. There's no street noise. I can't remember the last time I heard a train.

“I put her in the trunk of the car,” he says. “I drove a bit farther into the woods, off the road. There was no one around. I had to dig . . .” His breathing is even shallower, getting faster. “I had to dig with my bare hands. I was afraid.” He looks up at me, his eyes wide. “Afraid that someone would come. And it was painful, my fingernails ripped in the dirt. It took a long time. I had to stop to call Anna, to tell her I was out looking for you.”

He clears his throat. “The ground was actually quite soft, but I still couldn't dig as deep as I wanted. I was so afraid that someone would come. I thought there would be a chance to go back, later on, when things had calmed down. I thought I would be able to move her, put her somewhere . . . better. But then it started raining and I never got the chance.”

He looks at me with a frown. “I was almost sure the police would think Scott did it. She told me he was very worried about her cheating, that he used to read her emails and check on her. I thought… well, I was planning to put her phone in his house sometime. I don’t know. I thought I might visit him for a beer or something, like a friendly neighbor. I don’t know. I didn’t have a plan. I hadn’t thought it all through. It wasn’t planned. It was just a terrible accident.”

But then his mood changes again. It’s like clouds moving across the sky, now dark, now light. He stands up and walks slowly to the kitchen, where Anna is now sitting at the table, feeding Evie. He kisses her head, then picks his daughter up from the chair.

“Tom…” Anna starts to say.

“It’s OK.” He smiles at his wife. “I just want to hold her. Right, darling?” He goes to the fridge with his daughter and gets a beer. He looks at me. “Do you want one?”

I shake my head.

“No, probably not.”

I barely hear him. I’m thinking about whether I can reach the front door before he can grab me. If it’s not locked, I think I can make it. If he’s locked it, I’m in trouble. I quickly move forward and run. I get to the hallway—my hand is almost on the door handle—when I feel the bottle hit the back of my head. There’s a burst of pain, everything goes white, and I fall to my knees. His fingers grab my hair and pull, dragging me back into the living room, where he lets go. He stands over me, with one foot on each side of my hips. His daughter is still in his arms, but Anna is next to him, pulling at her.

“Give her to me, Tom, please. You’re going to hurt her. Please, give her to me.”

He gives the crying Evie to Anna.

I can hear Tom talking, but it seems like he’s far away, or like I’m hearing him through water. I can hear the words but they don’t seem to be about me, about what’s happening to me. Everything feels distant.

"Go upstairs," he says. "Go into the bedroom and close the door. You can't call anyone, okay? I mean it, Anna. You don't want to call anyone. Not with Evie here. We don't want things to get bad." Anna doesn't look at me. She holds the child tightly, steps over me, and quickly leaves.

Tom bends down, puts his hands in my jeans, grabs them, and pulls me across the floor into the kitchen. I'm kicking my legs, trying to grab something, but I can't. I can't see well—tears are hurting my eyes, and everything is blurry. The pain in my head is terrible as I hit the floor, and I feel like I'm going to throw up. There's a hot, sharp pain as something hits my head. Then nothing.

ANNA

• • •

SUNDAY, AUGUST 18, 2013

EVENING

She's on the floor in the kitchen. She's bleeding, but I don't think it's bad. He hasn't finished it. I'm not sure what he's waiting for. I guess it's not easy for him. He did love her, a long time ago.

I was upstairs, putting Evie to bed, and I was thinking that this is what I wanted, right? Rachel will be gone for good, never to come back. This is what I wished would happen. Well, not exactly this, of course. But I did want her gone. I wished for a life without Rachel, and now I could have one. It would be just the three of us, me and Tom and Evie, like it should be.

For a short time, I let myself enjoy the idea, but then I looked at my sleeping daughter and I knew it was just an idea. I kissed my finger and touched it to her perfect lips and I knew that we would never be safe. I would never be safe, because I know, and he won't be able to trust me. And who's to say another Megan won't come along? Or—even worse—another Anna, another me?

I went back downstairs and he was sitting at the kitchen table, drinking a beer. I couldn't see her at first, but then I saw her feet, and I thought it was over, but he said she was okay.

"Just a little hit," he said. He won't be able to say this was an accident.

So we waited. I got a beer, too, and we drank them together. He told me he was really sorry about Megan, about the affair. He kissed me, he told me he'd make it up to me, that we'd be okay, that everything would be alright.

"We'll leave this place, like you've always wanted. We'll go wherever you want. Anywhere." He asked if I could forgive him, and I said I could, if I had time, and he believed me. I think he believed me.

The storm has begun, just like they said it would. The loud thunder wakes her up. She starts to make noise and move on the floor.

"You should leave," he says to me. "Go back upstairs."

I kiss him and leave, but I don't go back upstairs. Instead, I pick up the phone in the hallway, sit on the bottom step, and listen. I hold the phone, waiting for the right moment.

I can hear him talking to her, quietly, and then I hear her. I think she's crying.

RACHEL

• • •

SUNDAY, AUGUST 18, 2013

EVENING

I hear a hissing sound. There’s a flash of light and I see it’s the rain, coming down hard. It’s dark outside, there’s a storm. Lightning. I don’t remember when it got dark. The pain in my head makes me aware of myself again, my heart jumps. I’m on the floor. In the kitchen. I struggle to lift my head and push myself up onto one elbow. He’s sitting at the kitchen table, looking at the storm, holding a beer bottle.

“What am I going to do, Rach?” he asks when he sees me lift my head. “I’ve been sitting here for . . . almost half an hour now, just asking myself that. What am I supposed to do with you? What choice are you giving me?” He takes a big drink of beer and looks at me carefully. I sit up, my back against the kitchen cabinets. My head spins, my mouth fills with spit. I feel like I’m going to throw up. I bite my lip and dig my nails into my hands. I need to wake up, I can’t be weak. I can’t depend on anyone else. I know that. Anna isn’t going to call the police. She isn’t going to risk her daughter’s safety for me.

“You have to admit it,” Tom is saying. “You caused this yourself. Think about it: if you had just left us alone, you wouldn’t be in this situation. I wouldn’t be in this situation. None of us would. If you hadn’t been there that night, if Anna hadn’t come back here after she saw you at the station, then I probably could have just fixed things with Megan. I wouldn’t have been so . . . angry. I wouldn’t have lost my temper. I wouldn’t have hurt her. None of this would have happened.”

I feel like I’m going to cry, but I hold it back. This is what he does—this is what he always does. He’s good at it, making me feel like everything is my fault, making me feel worthless.

He finishes his beer and rolls the empty bottle across the table. He sadly shakes his head, gets up, comes to me and holds out his hands. “Come on,” he says. “Grab on. Come on, Rach, get up.”

I let him pull me up. My back is to the kitchen counter, he is standing in front of me, close to me, his hips touching mine. He reaches up to my face, wipes the tears off my cheeks with his thumb. “What am I supposed to do with you, Rach? What do you think I should do?”

"You don't have to do anything," I say to him, and I try to smile. "You know that I love you. I still do. You know that I wouldn't tell anyone... I couldn't do that to you."

He smiles—that big, beautiful smile that used to make me feel weak—and I start to cry. I can't believe it, can't believe we've come to this, that the greatest happiness I've ever known—my life with him—was a fake.

He lets me cry for a while, but it must make him bored, because the bright smile disappears and now his lip is twisted into a mean look.

"Come on, Rach, that's enough," he says. "Stop crying." He steps away and grabs some tissues from a box on the kitchen table. "Blow your nose," he says, and I do what he says.

He watches me, his face full of dislike. "That day when we went to the lake," he says. "You thought you had a chance, didn't you?" He starts to laugh. "You did, didn't you? Looking up at me, all innocent and begging... I could have had you, couldn't I? You're so easy." I bite my lip hard. He steps closer to me again. "You're like one of those dogs, the unwanted ones that have been treated badly their whole lives. You can kick them and kick them, but they'll still come back to you, scared and wagging their tails. Begging. Hoping that this time it'll be different, that this time they'll do something right and you'll love them. You're just like that, aren't you, Rach? You're a dog." He puts his hand around my waist and kisses me. I let his tongue into my mouth and press my hips against his. I can feel him getting excited.

I don't know if everything is in the same place it was when I lived here. I don't know if Anna changed the cupboards, put the spaghetti in a different jar, moved the scales from the bottom left to the bottom right. I don't know. I just hope, as I put my hand in the drawer behind me, that she didn't.

"You could be right, you know," I say when the kiss ends. I look up at him. "Maybe if I hadn't come to Blenheim Road that night, Megan would still be alive."

He nods and my right hand closes around something familiar. I smile and lean in to him, closer, closer, putting my left hand around his waist. I whisper in his ear, "But do you really think, since you're the one who broke her skull, that I'm responsible?"

He pulls his head away from me and that's when I jump forward, pushing all my weight against him, making him lose his balance so that he falls back against the kitchen table. I lift my foot and stomp down on his as hard as I can, and as he falls forward in pain, I grab a handful of hair at the back of his head and pull him towards me, while at the same time hitting my knee into his face. I feel a crack as he cries out. I push him to the floor, grab the keys from the kitchen table and am out of the doors before he can get up.

I run for the fence, but I slip in the mud and fall, and he's on top of me before I get there, pulling me backwards, pulling my hair, scratching my face, yelling curses through blood—“You stupid, stupid woman, why can’t you stay away from us? Why can’t you leave me alone?” I get away from him again, but there's nowhere to go. I won't make it back through the house and I won't make it over the fence. I cry out, but no one's going to hear me, not over the rain and the thunder and the sound of the train coming. I run to the end of the garden, down towards the tracks. Dead end. I stand where, a year or more ago, I stood with his child in my arms. I turn, my back to the fence, and watch him walking towards me. He wipes his mouth with his arm, spitting blood to the ground. I can feel the shaking from the tracks in the fence behind me—the train is almost here, its sound like a scream. Tom's lips are moving, he's saying something to me, but I can't hear him. I watch him come, I watch him, and I don't move until he's almost on me, and then I swing. I push the sharp twist of the corkscrew into his neck.

His eyes get big as he falls without a sound. He lifts his hands to his throat, looking at me. He looks like he’s crying. I watch until I can’t anymore, then I turn away from him. As the train passes, I see faces in bright windows, people reading or on their phones, travelers warm and safe on their way home.

TUESDAY, SEPTEMBER 10, 2013

MORNING

You can feel it: it’s like the buzz of lights, the change in the air as the train stops at the red light. I’m not the only one looking now. I guess I never was. I guess everyone looks at the houses they pass, but we all see them differently. We all saw them differently. Now, everyone else is seeing the same thing. Sometimes you can hear people talk about it.

“There, it’s that one. No, no, that one, on the left—there. With the roses by the fence. That’s where it happened.”

The houses are empty, number fifteen and number twenty-three. They don’t look it—the blinds are up and the doors open, but I know it’s because they’re being shown. They’re both for sale now, but it might take a while for someone to buy them. I imagine the real estate agents showing people around, people who are curious to see the place where he fell and his blood was on the ground.

It hurts to think of them walking through the house—my house, where I used to be happy. I try not to think about what happened after. I try not to think about that night. I try, but I can’t.

We sat on the sofa, Anna and I, covered in his blood. The wives, waiting for the ambulance. Anna called them—she called the police, she did everything. She took care of everything. The paramedics came, too late for Tom, and then the police, then the detectives, Gaskill and Riley. They were shocked when they saw us. They asked questions, but I couldn’t understand them. I could barely move or breathe. Anna spoke, calm and sure.

“It was self-defense,” she told them. “I saw it all from the window. He attacked her with the corkscrew. He would have killed her. She had no choice. I tried . . .” It was the only time she hesitated, the only time I saw her cry. “I tried to stop the bleeding, but I couldn’t. I couldn’t.”

One of the police officers got Evie, who had slept through everything, and they took us all to the police station. They put Anna and me in separate rooms and asked more questions that I don’t remember. I had trouble answering, trouble focusing. I had trouble speaking at all. I told them he attacked me, hit me with a bottle. I said that he came at me with the corkscrew. I said that I took it from him and used it to protect myself. They checked me: they looked at the cut on my head, at my hands, at my fingernails.

"There weren't many signs of fighting," Riley said, sounding unsure. They left me there with a police officer in uniform—the one with the pimples on his neck who came to Cathy's apartment in Ashbury a long time ago—standing at the door, not looking at me. Later, Riley came back. "Mrs. Watson says your story is true, Rachel," she said. "You can leave now." She also wouldn't look at me. A police officer in uniform took me to the hospital, where they sewed up the cut on my head.

There's been a lot about Tom in the news. I found out he was never in the army. He tried to join, but they turned him down twice. The story about his father was also a lie—he changed it completely. He took his parents' money and lost it all. They forgave him, but he stopped talking to them when his father wouldn't take out a new loan on their house to give him more money. He lied all the time, about everything. Even when he didn't need to, even when it didn't matter.

I clearly remember Scott talking about Megan, saying, "I don't even know who she was," and I feel the same way. Tom's whole life was built on lies—false stories and half-truths to make him seem better, stronger, and more interesting than he was. And I believed them, I fell for them all. Anna, too. We loved him. I wonder if we would have loved the weaker, flawed, real version of him. I think I would have. I would have forgiven his mistakes and failures. I've made enough of my own.

EVENING

I'm at a hotel in a small town on the Norfolk coast. Tomorrow, I'll go farther north. Maybe to Edinburgh, or even farther. I haven't decided yet. I just want to make sure I get far away. I have some money. My mom was very generous when she found out everything I'd been through, so I don't have to worry. Not for a while.

I rented a car and drove to Holkham this afternoon. There's a church just outside the town where Megan's ashes are buried, next to her daughter Libby's bones. I read about it in the news. There was some argument about the burial because of Megan's supposed part in her child's death. But it was allowed in the end, and it seems right that it was. Whatever she did, she's been punished enough.

It was just starting to rain when I got there, and there was no one around. But I parked the car and walked around the graveyard anyway. I found her grave in the farthest corner, almost hidden under some fir trees. You wouldn't know she was there unless you knew to look. The headstone has her name and the dates of her life—no "loving memory," no "beloved wife," or "daughter," or "mother." Her child's stone just says "Libby." At least now her grave is marked properly; she's not all alone by the train tracks.

The rain started to fall harder, and when I walked back through the graveyard, I saw a man standing in the doorway of the chapel. For a second, I thought he was Scott. My heart jumped, I wiped the rain from my eyes, and looked again and saw that it was a priest. He raised his hand to me as a greeting.

I almost ran back to the car, feeling scared for no reason. I was thinking about how violent my last meeting with Scott was, how he was at the end—wild and crazy, close to being insane. There will be no peace for him now. How can there be? I think about that, and how he used to be—how they used to be, how I thought they were—and I feel a great loss. I feel their loss, too.

I sent an email to Scott, apologizing for all the lies I told him. I wanted to say sorry about Tom, too, because I should have known. If I hadn't been drunk all those years, would I have known? Maybe there will be no peace for me, either.

He didn't answer my message. I didn't think he would.

I drive to the hotel and check in. To avoid thinking about how nice it would be to sit in a comfy chair in their cozy, dimly lit bar with a glass of wine, I go for a walk to the harbor instead.

I can imagine how good I would feel after having a drink. To stop myself from feeling that way, I count the days since my last drink: twenty. Twenty-one, if you include today. Three weeks exactly: the longest I've gone without drinking in years.

It was Cathy, surprisingly, who gave me my last drink. When the police brought me home, looking pale and hurt, and told her what happened, she got a bottle of Jack Daniel's and poured us each a large amount. She cried a lot, saying how sorry she was, as if it was her fault. I drank the whiskey and then threw it up right away. I haven't had a drink since. But I still want one.

When I get to the harbor, I turn left and walk along the edge towards the beach. I could walk all the way back to Holkham if I wanted to. It's almost dark now, and cold by the water, but I keep walking. I want to walk until I'm very tired, so tired that I can't think. Maybe then I will be able to sleep.

The beach is empty, and it's so cold that I have to clench my jaw to stop my teeth from chattering. I walk quickly on the stones, past the beach huts. They look pretty in the daytime, but now they look scary, like hiding places. When the wind blows, they make noise, their wooden boards creaking. Under the sound of the sea, there are quiet sounds of movement: someone or something, getting closer.

I turn back and start to run.

I know there's nothing out here, nothing to be afraid of, but I still feel fear rising from my stomach to my chest and into my throat. I run as fast as I can. I don't stop until I'm back at the harbor, under the bright street lights.

Back in my room, I sit on my bed, holding my hands until they stop shaking. I open the minibar and take out the bottled water and the macadamia nuts. I leave the wine and the small bottles of gin, even though they would help me sleep, even though they would let me relax and forget everything. Even though they would let me forget, for a while, the look on his face when I turned back to watch him die.

The train had passed. I heard a noise behind me and saw Anna coming out of the house. She walked quickly towards us and, when she reached his side, she fell to her knees and put her hands on his throat.

He looked shocked and hurt. I wanted to tell her, It’s no use, you can’t help him now, but then I saw she wasn’t trying to stop the bleeding. She was making it worse. She twisted the corkscrew deeper and deeper into his throat, and she kept talking to him gently. I couldn’t hear what she was saying.

The last time I saw her was at the police station, when we gave our statements. She went to one room and I went to another, but before she left, she touched my arm. “You take care of yourself, Rachel,” she said, and the way she said it felt like a warning. We are connected forever by the stories we told: that I had to stab him in the neck; that Anna tried to save him.

I get in bed and turn off the lights. I won’t be able to sleep, but I have to try. Maybe the nightmares will stop and I’ll stop thinking about it over and over, but right now I know it will be a long night. And I have to wake up early tomorrow to catch the train.
